### 4. Revealing the Right (*Xian Shi*)
- **The Four Vows**: The corrective to the Ten Wrongs (Beings, Afflictions, Dharma-gates, Buddha-way)
- **The Four Kinds of Four Vows**: Vows differ according to the Four Teachings (Tripiṭaka, Shared, Distinct, Perfect)
- **The Point**: Right intention must be structured by the Bodhisattva vow.

### 5. The Six Identities (*Liu Ji*)
- **The Problem**: Arrogance ("I am already a Buddha") and despair ("I can never become a Buddha")
- **The Solution**: Six stages from theoretical to actualized Buddhahood
  - Identity in Principle (*Li Ji*)
  - Identity in Name (*Ming Zi Ji*)
  - Identity in Contemplative Practice (*Guan Xing Ji*)
  - Identity in Resemblance (*Xiang Si Ji*)
  - Identity in Partial Realization (*Fen Zhen Ji*)
  - Ultimate Identity (*Jiu Jing Ji*)
- **The Point**: Both "identical" and "staged." Neither arrogance nor despair.

### 6. The Ten Chapters and Five Abbreviations
- **Ten Chapters**: The complete structure of the *Mohe Zhiguan*
- **Five Abbreviations**: A condensed version for beginners
- **The Point**: Know the map before you travel.

### 7. The Three Kinds of Zhiguan
- **Gradual**: Step-by-step. Like climbing a ladder.
- **Indeterminate**: Flexible. Like a diamond changing colors.
- **Perfectly Sudden**: Immediate. Like the sky—everywhere at once.
- **The Point**: The *Mohe Zhiguan* is the text of the **Perfectly Sudden** path.

---

## The Central Teaching of Fascicle 1

All of the above can be summarized in one principle:

> **"The practitioner is already a Buddha—in principle. The practice is the awakening to what is already the case."**

This is the meaning of **圓頓止觀** (*Yuan Dun Zhi Guan*):
- **圓** (*Yuan*): Complete. Nothing is lacking.
- **頓** (*Dun*): Sudden. No gradual approach required.
- **止** (*Zhi*): Calming. Ceasing the afflictions.
- **觀** (*Guan*): Contemplation. Seeing the truth.

---

## What Comes Next (Fascicles 2-10)

With the foundation established, the *Mohe Zhiguan* proceeds to:

- **Fascicle 2**: Explanation of the Name (*Shi Ming*)
- **Fascicle 3**: Revealing the Essence (*Xian Ti*)
- **Fascicle 4-5**: Embracing the Dharmas / Partial and Complete (*She Fa* / *Pian Yuan*)
- **Fascicle 6**: Skillful Means / Preliminaries (*Fang Bian*)—The 25 Preparatory Conditions
- **Fascicles 7-10**: Correct Contemplation (*Zheng Guan*)—The Ten Objects and Ten Modes

---

## Closing Remark from Miao-lo

> "If one understands this foundation, the rest of the text will unfold naturally. If one does not understand it, all subsequent practice is like building a house on sand."

---

*End of Fascicle 1 Summary*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 2: Explanation of the Name (*Shi Ming*)
## The Four Aspects of "Zhiguan" (止觀)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: What Does the Name Mean?

The second of the Ten Chapters is **釋名** (*Shi Ming* — "Explanation of the Name"). Before any practice can begin, the practitioner must understand what "Zhiguan" means.

Miao-lo explains that Zhiyi analyzes the name through **four aspects** (四門 *si men*):

| # | Chinese | English | Function |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **相待** (*Xiang Dai*) | **Relative** | Defining "Zhi" and "Guan" in relation to each other |
| 2 | **絕待** (*Jue Dai*) | **Absolute** | Transcending the duality of "Zhi" and "Guan" |
| 3 | **次與不次** (*Ci yu Bu Ci*) | **Sequential vs. Non-Sequential** | Whether the two are practiced in order |
| 4 | **通三德** (*Tong San De*) | **Communication with the Three Virtues** | How Zhi and Guan correspond to the Three Virtues |

---

## 1. Relative (*Xiang Dai*)

### Definition

**Zhi** (止 — "calming," "stopping") and **Guan** (觀 — "contemplation," "insight") are defined *in relation to each other*.

-   **Zhi** is defined as "not-Guan."
-   **Guan** is defined as "not-Zhi."

Like "long" and "short," each term gets its meaning by contrast with the other.

### The Three Kinds of Relative Zhi

| # | Name | Chinese | Meaning |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Ceasing** | 止息 (*Zhi Xi*) | Ceasing the afflictions (like "extinguishing a fire") |
| 2 | **Stopping** | 停止 (*Ting Zhi*) | Stopping the mind on an object (like "halting a horse") |
| 3 | **Non-Stop Stopping** | 不止止 (*Bu Zhi Zhi*) | The principle (理 *li*) neither stops nor is stopped |

### The Three Kinds of Relative Guan

| # | Name | Chinese | Meaning |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Penetrating** | 貫穿 (*Guan Chuan*) | Penetrating through the layers of delusion |
| 2 | **Observing** | 觀察 (*Guan Cha*) | Observing the nature of phenomena |
| 3 | **Non-Observe Observing** | 不觀觀 (*Bu Guan Guan*) | The principle neither observes nor is observed |

> Miao-lo: "The first two kinds are 'thought-about' (*ke si* 可思). The third is 'not thought-about' (*bu ke si* 不可思議)."

---

## 2. Absolute (*Jue Dai*)

### Definition

**絕待** (*Jue Dai*) means "cutting off relativity."

In the Relative mode, Zhi and Guan are two different things. In the Absolute mode, they are **not two**.

-   **Zhi** is empty. **Guan** is empty.
-   That which is "stopped" is empty. That which is "observed" is empty.
-   Even the "stopping" and "observing" are empty.

> "When you enter the Absolute, there is no longer any 'Zhi' to contrast with 'Guan,' no 'Guan' to contrast with 'Zhi.' The names disappear into the reality."

### The Relationship Between Relative and Absolute

This is structurally identical to the relationship between **Expedient** and **True** teachings in the *Fahua Xuanyi*:

-   The **Relative** is used to *enter* the teaching.
-   The **Absolute** is the destination.

One moves through the Relative to arrive at the Absolute—but the Absolute was always present.

---

## 3. Sequential vs. Non-Sequential (*Ci yu Bu Ci*)

### Definition

This aspect asks: Do you practice Zhi first and then Guan? Or are they simultaneous?

| Mode | Meaning |
|---|---|
| **Sequential** (*Ci*) | First calm, then insight. First cessation, then observation. (Belongs to the Gradual/Indeterminate teachings.) |
| **Non-Sequential** (*Bu Ci*) | Calming and insight at the same time. (Belongs to the Perfectly Sudden teaching.) |

Miao-lo clarifies:

> "In the Gradual teaching, Zhi and Guan are practiced sequentially. In the Perfect teaching, to 'calm' is already to 'observe.' To 'observe' is already to 'calm.' There is no before or after."

---

## 4. Communication with the Three Virtues (*Tong San De*)

### Definition

The **Three Virtues** (*San De*) are:

1.  **法身** (*Fa Shen*) — Dharma-body (Truth)
2.  **般若** (*Bo Re*) — Prajñā (Wisdom)
3.  **解脫** (*Jie Tuo*) — Liberation (Freedom)

How do Zhi and Guan correspond to these?

| Term | Correspondence |
|---|---|
| **Zhi** (Calming) | Corresponds to **Liberation** (cutting off afflictions) |
| **Guan** (Insight) | Corresponds to **Prajñā** (wisdom) |
| **Both Together** | Corresponds to **Dharma-body** (the non-dual reality) |

But Miao-lo warns: This correspondence is only pedagogically convenient. In the Perfectly Sudden teaching:

> "Zhi is Prajñā. Guan is Liberation. Both are Dharma-body. The Three Virtues are not three. Zhi and Guan are not two."

---

## Summary

The name "Zhiguan" is explained through four angles:

1.  **Relative**: Zhi and Guan are distinguished from each other.
2.  **Absolute**: Zhi and Guan are not two.
3.  **Sequential/Non-Sequential**: Depends on the teaching (Gradual vs. Perfect).
4.  **Three Virtues**: Zhi and Guan communicate with the complete Buddhahood.

This fourfold analysis ensures that the practitioner does not understand "Zhiguan" in a shallow, technique-oriented way. The name itself is already the teaching.

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 1*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 2: Revealing the Essence (*Xian Ti*)
## The Substance of Zhiguan (顯體)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: What Is the "Body" of Zhiguan?

The third of the Ten Chapters is **顯體** (*Xian Ti* — "Revealing the Essence/Body"). After knowing the *name*, the practitioner must know the *substance* (essence, body) that the name refers to.

Miao-lo explains that the Essence is revealed through **four sections** (四科 *si ke*):

| # | Chinese | English | Function |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **教相** (*Jiao Xiang*) | **Teaching Characteristics** | What doctrines describe this essence? |
| 2 | **眼智** (*Yan Zhi*) | **Eye and Wisdom** | What faculties perceive this essence? |
| 3 | **境界** (*Jing Jie*) | **Object/Realm** | What is the object/realm of this essence? |
| 4 | **得失** (*De Shi*) | **Attainment and Loss** | Who gets it and who loses it? |

---

## 1. Teaching Characteristics (*Jiao Xiang*)

### The Problem

There are many kinds of "Zhiguan" taught in Buddhism:

-   **Worldly/Secular** (*Shi Jian*): Calming techniques in non-Buddhist systems.
-   **Tripiṭaka** (*Zang Jiao*): The analytical Zhiguan of the Hīnayāna.
-   **Shared Teaching** (*Tong Jiao*): The "body-contemplating" Zhiguan.
-   **Distinct Teaching** (*Bie Jiao*): The sequential Middle Way Zhiguan.
-   **Perfect Teaching** (*Yuan Jiao*): The non-dual, non-sequential Zhiguan.

### The Resolution

The *Mohe Zhiguan* teaches the **Perfect** (*Yuan*) Zhiguan alone. All other forms are:
1.  Either *included* within the Perfect (by implication).
2.  Or *opened* and revealed as already Perfect (by the principle of *kai quan xian shi* 開權顯實).

> "To practice the name 'Zhiguan' without distinguishing these teachings is like calling every mineral 'gold.' The Perfect Teaching alone reveals the true body."

---

## 2. Eye and Wisdom (*Yan Zhi*)

### The Five Eyes

| Eye | Chinese | What It Sees |
|---|---|---|
| 1. Flesh Eye | 肉眼 | Physical forms (limited) |
| 2. Heavenly Eye | 天眼 | Subtle forms, distant objects |
| 3. Wisdom Eye | 慧眼 | Emptiness (真諦 *Zhen Di*) |
| 4. Dharma Eye | 法眼 | Conventional truth (俗諦 *Su Di*) |
| 5. Buddha Eye | 佛眼 | Middle Way (中諦 *Zhong Di*) |

### The Three Wisdoms

| Wisdom | Chinese | Corresponds To |
|---|---|---|
| 1. All-Knowing Wisdom | 一切智 | Emptiness |
| 2. Path Wisdom | 道種智 | Conventional truth, expedient methods |
| 3. All-Mode Wisdom | 一切種智 | Middle Way, non-dual |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is aimed at the **Buddha Eye** and the **All-Mode Wisdom**. This does not mean the other eyes and wisdoms are abandoned—they are *included* within the Buddha Eye.

> "The Buddha Eye contains all five eyes. The All-Mode Wisdom contains all three wisdoms. To reveal the Perfect Essence is to reveal that which includes all."

---

## 3. Object/Realm (*Jing Jie*)

### The Three Truths as Object

The object of the Perfect Zhiguan is the **Three Truths** (*San Di*):

| Truth | Chinese | Description |
|---|---|---|
| 1. Emptiness | 空諦 | All dharmas are empty of inherent existence. |
| 2. Conventional | 假諦 | All dharmas are provisionally established. |
| 3. Middle | 中諦 | Neither empty nor provisional; both and neither. |

But in the Perfect Teaching, these three are **not three**:

> "Emptiness is not other than Conventional. Conventional is not other than Middle. Middle is not other than Emptiness. The Three Truths are one reality viewed from three angles."

### The Dharma-Nature (*Fa Xing*)

The ultimate "object" of contemplation is **法性** (*Fa Xing* — "Dharma-nature"), also called:
-   **真如** (*Zhen Ru* — "Suchness")
-   **實相** (*Shi Xiang* — "True Characteristic")
-   **法界** (*Fa Jie* — "Dharma-realm")

> "The Dharma-nature is neither internal nor external. It is neither produced nor destroyed. It is neither pure nor impure. It is the essence that Zhiguan reveals."

---

## 4. Attainment and Loss (*De Shi*)

### Who Attains?

-   **Those who attain**: Practitioners who follow the Perfect Teaching, who use the Buddha Eye to contemplate the Three Truths, who understand the non-duality of Zhi and Guan.
-   **Those who lose**: Practitioners who cling to partial teachings, who mistake technique for realization, who remain in the Relative without entering the Absolute.

### The Gradations

| Level | Description |
|---|---|
| **Principle** (*Li*) | Everyone already possesses the essence (理即). |
| **Name** (*Ming Zi*) | Some hear the teaching and recognize the name (名字即). |
| **Contemplation** (*Guan Xing*) | Some practice and gain partial insight (觀行即). |
| **Resemblance** (*Xiang Si*) | Some attain a resemblance of the truth (相似即). |
| **Partial Realization** (*Fen Zhen*) | Some break ignorance and see the essence (分真即). |
| **Ultimate** (*Jiu Jing*) | The Buddha alone has fully realized it (究竟即). |

---

## Summary

The "Essence" chapter reveals what the name "Zhiguan" points to:

1.  **Teaching Characteristics**: The Perfect Teaching is the sole doctrine.
2.  **Eye and Wisdom**: The Buddha Eye and All-Mode Wisdom are the faculties.
3.  **Object/Realm**: The Three Truths (as one) are the object.
4.  **Attainment and Loss**: The Six Identities measure the degree of realization.

Together, these four sections ensure that the practitioner does not mistake a partial or inferior essence for the true body of Zhiguan.

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 2*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 03 -->

# Fascicle 2: Embracing the Dharmas (*She Fa*)
## How Zhiguan Contains All Teachings (攝法)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: What Does Zhiguan "Embrace"?

The fourth of the Ten Chapters is **攝法** (*She Fa* — "Embracing the Dharmas"). After knowing the name and the essence, the practitioner must understand how Zhiguan *relates to* and *contains* all other Buddhist teachings.

Miao-lo explains that Zhiguan "embraces" (*she* 攝) teachings through **six meanings** (六義 *liu yi*):

| # | Chinese | English | What It Embraces |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **攝理** (*She Li*) | Embracing Principle | The ultimate truth (理 *li*) |
| 2 | **攝惑** (*She Huo*) | Embracing Delusion | All afflictions (惑 *huo*) |
| 3 | **攝智** (*She Zhi*) | Embracing Wisdom | All kinds of knowing (智 *zhi*) |
| 4 | **攝行** (*She Xing*) | Embracing Practice | All methods of cultivation (行 *xing*) |
| 5 | **攝位** (*She Wei*) | Embracing Stages | All levels of attainment (位 *wei*) |
| 6 | **攝教** (*She Jiao*) | Embracing Teachings | All doctrinal categories (教 *jiao*) |

---

## 1. Embracing Principle (*She Li*)

### Definition

The "principle" (*li*) is the ultimate reality that all teachings point to. Zhiguan embraces two kinds of principle:

| Type | Chinese | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| **Expedient Principle** | 權理 | The partial truths taught in provisional teachings (Emptiness, Conventional) |
| **True Principle** | 實理 | The complete truth of the Middle Way |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan embraces *both* principles. It does not reject the Expedient in favor of the True. Instead:

> "The Expedient is revealed as already True. The True does not exist apart from the Expedient."

---

## 2. Embracing Delusion (*She Huo*)

### The Three Delusions

| Delusion | Chinese | What It Obscures | Teaching That Addresses It |
|---|---|---|---|
| **View/Thought Delusions** | 見思惑 | True Emptiness | Tripiṭaka, Shared |
| **Dust-and-Sand Delusions** | 塵沙惑 | Conventional Truth | Distinct |
| **Ignorance** | 無明 | Middle Way | Perfect |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan *embraces* all three delusions simultaneously. It does not break them sequentially (first View/Thought, then Dust-and-Sand, then Ignorance). Instead:

> "In one contemplation, all three delusions are seen as empty, conventional, and middle. To 'embrace' means to transform, not to eliminate."

---

## 3. Embracing Wisdom (*She Zhi*)

### The Three Wisdoms (Reprise)

| Wisdom | Chinese | Corresponds To |
|---|---|---|
| **All-Knowing Wisdom** | 一切智 | Emptiness |
| **Path Wisdom** | 道種智 | Conventional methods |
| **All-Mode Wisdom** | 一切種智 | Middle Way |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan *embraces* all three wisdoms because it contemplates all three truths at once:

> "One wisdom is three wisdoms. Three wisdoms are one wisdom. This is the meaning of *embracing*."

---

## 4. Embracing Practice (*She Xing*)

### The Two Kinds of Practice

| Type | Chinese | Description |
|---|---|---|
| **Principal Practice** | 正行 | The main cultivation (i.e., the Ten Modes of Contemplation) |
| **Auxiliary Practice** | 助行 | Supporting practices (precepts, repentance, etc.) |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan embraces both Principal and Auxiliary:

> "Without the Auxiliary, the Principal lacks support. Without the Principal, the Auxiliary lacks direction. Both are embraced in one practice."

---

## 5. Embracing Stages (*She Wei*)

### The Six Identities (Reprise)

| Stage | Chinese | Description |
|---|---|---|
| 1. Principle | 理即 | All beings are Buddha in principle. |
| 2. Name | 名字即 | Hearing the teaching. |
| 3. Contemplation | 觀行即 | Beginning to practice. |
| 4. Resemblance | 相似即 | Approaching realization. |
| 5. Partial Realization | 分真即 | Breaking ignorance. |
| 6. Ultimate | 究竟即 | Full Buddhahood. |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan *embraces* all six stages because:

> "From the first stage to the last, the practitioner is contemplating the same truth. The difference is only in the degree of clarity."

---

## 6. Embracing Teachings (*She Jiao*)

### The Four Teachings

| Teaching | Chinese | Characteristic |
|---|---|---|
| **Tripiṭaka** | 藏教 | Analytical, gradual |
| **Shared** | 通教 | Body-contemplating, shared with Hīnayāna |
| **Distinct** | 別教 | Sequential Middle Way |
| **Perfect** | 圓教 | Non-dual, immediate |

### Application

The Perfect Zhiguan *embraces* all four teachings because:

> "The first three are not rejected. They are *opened* to reveal the Perfect that was always present."

This is the principle of **開權顯實** (*kai quan xian shi* — "opening the expedient to reveal the real").

---

## Summary

The "Embracing the Dharmas" chapter shows that the Perfect Zhiguan is not one method among many. It is the *encompassing* method that:

1.  Contains both Expedient and True Principle.
2.  Transforms all three Delusions.
3.  Includes all three Wisdoms.
4.  Unifies Principal and Auxiliary Practice.
5.  Spans all Six Stages.
6.  Incorporates all Four Teachings.

Nothing is left outside. This is why the *Mohe Zhiguan* is called "Great" (*Mo He* = Mahā).

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 3*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 04 -->

# Fascicle 2: Partial vs. Complete (*Pian Yuan*)
## Distinguishing Inferior from Superior Teachings (偏圓)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: Why Distinguish Partial from Complete?

The fifth of the Ten Chapters is **偏圓** (*Pian Yuan* — "Partial and Complete"). After knowing how Zhiguan embraces all teachings, the practitioner must understand *which* form of Zhiguan is ultimate.

This chapter distinguishes:
-   **偏** (*Pian*) — Partial, one-sided, incomplete.
-   **圓** (*Yuan*) — Complete, round, perfect.

---

## The Five Gates of Distinction

Miao-lo explains that Partial and Complete are distinguished through **five gates** (五門 *wu men*):

| # | Chinese | English | Question |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **大小** (*Da Xiao*) | **Great and Small** | Is the teaching Mahāyāna or Hīnayāna? |
| 2 | **半滿** (*Ban Man*) | **Half and Full** | Is the teaching complete or incomplete? |
| 3 | **漸頓** (*Jian Dun*) | **Gradual and Sudden** | Is the path step-by-step or immediate? |
| 4 | **權實** (*Quan Shi*) | **Expedient and True** | Is the teaching provisional or ultimate? |
| 5 | **偏圓** (*Pian Yuan*) | **Partial and Complete** | Is the principle one-sided or all-encompassing? |

---

## 1. Great and Small (*Da Xiao*)

### Definition

| Category | Characteristic | Teachings |
|---|---|---|
| **Small** (*Xiao*) | Aimed at individual liberation | Tripiṭaka (三藏) |
| **Great** (*Da*) | Aimed at universal liberation | Shared, Distinct, Perfect (通・別・圓) |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is a **Great Vehicle** teaching. It is not a method for personal escape from saṃsāra. It is a method for realizing the truth that all beings will attain.

---

## 2. Half and Full (*Ban Man*)

### Definition

| Category | Characteristic | Teachings |
|---|---|---|
| **Half** (*Ban*) | Only part of the truth is taught | Tripiṭaka, Shared |
| **Full** (*Man*) | The complete truth is taught | Distinct, Perfect |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is a **Full** teaching. It does not withhold anything. Even the Distinct Teaching, though "Full," is only half-revealed because it teaches the Middle Way *sequentially* rather than *immediately*.

> "A half-full cup is not empty. But it is not full either."

---

## 3. Gradual and Sudden (*Jian Dun*)

### Definition

| Category | Characteristic | Teachings |
|---|---|---|
| **Gradual** (*Jian*) | Step-by-step cultivation | Tripiṭaka, Shared, Distinct |
| **Sudden** (*Dun*) | Immediate realization | Perfect |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is a **Sudden** teaching. This does not mean "quick" or "easy." It means:

> "The first moment of practice is not different in *kind* from the final moment of Buddhahood. The difference is only in *degree*."

---

## 4. Expedient and True (*Quan Shi*)

### Definition

| Category | Characteristic | Teachings |
|---|---|---|
| **Expedient** (*Quan*) | Adapted to the needs of the audience | Tripiṭaka, Shared, Distinct |
| **True** (*Shi*) | The ultimate teaching | Perfect |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is the **True** teaching. All other teachings are "expedient" (*quan*) in the sense that they are adapted to lower capacities. But:

> "The Expedient is not rejected. It is *opened* to reveal the True that was always present."

---

## 5. Partial and Complete (*Pian Yuan*)

### Definition

| Category | Characteristic | Teachings |
|---|---|---|
| **Partial** (*Pian*) | One-sided; lacking the full scope | Tripiṭaka, Shared, Distinct |
| **Complete** (*Yuan*) | All-encompassing; nothing excluded | Perfect |

### Application

The *Mohe Zhiguan* is the **Complete** teaching. The term **圓** (*Yuan*) has three meanings:

1.  **Round** (*Yuan*): No corners, no exclusions, nothing left out.
2.  **Perfect** (*Yuan*): Fully realized, no deficiency.
3.  **Sudden** (*Yuan*): Immediate, not gradual.

> "The moon is called *yuan* because it is round. The Complete Teaching is called *yuan* because it lacks nothing."

---

## Summary Table

| Gate | Small/Large | Half/Full | Gradual/Sudden | Expedient/True | Partial/Complete |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Tripiṭaka** | Small | Half | Gradual | Expedient | Partial |
| **Shared** | Great | Half | Gradual | Expedient | Partial |
| **Distinct** | Great | Full | Gradual | Expedient | Partial |
| **Perfect** | Great | Full | Sudden | True | Complete |

---

## The Practical Implication

The practitioner of the *Mohe Zhiguan* must understand:

1.  **Do not mistake the Partial for the Complete.** If you practice only the Tripiṭaka or Shared Teaching, you will attain only partial liberation.
2.  **Do not reject the Partial.** The Partial teachings are *contained* within the Complete. They are opened, not abandoned.
3.  **Aim for the Complete.** The goal is the Perfectly Complete (*Yuan Dun*) Zhiguan.

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 4*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 05 -->

# Fascicle 2: Skillful Means (*Fang Bian*)
## The Twenty-Five Preparatory Conditions (方便 / 二十五方便)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: Why Preparations?

The sixth of the Ten Chapters is **方便** (*Fang Bian* — "Skillful Means" or "Preliminaries"). Before the practitioner can enter the formal contemplation (*Zheng Guan*), certain conditions must be established.

Miao-lo explains that these are the **Twenty-Five Preparatory Conditions** (二十五方便 *Er Shi Wu Fang Bian*), organized into **five groups of five**:

| Group | Chinese | English | Function |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **具五緣** | **Fulfilling Five Conditions** | External supports |
| 2 | **訶五欲** | **Rejecting Five Desires** | Removing gross obstacles |
| 3 | **棄五蓋** | **Abandoning Five Hindrances** | Removing subtle obstacles |
| 4 | **調五事** | **Regulating Five Matters** | Balancing body and mind |
| 5 | **行五法** | **Practicing Five Dharmas** | Cultivating the mind |

---

## Group 1: Fulfilling Five Conditions (*Ju Wu Yuan*)

These are the **external supports** necessary for practice:

| # | Chinese | English | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **持戒清淨** | Pure Precepts | Moral discipline must be intact |
| 2 | **衣食具足** | Sufficient Clothing/Food | Basic needs must be met |
| 3 | **閑居靜處** | Quiet Dwelling | A peaceful place to practice |
| 4 | **息諸緣務** | Cessation of Affairs | Worldly business must be set aside |
| 5 | **近善知識** | Proximity to Good Teachers | Access to qualified guidance |

> "Without these five, the practitioner is like a bird trying to fly without wings."

---

## Group 2: Rejecting Five Desires (*He Wu Yu*)

These are the **gross obstacles** arising from the five sense-objects:

| # | Chinese | English | Object |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **色** | Forms | Visual objects |
| 2 | **聲** | Sounds | Auditory objects |
| 3 | **香** | Smells | Olfactory objects |
| 4 | **味** | Tastes | Gustatory objects |
| 5 | **觸** | Touches | Tactile objects |

### The Practice

"Rejecting" (*he* 訶) does not mean "hating." It means:
1.  **Recognizing** the desire when it arises.
2.  **Understanding** that the object is not inherently pleasurable.
3.  **Releasing** the attachment without suppression.

> "The desires are like salt water. The more you drink, the thirstier you become."

---

## Group 3: Abandoning Five Hindrances (*Qi Wu Gai*)

These are the **subtle obstacles** that obstruct concentration:

| # | Chinese | English | Effect |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **貪欲蓋** | Desire | Attachment to pleasant objects |
| 2 | **瞋恚蓋** | Aversion | Hostility toward unpleasant objects |
| 3 | **睡眠蓋** | Sloth/Torpor | Dullness and drowsiness |
| 4 | **掉悔蓋** | Restlessness/Regret | Agitation and remorse |
| 5 | **疑蓋** | Doubt | Uncertainty about the teaching |

### The Practice

Each hindrance has a specific antidote:

| Hindrance | Antidote |
|---|---|
| Desire | Contemplation of impurity |
| Aversion | Contemplation of loving-kindness |
| Sloth | Contemplation of light |
| Restlessness | Contemplation of tranquility |
| Doubt | Contemplation of dependent origination |

> "The hindrances are like clouds covering the sun. The sun is always there; it is the clouds that must be dispersed."

---

## Group 4: Regulating Five Matters (*Tiao Wu Shi*)

These are the **body-mind adjustments** that balance the practitioner:

| # | Chinese | English | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **調食** | Regulating Food | Neither too much nor too little |
| 2 | **調睡** | Regulating Sleep | Neither excessive nor deficient |
| 3 | **調身** | Regulating Body | Correct posture |
| 4 | **調息** | Regulating Breath | Smooth and even breathing |
| 5 | **調心** | Regulating Mind | Neither tense nor slack |

### The Practice

The metaphor is a stringed instrument:
-   **Too tight** → The string breaks.
-   **Too loose** → No sound.
-   **Just right** → The music plays.

> "The practitioner is like a lute-player. The strings must be tuned before the music can be played."

---

## Group 5: Practicing Five Dharmas (*Xing Wu Fa*)

These are the **mental qualities** that must be cultivated:

| # | Chinese | English | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **欲** | Aspiration | The wish to attain the goal |
| 2 | **精進** | Effort | Continuous diligence |
| 3 | **念** | Mindfulness | Non-forgetfulness |
| 4 | **巧慧** | Skillful Wisdom | Discrimination between wholesome and unwholesome |
| 5 | **一心** | One-pointedness | Unified concentration |

### The Practice

These five are progressive:
1.  **Aspiration** motivates the practitioner to begin.
2.  **Effort** sustains the practice.
3.  **Mindfulness** prevents distraction.
4.  **Skillful Wisdom** guides the choices.
5.  **One-pointedness** is the result.

> "Without aspiration, there is no beginning. Without one-pointedness, there is no completion."

---

## Summary Table

| Group | Focus | 5 Items |
|---|---|---|
| **1. Conditions** | External | Precepts, Clothing/Food, Quiet Place, No Affairs, Good Teachers |
| **2. Desires** | Gross Obstacles | Forms, Sounds, Smells, Tastes, Touches |
| **3. Hindrances** | Subtle Obstacles | Desire, Aversion, Sloth, Restlessness, Doubt |
| **4. Matters** | Balance | Food, Sleep, Body, Breath, Mind |
| **5. Dharmas** | Cultivation | Aspiration, Effort, Mindfulness, Wisdom, One-pointedness |

---

## The Practical Implication

These 25 preparations are not optional. They are the **foundation** upon which the Ten Modes of Contemplation are built.

> "To skip the preparations is to build a house on sand."

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 5*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 SECTION 06 -->

# Fascicle 2: Correct Contemplation (*Zheng Guan*)
## The Ten Objects and Ten Modes of Contemplation (正觀)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: The Heart of the Practice

The seventh of the Ten Chapters is **正觀** (*Zheng Guan* — "Correct Contemplation"). This is the **main practice** of the *Mohe Zhiguan*—the actual method of meditation.

It consists of:
-   **Ten Objects** (十境 *Shi Jing*) — What you observe
-   **Ten Modes** (十乘觀法 *Shi Cheng Guan Fa*) — How you observe

---

## The Ten Objects (*Shi Jing*)

The Ten Objects are the **phenomena that arise** during meditation. The first object is fundamental; the others may or may not arise depending on the practitioner.

| # | Chinese | English | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **陰入界** | Aggregates, Bases, Elements | The five aggregates, twelve bases, eighteen elements |
| 2 | **煩惱** | Afflictions | Desire, aversion, ignorance arising |
| 3 | **病患** | Illness | Physical sickness arising during practice |
| 4 | **業相** | Karmic Impressions | Past karma manifesting |
| 5 | **魔事** | Demonic Disturbances | Māra's interference |
| 6 | **禪定** | Meditative States | Attainment of dhyāna |
| 7 | **諸見** | Wrong Views | Mistaken understandings arising |
| 8 | **慢** | Pride | Arrogance arising |
| 9 | **二乘** | Two Vehicles | Hīnayāna tendencies arising |
| 10 | **菩薩** | Bodhisattva | Bodhisattva experiences arising |

### The Logic

1.  **Object 1 (Aggregates)** is the starting point. Every practitioner begins by observing their own body-mind.
2.  **Objects 2-5** are obstacles that may arise from within or without.
3.  **Objects 6-10** are attainments or developments that the practitioner may mistake for the final goal.

> "Whatever arises, use it. The obstacle becomes the path."

---

## The Ten Modes of Contemplation (*Shi Cheng Guan Fa*)

For **each** of the Ten Objects, the practitioner applies the **Ten Modes**. These are also called the "Ten Dharma-Vehicles" because they carry the practitioner to the goal.

| # | Chinese | English | Function |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | **觀不思議境** | Contemplate the Inconceivable Object | See the Three Truths in the object |
| 2 | **發真正菩提心** | Generate the True Bodhi-Mind | Aspire to complete Buddhahood for all |
| 3 | **善巧安心** | Skillfully Stabilize the Mind | Use appropriate methods to calm |
| 4 | **破法遍** | Universally Break Through Dharmas | Deconstruct all views |
| 5 | **識通塞** | Discern Openness and Obstruction | Know what aids and what hinders |
| 6 | **道品調適** | Regulate the Path-Factors | Adjust the 37 Factors of Awakening |
| 7 | **對治助開** | Apply Antidotes and Supports | Use auxiliary practices when needed |
| 8 | **知次位** | Know the Stages | Understand one's current level |
| 9 | **能安忍** | Develop Patience | Endure without retreating |
| 10 | **離法愛** | Release Attachment to Dharma | Let go of even the attainment |

### The Logic

-   **Modes 1-5** are the core practice. For a sharp-rooted practitioner, these may be sufficient.
-   **Modes 6-7** are remedial. Used when the first five are insufficient.
-   **Modes 8-10** are protective. They prevent falling into errors.

> "Use as many as you need. The goal is realization, not following a formula."

---

## The First Mode: Contemplating the Inconceivable Object

The heart of the practice is **觀不思議境** — contemplating the **Inconceivable Object**.

### What Is the Inconceivable Object?

The object of contemplation is **one moment of mind** (*yi nian xin* 一念心). This single thought-moment contains:

-   **Ten Dharma-Realms** (十法界): Hell, Hungry Ghosts, Animals, Asuras, Humans, Heavenly Beings, Śrāvakas, Pratyekabuddhas, Bodhisattvas, Buddhas.
-   **Each realm contains all ten realms**: 10 × 10 = 100 realms.
-   **Each realm has Ten Suchnesses** (十如是): 100 × 10 = 1,000 suchnesses.
-   **Each suchness is present in Three Domains**: 1,000 × 3 = 3,000 worlds.

This is the famous doctrine of **一念三千** (*yi nian san qian* — "Three Thousand Worlds in One Thought-Moment").

### How to Contemplate

1.  **Observe** the arising of a single thought.
2.  **Recognize** that this thought is not separate from the Three Truths (Empty, Conventional, Middle).
3.  **Realize** that this one thought contains all dharmas, all realms, all times.

> "One thought is the entire Dharma-realm. The entire Dharma-realm is one thought."

---

## Summary

The Correct Contemplation chapter is the **practical core** of the *Mohe Zhiguan*:

| Component | Number | Function |
|---|---|---|
| **Objects** | 10 | What arises during practice |
| **Modes** | 10 | How to work with what arises |
| **Core Doctrine** | 3,000 in One Thought | The inconceivable nature of mind |

This is where the theory becomes practice. Everything prior was preparation.

---

*End of Fascicle 2, Section 6*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 02 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 2: Closing Summary
## The Complete Architecture of the Mohe Zhiguan

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Overview of Fascicle 2

Fascicle 2 of Miao-lo's *Zhiguan Fuxing Zhuan Hongjue* completes the **doctrinal architecture** of the *Mohe Zhiguan*. After the foundational material of Fascicle 1 (lineage, biography, motivation, vows, stages), Fascicle 2 explains the first six of the Ten Chapters.

---

## Summary of Key Sections

### Section 1: Explanation of the Name (*Shi Ming*)
-   **Four Aspects**: Relative, Absolute, Sequential/Non-Sequential, Communication with Three Virtues
-   **Point**: The name "Zhiguan" is not a label—it is already the teaching.

### Section 2: Revealing the Essence (*Xian Ti*)
-   **Four Sections**: Teaching Characteristics, Eye and Wisdom, Object/Realm, Attainment and Loss
-   **Point**: The essence is the Three Truths seen through the Buddha Eye.

### Section 3: Embracing the Dharmas (*She Fa*)
-   **Six Meanings**: Principle, Delusion, Wisdom, Practice, Stages, Teachings
-   **Point**: The Perfect Zhiguan contains all teachings within itself.

### Section 4: Partial vs. Complete (*Pian Yuan*)
-   **Five Gates**: Great/Small, Half/Full, Gradual/Sudden, Expedient/True, Partial/Complete
-   **Point**: The *Mohe Zhiguan* is Great, Full, Sudden, True, and Complete.

### Section 5: Skillful Means (*Fang Bian*)
-   **25 Preparations**: Five groups of five (Conditions, Desires, Hindrances, Matters, Dharmas)
-   **Point**: Preparation is not optional—it is the foundation.

### Section 6: Correct Contemplation (*Zheng Guan*)
-   **Ten Objects**: What arises during meditation
-   **Ten Modes**: How to work with what arises
-   **Core Doctrine**: 3,000 Worlds in One Thought-Moment
-   **Point**: This is the actual practice.

---

## The Structure of the Ten Chapters

| # | Chapter | Chinese | Covered In |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | Grand Outline | 大意 | Fascicle 1 |
| 2 | Explanation of the Name | 釋名 | Fascicle 2, Section 1 |
| 3 | Revealing the Essence | 顯體 | Fascicle 2, Section 2 |
| 4 | Embracing the Dharmas | 攝法 | Fascicle 2, Section 3 |
| 5 | Partial vs. Complete | 偏圓 | Fascicle 2, Section 4 |
| 6 | Skillful Means | 方便 | Fascicle 2, Section 5 |
| 7 | Correct Contemplation | 正觀 | Fascicle 2, Section 6+ |
| 8 | Fruit/Result | 果報 | (Later Fascicles) |
| 9 | Arising of Teaching | 起教 | (Later Fascicles) |
| 10 | Ultimate Return | 旨歸 | (Later Fascicles) |

---

## The Central Teaching of Fascicle 2

All of the above can be summarized in one principle:

> **"One thought-moment of the ordinary mind contains the entire Dharma-realm."**

This is the meaning of **一念三千** (*Yi Nian San Qian* — "Three Thousand Worlds in One Thought"):

-   **一念** (*Yi Nian*): One thought-moment
-   **三千** (*San Qian*): Three thousand worlds (10 realms × 10 mutual containment × 10 suchnesses × 3 domains)

---

## What Comes Next (Fascicles 3-10)

With the doctrinal architecture completed, the *Mohe Zhiguan* proceeds to:

-   **Fascicles 3-5**: Further explication of the Ten Objects
-   **Fascicles 6-7**: Detailed treatment of the Ten Modes
-   **Fascicles 8-10**: Fruit, Teaching, and Return

The Guketsu (*Repair Manual*) provides commentary on all of these, explaining obscure passages, resolving contradictions, and presenting Miao-lo's own insights.

---

## Closing Remark from Miao-lo

> "If one understands the doctrine, the practice follows naturally. If one practices without understanding, one is like a blind man walking at night."

---

*End of Fascicle 2 Summary*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 3: Introduction to the Ten Objects
## Entering the Practice of Correct Contemplation (正觀)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: From Theory to Practice

Fascicle 3 marks the transition from **doctrinal foundation** to **actual practice**. After completing the preparations (25 Conditions), the practitioner now enters **正觀** (*Zheng Guan* — "Correct Contemplation").

The structure of Correct Contemplation consists of:
-   **Ten Objects** (十境 *Shi Jing*) — phenomena that arise during meditation
-   **Ten Modes** (十乘觀法 *Shi Cheng Guan Fa*) — methods applied to each object

Fascicle 3 begins the detailed treatment of the **First Object**: the Aggregates, Bases, and Elements (陰入界).

---

## The Ten Objects Reviewed

| # | Chinese | English | Nature |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | 陰入界 | Aggregates/Bases/Elements | **Universal** — All practitioners start here |
| 2 | 煩惱 | Afflictions | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 3 | 病患 | Illness | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 4 | 業相 | Karmic Impressions | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 5 | 魔事 | Demonic Interference | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 6 | 禪定 | Meditative States | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 7 | 諸見 | Wrong Views | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 8 | 慢 | Pride | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 9 | 二乘 | Two Vehicles | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |
| 10 | 菩薩 | Bodhisattva | **Conditional** — Arises if needed |

### The Logic of the Ten Objects

**Object 1** is the **foundation**. Every practitioner begins by observing their own body-mind (the five aggregates, twelve bases, eighteen elements).

**Objects 2-10** are **developments** that may or may not arise during practice:
-   **Objects 2-5** = Obstacles that hinder practice
-   **Objects 6-10** = Attainments that the practitioner might mistake for the final goal

> "If you master Object 1, you may not need the others. If difficulties arise, the appropriate object will reveal itself."

---

## Why Start with the Aggregates?

The aggregates (五陰 *wu yin* / *pañca-skandha*) are:
1.  **Form** (色 *rūpa*) — the physical body
2.  **Feeling** (受 *vedanā*) — sensations (pleasant, unpleasant, neutral)
3.  **Perception** (想 *saṃjñā*) — recognition and mental labeling
4.  **Formations** (行 *saṃskāra*) — mental impulses and volitions
5.  **Consciousness** (識 *vijñāna*) — the knowing faculty

### Three Reasons

1.  **Accessibility**: The aggregates are always present. Every moment of experience consists of these five.
2.  **Universality**: All beings have aggregates. This is common ground for all practitioners.
3.  **Comprehensiveness**: The aggregates encompass the entire body-mind. To contemplate them is to contemplate the totality of one's existence.

> "The aggregates are the doorway. Once you pass through, the entire Dharma-realm opens."

---

## The Method: Ten Modes Applied to the First Object

For the First Object (Aggregates), the practitioner applies all **Ten Modes** sequentially:

| Mode | Application to Aggregates |
|---|---|
| 1. Contemplate the Inconceivable | See the aggregates as the Three Truths |
| 2. Generate Bodhi-Mind | Vow to liberate all beings trapped in aggregates |
| 3. Stabilize the Mind | Use止觀 to settle on the aggregates |
| 4. Break Through | Deconstruct all views about the aggregates |
| 5. Discern Openness/Obstruction | Know what aids/hinders aggregate contemplation |
| 6. Regulate Path-Factors | Apply the 37 Factors to aggregate contemplation |
| 7. Apply Antidotes | Use auxiliary practices if needed |
| 8. Know the Stages | Understand one's level of realization |
| 9. Develop Patience | Endure difficulties without retreating |
| 10. Release Dharma-Attachment | Let go of clinging to the method itself |

---

## The Core Insight: One Thought Contains All

When contemplating the aggregates, the key realization is **一念三千** (*yi nian san qian* — "Three Thousand Worlds in One Thought-Moment"):

-   In **one moment** of the aggregate consciousness (識陰)...
-   There are **ten dharma-realms** (from hell to Buddha)
-   Each realm contains **all ten** = 100 realms
-   Each has **ten suchnesses** = 1,000
-   Each in **three domains** (aggregates, beings, lands) = **3,000 worlds**

This is not a metaphor. This is the **actual structure of reality** as seen through contemplation.

> "Your ordinary mind right now — this very moment of seeing, hearing, thinking — contains hell, heaven, Buddhahood, and everything in between."

---

## What Comes Next in Fascicle 3

The remainder of Fascicle 3 provides:
1.  **Detailed analysis** of the five aggregates as objects of contemplation
2.  **Application of the Ten Modes** to aggregate contemplation
3.  **Resolution of difficulties** that arise during practice
4.  **Distinctions** between different levels of realization

---

## Summary

-   Fascicle 3 begins the **actual practice** of Correct Contemplation
-   The **First Object** (Aggregates) is the universal starting point
-   The **Ten Modes** are applied systematically to this object
-   The goal is to realize **一念三千** — that one thought contains all worlds

---

*End of Fascicle 3 Introduction*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 3, Section 1: The First Object — Aggregates Overview
## Contemplating the Five Aggregates (陰入界境)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: Why the Aggregates Are the Gateway

The **five aggregates** (五陰 *wu yin*) are the first and foundational object of contemplation in the *Mohe Zhiguan*. They represent the totality of conditioned existence—your entire body-mind complex.

Miao-lo explains that there are three ways to contemplate the aggregates, corresponding to three teachings:

| Teaching | Method | Result |
|---|---|---|
| **Tripiṭaka** (三藏) | Analytical (*xi fa* 析法) | Breaks aggregates into parts |
| **Shared** (通教) | Embodying (*ti fa* 體法) | Sees aggregates as empty |
| **Perfect** (圓教) | Inconceivable (*bu ke si yi* 不可思議) | Realizes aggregates as Three Truths |

The Perfect Teaching method is the focus of this section.

---

## The Five Aggregates Explained

### 1. Form Aggregate (色陰 *Sè Yīn* / *Rūpa-skandha*)

**Definition**: All material phenomena, including the physical body.

**Components**:
-   The **four great elements** (四大): earth, water, fire, wind
-   The **derived matter** (造色): five sense organs, five sense objects, etc.

**Contemplation**: 
> "Form is not other than emptiness. Emptiness is not other than form. Form is precisely emptiness. Emptiness is precisely form."

This is not philosophy. When you contemplate the body, you **directly see** that:
-   It has no permanent self
-   It is empty of inherent existence
-   Yet it provisionally appears
-   This paradox is the Middle Way

---

### 2. Feeling Aggregate (受陰 *Shòu Yīn* / *Vedanā-skandha*)

**Definition**: The sensations that arise when sense organs contact objects.

**Three Types**:
| Type | Chinese | Experience |
|---|---|---|
| Pleasant | 樂受 | Desire arises |
| Unpleasant | 苦受 | Aversion arises |
| Neutral | 捨受 | Delusion arises |

**Contemplation**:
> "Feelings arise from conditions. They have no owner. They are neither self nor other."

When pleasant feeling arises, observe: Where is the pleasure? In the object? In the sense organ? In consciousness? **Nowhere to be found.** Yet it appears. This is the inconceivable nature of feeling.

---

### 3. Perception Aggregate (想陰 *Xiǎng Yīn* / *Saṃjñā-skandha*)

**Definition**: The mental faculty that recognizes and labels objects. "This is a tree." "That is a sound."

**Function**: Creates concepts, memories, associations.

**Contemplation**:
> "All perceptions are like dreams, illusions, echoes, reflections."

The perception aggregate takes the raw data of feeling and constructs a mental representation. But the representation is **not** the thing itself. The word "tree" is not the tree. The concept "pain" is not pain.

When you see this clearly, perception loses its grip.

---

### 4. Formations Aggregate (行陰 *Xíng Yīn* / *Saṃskāra-skandha*)

**Definition**: All mental formations—volitions, intentions, impulses, emotions.

**Scope**: This is the broadest aggregate. It includes:
-   Wholesome mental states (慈悲喜捨)
-   Unwholesome mental states (貪瞋癡慢疑)
-   Neutral mental states (睡眠作意等)

**Contemplation**:
> "Formations are the engine of karma. They propel you into the next moment, the next life."

The formations aggregate is **where action originates**. This is where you have agency. When you see an angry thought arise, that is the formations aggregate. If you **recognize** it as empty, it dissolves. If you **grasp** it, it becomes action.

---

### 5. Consciousness Aggregate (識陰 *Shí Yīn* / *Vijñāna-skandha*)

**Definition**: The awareness that cognizes objects. The "knowing" faculty.

**Six Types**:
| Consciousness | Organ | Object |
|---|---|---|
| Eye-consciousness | Eye | Forms |
| Ear-consciousness | Ear | Sounds |
| Nose-consciousness | Nose | Smells |
| Tongue-consciousness | Tongue | Tastes |
| Body-consciousness | Body | Touches |
| Mind-consciousness | Mind | Mental objects |

**Contemplation**:
> "Consciousness has no color, no shape, no location. Yet it is the basis of all experience."

This is the aggregate that most people mistake for "self." But consciousness is **conditioned**. It arises when sense organ meets object. It ceases when conditions end. No conditions, no consciousness.

---

## The Perfect Teaching: One Thought Contains All

In the Perfect Teaching, the contemplation of the aggregates is not sequential (first form, then feeling, etc.). It is **simultaneous and complete**.

### The Method

1.  **Observe one moment of mind** (*yi nian xin* 一念心).
2.  **Recognize** that this one moment contains all five aggregates.
3.  **See** that this one moment contains the ten dharma-realms (hell → Buddha).
4.  **Realize** that this one moment is:
    -   **Empty** (no inherent existence)
    -   **Conventional** (provisionally appearing)
    -   **Middle** (neither empty nor conventional, yet both)

This is **一念三千** (*yi nian san qian*) — "3,000 Worlds in One Thought-Moment."

---

## Miao-lo's Commentary

Miao-lo emphasizes that the aggregates are not mere objects to be analyzed. They are **the manifestation of Dharma-nature itself**.

> "The aggregates are not apart from Buddha-nature. Buddha-nature is not apart from the aggregates. To contemplate the aggregates with the Perfect view is to contemplate Buddha-nature directly."

This is why the *Mohe Zhiguan* does not say "destroy the aggregates" or "transcend the aggregates." It says: **realize the aggregates as they truly are**.

---

## Summary

| Aggregate | Chinese | Key Point |
|---|---|---|
| **Form** | 色 | Empty yet appearing |
| **Feeling** | 受 | Conditioned, no owner |
| **Perception** | 想 | Mental construct, not reality |
| **Formations** | 行 | Source of karma and volition |
| **Consciousness** | 識 | Knowing faculty, also conditioned |

**Perfect Method**: See all five in one thought-moment as the Three Truths.

---

*End of Fascicle 3, Section 1*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 3, Section 2: The Ten Modes Applied to Aggregates
## Using the Ten Dharma-Vehicles (十乘觀法)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: The Ten Modes as a Complete System

After understanding the aggregates as the object of contemplation (Section 1), the practitioner now applies the **Ten Modes** (十乘觀法 *Shi Cheng Guan Fa*) to this object.

These Ten Modes are called "Dharma-Vehicles" (法乘) because they **carry** the practitioner from delusion to realization. They are not ten separate practices but **one integrated method** with ten aspects.

---

## Mode 1: Contemplate the Inconceivable Object (觀不思議境)

### The Core Practice

This is the **foundation** of all ten modes. If this mode is mastered, the remaining nine may not be necessary.

**Method**: Observe one moment of mind arising in the aggregates.

**What to See**:
1.  This one thought-moment contains the **ten dharma-realms** (hell, hungry ghosts, animals, asuras, humans, gods, śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, bodhisattvas, Buddhas)
2.  Each realm contains all ten realms = **100 realms**
3.  Each realm has **ten suchnesses** (如是相 through 如是本末究竟等) = **1,000**
4.  Each is present in **three domains** (five aggregates, sentient beings, lands) = **3,000 worlds**

### The Three Truths in One Thought

When you observe this one thought-moment:
-   **Empty Truth** (空諦): The thought has no inherent existence, no permanent self
-   **Conventional Truth** (假諦): The thought provisionally appears with all its characteristics
-   **Middle Truth** (中諦): The thought is neither empty nor conventional, yet both simultaneously

> "This is not analysis. This is direct seeing. When you see one thought clearly, you see the entire Dharma-realm."

---

## Mode 2: Generate the True Bodhi-Mind (發真正菩提心)

### The Vow

After seeing that one thought contains all beings in all realms, the practitioner generates the vow:

> "I vow to liberate all beings from suffering. I vow to help all beings realize what I have realized."

### The Four Great Vows Applied

| Vow | Application to Aggregates |
|---|---|
| Beings are numberless; I vow to save them | All beings trapped in the five aggregates |
| Afflictions are inexhaustible; I vow to end them | All afflictions arising from aggregate-clinging |
| Dharma-gates are boundless; I vow to master them | All methods for liberating aggregates |
| Buddhahood is unsurpassable; I vow to attain it | Complete realization of aggregate-nature |

### Why This Mode Is Necessary

Without the Bodhi-Mind, contemplation becomes **self-centered**. The practitioner might realize emptiness but lack compassion. This mode ensures that realization leads to action.

---

## Mode 3: Skillfully Stabilize the Mind (善巧安心止觀)

### The Method

Use **Zhi** (止 — calming) and **Guan** (觀 — contemplation) to settle the mind on the aggregates.

**Three Levels**:

| Level | Zhi (Calming) | Guan (Contemplation) |
|---|---|---|
| **Coarse** | Stop gross distraction | Observe gross afflictions |
| **Subtle** | Stop subtle wandering | Observe subtle movements |
| **Ultimate** | Rest in non-stopping | Observe non-observing |

### The Balance

-   **Too much Zhi** → The mind becomes dull, loses clarity
-   **Too much Guan** → The mind becomes scattered, loses stability
-   **Balanced** → The mind is both clear and stable

> "Like tuning a lute: too tight, the string breaks; too loose, no sound; just right, music plays."

---

## Mode 4: Universally Break Through Dharmas (破法遍)

### The Purpose

Even after contemplating the inconceivable nature of the aggregates, **subtle attachments** may remain. This mode systematically deconstructs all views.

### The Four Levels of Breaking Through

| Level | What Is Broken | Method |
|---|---|---|
| **Views of Self** | "I am the aggregates" | See aggregates as empty |
| **Views of Dharmas** | "Aggregates are real entities" | See dharmas as empty |
| **Views of Emptiness** | "Emptiness is the ultimate" | See emptiness as empty |
| **Views of the Middle** | "The Middle Way is supreme" | See Middle as non-abiding |

### The Complete Breakthrough

When all views are broken, what remains? **Nothing to grasp, nothing to reject.** This is true freedom.

---

## Mode 5: Discern Openness and Obstruction (識通塞)

### The Principle

Not all paths lead forward. Some practices **open** the way; others **obstruct** it.

**Openness** (通): Practices that accord with the Three Truths
**Obstruction** (塞): Practices that cling to one-sided views

### Examples

| Practice | Openness | Obstruction |
|---|---|---|
| Contemplating emptiness | ✓ Opens if non-attached | ✗ Obstructs if nihilistic |
| Contemplating form | ✓ Opens if seeing provisional | ✗ Obstructs if reifying |
| Contemplating Middle | ✓ Opens if non-dual | ✗ Obstructs if conceptual |

### The Skill

The practitioner must **recognize** when a practice has become an obstruction and **shift** to what opens the way.

---

## Mode 6: Regulate the Path-Factors (道品調適)

### The 37 Factors of Awakening

When the first five modes are insufficient, the practitioner applies the **37 Factors**:

| Group | Factors | Application |
|---|---|---|
| Four Foundations of Mindfulness | Body, Feelings, Mind, Dharmas | Observe aggregates in detail |
| Four Right Efforts | Prevent, Abandon, Cultivate, Maintain | Manage wholesome/unwholesome |
| Four Bases of Power | Desire, Effort, Mind, Investigation | Develop concentration |
| Five Faculties | Faith, Effort, Mindfulness, Concentration, Wisdom | Balance the practice |
| Five Powers | (Same as Faculties, but stronger) | Overcome obstacles |
| Seven Factors of Awakening | Mindfulness, Investigation, Effort, Joy, Tranquility, Concentration, Equanimity | Approach realization |
| Eightfold Path | Right View through Right Concentration | Complete the path |

### The Regulation

Each factor must be **adjusted** according to the practitioner's current state. Too much effort? Increase tranquility. Too much concentration? Increase investigation.

---

## Mode 7: Apply Antidotes and Supports (對治助開)

### When to Use

If the first six modes are still insufficient, apply **auxiliary practices**:

| Obstacle | Antidote |
|---|---|
| Desire | Contemplate impurity |
| Aversion | Contemplate loving-kindness |
| Delusion | Contemplate dependent origination |
| Pride | Contemplate the elements (earth, water, fire, wind) |
| Scattered thoughts | Count the breath |

### The Support

These practices **support** the main contemplation. They are not the goal but **skillful means** to remove specific obstacles.

---

## Mode 8: Know the Stages (知次位)

### The Six Identities

The practitioner must understand their current level to avoid **pride** (thinking they've arrived) or **despair** (thinking they'll never arrive).

| Stage | Description | Relation to Aggregates |
|---|---|---|
| **Principle Identity** (理即) | Inherently Buddha | Aggregates are Buddha-nature |
| **Name Identity** (名字即) | Hear the teaching | Understand aggregates conceptually |
| **Contemplation Identity** (觀行即) | Begin practice | Contemplate aggregates directly |
| **Resemblance Identity** (相似即) | Approach realization | Aggregates appear as Three Truths |
| **Partial Realization** (分真即) | Break ignorance | See aggregates as they are |
| **Ultimate Identity** (究竟即) | Complete Buddhahood | Perfect realization |

---

## Mode 9: Develop Patience (能安忍)

### The Necessity

The path is long. Obstacles arise. Without **patience** (忍), the practitioner will retreat.

**Three Types of Patience**:
1.  **Patience with suffering** — Endure physical/mental difficulties
2.  **Patience with dharmas** — Accept the teachings without doubt
3.  **Patience with non-arising** — Realize the unborn nature of phenomena

---

## Mode 10: Release Attachment to Dharma (離法愛)

### The Final Obstacle

Even after realizing the nature of aggregates, a subtle attachment may remain: **attachment to the realization itself**.

**Signs of Dharma-Attachment**:
-   Pride in one's attainment
-   Clinging to meditative states
-   Attachment to emptiness
-   Attachment to the Middle Way

### The Release

> "Let go of even the letting go. Release even the release."

When this final attachment is released, the practitioner enters the **first bhūmi** (初地) or the **first abode** (初住) of the Bodhisattva path.

---

## Summary: The Ten Modes as One Vehicle

| Mode | Function |
|---|---|
| 1. Inconceivable Object | Foundation — see 3,000 in one thought |
| 2. Bodhi-Mind | Motivation — vow to save all beings |
| 3. Stabilize Mind | Method — balance Zhi and Guan |
| 4. Break Through | Deconstruction — eliminate all views |
| 5. Discern Openness/Obstruction | Skill — know what helps/hinders |
| 6. Path-Factors | Regulation — adjust the 37 Factors |
| 7. Antidotes | Support — remove specific obstacles |
| 8. Know Stages | Orientation — understand current level |
| 9. Patience | Endurance — persist through difficulties |
| 10. Release Dharma-Attachment | Liberation — let go of attainment |

**Sharp practitioners** may only need Modes 1-5.
**Average practitioners** will need Modes 6-7.
**All practitioners** must understand Modes 8-10 to avoid errors.

---

*End of Fascicle 3, Section 2*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 SECTION 03 -->

# Fascicle 3, Section 3: Troubleshooting Aggregate Contemplation
## Common Obstacles and Their Remedies (對治法門)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: Why Obstacles Arise

Even with correct understanding and proper application of the Ten Modes, practitioners encounter **obstacles** (障礙 *zhang'ai*). Miao-lo explains that these obstacles are not failures—they are **natural developments** in the practice.

The key is to **recognize** the obstacle and apply the appropriate remedy.

---

## Obstacle 1: Drowsiness and Dullness (昏沈)

### Symptoms
-   Mind becomes heavy and foggy
-   Cannot maintain clear awareness
-   Falls into sleep or stupor during contemplation

### Causes
-   Excessive calming (止) without contemplation (觀)
-   Physical fatigue or improper diet
-   Subtle attachment to peaceful states

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Increase Guan** | Shift emphasis to analytical contemplation |
| **Contemplate light** | Visualize bright light to energize the mind |
| **Adjust posture** | Stand or walk instead of sitting |
| **Regulate sleep** | Ensure adequate but not excessive rest |
| **Contemplate impermanence** | Reflect on the urgency of practice |

> "Dullness is like fog covering the sun. The sun is still there—you need wind to clear the fog."

---

## Obstacle 2: Restlessness and Agitation (掉舉)

### Symptoms
-   Mind cannot settle
-   Thoughts scatter in all directions
-   Physical restlessness, inability to sit still

### Causes
-   Excessive contemplation (觀) without calming (止)
-   Unresolved desires or anxieties
-   Improper breathing or posture

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Increase Zhi** | Emphasize calming practices |
| **Count the breath** | Focus on breath to anchor attention |
| **Contemplate tranquility** | Reflect on peace and stillness |
| **Adjust posture** | Ensure proper alignment and comfort |
| **Simplify practice** | Return to basic breath awareness |

> "Restlessness is like wind disturbing water. Let the wind settle, and the water becomes clear."

---

## Obstacle 3: Attachment to Blissful States (味著禪定)

### Symptoms
-   Experiences pleasant meditative states
-   Desires to remain in these states
-   Loses motivation for deeper practice

### Causes
-   Mistaking meditative bliss for liberation
-   Subtle craving for pleasant experiences
-   Lack of understanding of the goal

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Contemplate impermanence** | See that blissful states are conditioned and temporary |
| **Apply Mode 10** | Release attachment to dharma (離法愛) |
| **Recall Bodhi-Mind** | Remember the vow to liberate all beings |
| **Study the teachings** | Understand the difference between dhyāna and prajñā |

> "Bliss is like honey on the razor's edge. Sweet, but dangerous if you linger."

---

## Obstacle 4: Doubt and Uncertainty (疑)

### Symptoms
-   Questions the validity of the teaching
-   Uncertain about the method
-   Wavers between different approaches

### Causes
-   Insufficient study or understanding
-   Lack of faith in the lineage
-   Intellectual over-analysis

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Study the scriptures** | Deepen understanding through texts |
| **Seek guidance** | Consult qualified teachers |
| **Contemplate dependent origination** | See the logic of causality |
| **Recall past results** | Reflect on benefits already experienced |
| **Develop faith** | Trust the lineage and method |

> "Doubt is like standing at a crossroads. Choose a path and walk it. Standing still gets you nowhere."

---

## Obstacle 5: Pride and Arrogance (慢)

### Symptoms
-   Believes oneself superior to others
-   Dismisses other teachings or practitioners
-   Attachment to one's own attainments

### Causes
-   Partial realization mistaken for complete realization
-   Comparison with others
-   Lack of understanding of the Six Identities

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Study the Six Identities** | Understand the stages clearly |
| **Contemplate the elements** | See the body as earth, water, fire, wind |
| **Recall Bodhi-Mind** | Remember all beings are inherently Buddha |
| **Serve others** | Engage in compassionate action |
| **Seek feedback** | Accept guidance from teachers |

> "Pride is like a mountain blocking the view. Flatten the mountain, and the vista opens."

---

## Obstacle 6: Fear and Anxiety (怖畏)

### Symptoms
-   Fear of losing the self
-   Anxiety about emptiness
-   Panic during deep contemplation

### Causes
-   Misunderstanding emptiness as nihilism
-   Clinging to the sense of self
-   Lack of gradual preparation

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Contemplate the Middle Way** | Understand emptiness does not mean non-existence |
| **Gradual approach** | Don't rush into deep emptiness contemplation |
| **Develop faith** | Trust that Buddha-nature is indestructible |
| **Loving-kindness practice** | Cultivate warmth and safety |
| **Study the teachings** | Clarify misconceptions about emptiness |

> "Fear arises from misunderstanding. Emptiness is not annihilation—it is liberation."

---

## Obstacle 7: Physical Discomfort (身苦)

### Symptoms
-   Pain in the body during meditation
-   Illness arising during practice
-   Physical exhaustion

### Causes
-   Improper posture or breathing
-   Karmic ripening (業相)
-   Imbalance in the elements

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Adjust posture** | Ensure proper alignment |
| **Regulate breath** | Practice smooth, even breathing |
| **Contemplate the body** | See pain as empty and impermanent |
| **Medical treatment** | Address physical health issues |
| **Patience** | Endure with understanding (Mode 9) |

> "Pain is a teacher. It shows where attachment remains."

---

## Obstacle 8: Demonic Interference (魔事)

### Symptoms
-   Disturbing visions or experiences
-   Sudden strong afflictions
-   External disturbances

### Causes
-   Karmic obstacles
-   Māra's interference
-   Imbalance in practice

### Remedies

| Method | Application |
|---|---|
| **Do not engage** | Ignore visions and disturbances |
| **Contemplate emptiness** | See all phenomena as empty |
| **Recite mantras** | Use protective practices |
| **Maintain precepts** | Ensure moral foundation is strong |
| **Seek guidance** | Consult experienced teachers |

> "Demons are like shadows. They have no substance. Shine the light of wisdom, and they disappear."

---

## Summary: The Principle of Remedies

All obstacles share a common structure:
1.  **Recognition** — Identify the obstacle clearly
2.  **Understanding** — Know the cause
3.  **Application** — Apply the appropriate remedy
4.  **Release** — Let go when the obstacle dissolves

| Obstacle | Primary Remedy |
|---|---|
| Dullness | Increase Guan, contemplate light |
| Restlessness | Increase Zhi, count breath |
| Attachment to bliss | Contemplate impermanence |
| Doubt | Study teachings, seek guidance |
| Pride | Study Six Identities, serve others |
| Fear | Contemplate Middle Way |
| Physical pain | Adjust posture, contemplate emptiness |
| Demonic interference | Ignore, contemplate emptiness |

**Miao-lo's Key Point**:
> "Obstacles are not enemies. They are opportunities to deepen practice. Each obstacle, when properly addressed, becomes a step forward."

---

*End of Fascicle 3, Section 3*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 SECTION 04 -->

# Fascicle 3, Section 4: The Remaining Nine Objects Overview
## When Other Objects Arise (餘九境)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: Conditional Objects

After mastering the contemplation of the **First Object** (Aggregates), the practitioner may encounter one or more of the **remaining nine objects**. These are called "conditional" because they arise **only when needed**.

Miao-lo emphasizes: 
> "If the First Object is thoroughly realized, the remaining nine may never arise. But if they do arise, they must be recognized and worked with using the same Ten Modes."

---

## The Nine Conditional Objects

| # | Chinese | English | Nature |
|---|---|---|---|
| 2 | 煩惱境 | Afflictions | Internal obstacles |
| 3 | 病患境 | Illness | Physical obstacles |
| 4 | 業相境 | Karmic Impressions | Past karma manifesting |
| 5 | 魔事境 | Demonic Interference | External obstacles |
| 6 | 禪定境 | Meditative States | Attainments that may become obstacles |
| 7 | 諸見境 | Wrong Views | Intellectual obstacles |
| 8 | 慢境 | Pride | Attitudinal obstacles |
| 9 | 二乘境 | Two Vehicles | Hīnayāna tendencies |
| 10 | 菩薩境 | Bodhisattva | Advanced experiences |

---

## Object 2: Afflictions (煩惱境)

### What It Is
Strong afflictions (greed, anger, delusion) that arise during practice and **cannot be resolved** through aggregate contemplation alone.

### When It Arises
-   Practitioner has deep-rooted afflictions
-   Past karma activates strong emotions
-   Environmental triggers

### How to Work With It

**Apply the Ten Modes to the affliction itself**:
1.  **Contemplate the inconceivable**: See the affliction as empty, conventional, and middle
2.  **Generate Bodhi-Mind**: Vow to help all beings with this affliction
3.  **Stabilize**: Use Zhi-Guan to settle with the affliction as object
4.  **Break through**: Deconstruct views about the affliction
5.  **Discern**: Know what helps/hinders working with this affliction

> "The affliction is not other than Buddha-nature. To see anger clearly is to see the Dharma-realm."

---

## Object 3: Illness (病患境)

### What It Is
Physical sickness that arises during practice and **interferes** with contemplation.

### Types of Illness

| Type | Cause | Treatment |
|---|---|---|
| **Four Element Imbalance** | Earth, water, fire, wind out of balance | Medical treatment + contemplation |
| **Karmic Illness** | Past karma ripening | Contemplation + repentance |
| **Demonic Illness** | Māra's interference | Protective practices + contemplation |
| **Practice-Induced** | Improper method | Correct the method |

### How to Work With It

**Use illness as the object**:
-   Contemplate the pain as empty and impermanent
-   See the body as the aggregates (return to Object 1)
-   Apply patience (Mode 9)
-   Seek medical treatment when appropriate

> "Illness is a teacher. It reveals attachment to the body."

---

## Object 4: Karmic Impressions (業相境)

### What It Is
Past karma manifesting as **visions, memories, or experiences** during meditation.

### Examples
-   Visions of past lives
-   Memories of past actions
-   Karmic debts becoming conscious

### How to Work With It

**Do not engage with the content**:
-   Recognize it as karmic manifestation
-   Contemplate it as empty
-   Do not be disturbed or fascinated
-   Continue with the practice

> "Karmic visions are like dreams. They have no substance. Do not chase them or flee from them."

---

## Object 5: Demonic Interference (魔事境)

### What It Is
Obstacles created by **Māra** (internal or external) to disrupt practice.

### Four Types of Māra

| Type | Chinese | Manifestation |
|---|---|---|
| **Affliction-Māra** | 煩惱魔 | Internal afflictions intensifying |
| **Aggregate-Māra** | 陰魔 | Attachment to body-mind |
| **Death-Māra** | 死魔 | Fear of death or impermanence |
| **Heavenly-Māra** | 天魔 | External interference |

### How to Work With It

**The key is non-engagement**:
-   Do not fear or fight
-   Contemplate all phenomena as empty
-   Maintain precepts and protective practices
-   Seek guidance if needed

> "Māra has no power over one who sees emptiness. Like a shadow, it disappears in light."

---

## Object 6: Meditative States (禪定境)

### What It Is
Attainment of **dhyāna** (禪定) or other meditative absorptions that the practitioner might mistake for the final goal.

### The Four Dhyānas

| Dhyāna | Characteristics |
|---|---|
| **First** | Joy and bliss with thought |
| **Second** | Joy and bliss without thought |
| **Third** | Equanimity and subtle bliss |
| **Fourth** | Pure equanimity |

### The Danger

Attachment to these states leads to:
-   Rebirth in the form realm
-   Stagnation in practice
-   Mistaking dhyāna for prajñā

### How to Work With It

**Apply Mode 10 (Release Dharma-Attachment)**:
-   Recognize dhyāna as conditioned
-   See it as empty
-   Do not cling to the experience
-   Continue toward prajñā

> "Dhyāna is like a rest stop on a journey. Enjoy the rest, but do not make it your home."

---

## Object 7: Wrong Views (諸見境)

### What It Is
Mistaken intellectual understandings that arise during practice.

### Common Wrong Views

| View | Error |
|---|---|
| **Eternalism** | Believing in permanent self |
| **Nihilism** | Believing in annihilation |
| **One-sided emptiness** | Clinging to emptiness, rejecting form |
| **One-sided form** | Clinging to form, rejecting emptiness |

### How to Work With It

**Apply Mode 4 (Break Through)**:
-   Deconstruct all views systematically
-   Study the Middle Way
-   Seek guidance from teachers
-   Contemplate dependent origination

> "Views are like chains. Even golden chains bind."

---

## Object 8: Pride (慢境)

### What It Is
Arrogance arising from **partial attainment**.

### Seven Types of Pride

| Type | Manifestation |
|---|---|
| **Simple pride** | "I am better" |
| **Greater pride** | "I am superior to superiors" |
| **Pride of pride** | "I am the best" |
| **Self-pride** | "I am" |
| **Inferior pride** | "I am only slightly inferior" |
| **False pride** | Pride in non-attainments |
| **Perverse pride** | Pride in wrong things |

### How to Work With It

**Study the Six Identities** (Mode 8):
-   Understand the stages clearly
-   Recognize current level honestly
-   Serve others with humility
-   Contemplate the elements

> "Pride is the last fetter. Even advanced practitioners must watch for it."

---

## Object 9: Two Vehicles (二乘境)

### What It Is
Tendency toward **Hīnayāna** (śrāvaka or pratyekabuddha) practice—seeking personal liberation rather than universal Buddhahood.

### Signs
-   Desire to escape saṃsāra alone
-   Lack of compassion for others
-   Satisfaction with partial realization

### How to Work With It

**Recall Bodhi-Mind** (Mode 2):
-   Remember the Four Great Vows
-   Cultivate compassion
-   Study Mahāyāna teachings
-   Engage in bodhisattva practices

> "The Two Vehicles are not wrong, but they are incomplete. The Perfect Teaching embraces all."

---

## Object 10: Bodhisattva (菩薩境)

### What It Is
Advanced experiences on the **Bodhisattva path**—the ten stages (十地), supernatural powers, etc.

### The Danger

Even at this level:
-   Subtle attachment may remain
-   Pride in attainment
-   Dharma-attachment

### How to Work With It

**Apply all Ten Modes**:
-   Continue to see all phenomena as Three Truths
-   Release attachment even to bodhisattva attainments
-   Proceed toward complete Buddhahood

> "The bodhisattva path has many stages. Do not stop until the ultimate goal."

---

## Summary: The Principle of Working with All Objects

**The method is always the same**:
1.  **Recognize** which object has arisen
2.  **Apply the Ten Modes** to that object
3.  **Do not be disturbed** by the object
4.  **Continue** toward realization

| Object | Primary Approach |
|---|---|
| Afflictions | See as empty, generate compassion |
| Illness | Contemplate pain, seek treatment |
| Karmic Impressions | Do not engage, see as empty |
| Demonic Interference | Do not fear, contemplate emptiness |
| Meditative States | Do not cling, continue to prajñā |
| Wrong Views | Deconstruct, study Middle Way |
| Pride | Study Six Identities, serve others |
| Two Vehicles | Recall Bodhi-Mind, cultivate compassion |
| Bodhisattva | Apply all modes, release attachment |

**Miao-lo's Final Point**:
> "Whatever arises, use it. Every object, when properly contemplated, becomes a vehicle to realization."

---

*End of Fascicle 3, Section 4*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 03 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 3: Closing Summary
## The Practice of Correct Contemplation

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Overview of Fascicle 3

Fascicle 3 transitions from **doctrinal foundation** to **actual practice**. It provides the complete methodology for **Correct Contemplation** (正觀 *Zheng Guan*)—the heart of the *Mohe Zhiguan*.

---

## Summary of Key Sections

### Opening: Introduction to the Ten Objects
-   Explained the structure: Ten Objects + Ten Modes
-   Distinguished universal (Object 1) from conditional (Objects 2-10)
-   **Point**: The aggregates are the gateway to all practice

### Section 1: Aggregates Overview
-   Detailed explanation of the five aggregates (form, feeling, perception, formations, consciousness)
-   Three methods: Analytical, Embodying, Inconceivable
-   **Point**: One thought-moment contains 3,000 worlds

### Section 2: Ten Modes Applied to Aggregates
-   Complete exposition of the Ten Modes of Contemplation
-   Application to aggregate contemplation
-   **Point**: The Ten Modes are one integrated method with ten aspects

### Section 3: Troubleshooting
-   Eight common obstacles and their remedies
-   Practical guidance for difficulties
-   **Point**: Obstacles are opportunities, not failures

### Section 4: The Remaining Nine Objects
-   Overview of conditional objects (Afflictions through Bodhisattva)
-   When they arise and how to work with them
-   **Point**: Whatever arises, use it as the object

---

## The Core Teaching of Fascicle 3

**一念三千** (*Yi Nian San Qian* — "3,000 Worlds in One Thought-Moment")

This is not a metaphor or visualization. It is the **actual structure of reality** as revealed through contemplation:

-   **One thought-moment** contains the **ten dharma-realms**
-   Each realm contains all ten = **100 realms**
-   Each has **ten suchnesses** = **1,000**
-   Each in **three domains** (aggregates, beings, lands) = **3,000 worlds**

---

## The Complete Practice Structure

| Component | Number | Function |
|---|---|---|
| **Objects** | 10 | What arises during practice |
| **Modes** | 10 | How to work with what arises |
| **Obstacles** | 8 (common) | Difficulties and their remedies |
| **Core Realization** | 3,000 in One | The inconceivable nature of mind |

---

## The Relationship Between Objects and Modes

**Object 1 (Aggregates)** is the foundation:
-   All practitioners start here
-   If mastered, may not need the others
-   The Ten Modes are applied systematically

**Objects 2-10** are conditional:
-   Arise only when needed
-   Same Ten Modes applied
-   Each becomes a vehicle to realization

---

## Miao-lo's Key Insights

### On the Aggregates
> "The aggregates are not apart from Buddha-nature. To contemplate the aggregates with the Perfect view is to contemplate Buddha-nature directly."

### On the Ten Modes
> "Sharp practitioners may only need Modes 1-5. Average practitioners will need Modes 6-7. All practitioners must understand Modes 8-10 to avoid errors."

### On Obstacles
> "Obstacles are not enemies. They are opportunities to deepen practice. Each obstacle, when properly addressed, becomes a step forward."

### On the Remaining Objects
> "Whatever arises, use it. Every object, when properly contemplated, becomes a vehicle to realization."

---

## What Comes Next (Fascicles 4-10)

With the complete practice methodology established, the *Mohe Zhiguan* proceeds to:

-   **Fascicles 4-5**: Detailed treatment of specific objects and modes
-   **Fascicles 6-7**: Advanced applications and special cases
-   **Fascicles 8-10**: Fruit, Teaching, and Ultimate Return

The Guketsu continues to provide Miao-lo's commentary, clarifying difficult passages and resolving apparent contradictions.

---

## Practical Summary

**For the practitioner**:

1.  **Begin** with the aggregates (Object 1)
2.  **Apply** the Ten Modes systematically
3.  **Recognize** obstacles when they arise
4.  **Work with** whatever objects appear
5.  **Realize** that one thought contains all worlds

**The goal**: Not to transcend the aggregates, but to **realize their true nature** as the Three Truths—empty, conventional, and middle.

---

*End of Fascicle 3 Summary*


---



<!-- FASCICLE 04 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 4: Introduction to Advanced Practice
## Deepening Correct Contemplation (正觀深入)

**Translated by [Your Name/Persona]**
**Date**: January 23, 2026
**Source**: The "Repair Manual" (T46n1912)

---

## Introduction: From Foundation to Depth

Fascicle 4 continues the exposition of **Correct Contemplation** (正觀 *Zheng Guan*), moving from the foundational treatment in Fascicle 3 to **advanced applications** and **deeper insights**.

While Fascicle 3 established:
-   The First Object (Aggregates) as the universal starting point
-   The Ten Modes as the complete methodology
-   Overview of the remaining nine objects

Fascicle 4 explores:
-   **Detailed treatment** of specific conditional objects
-   **Advanced applications** of the Ten Modes
-   **Subtle distinctions** in practice
-   **Resolution of complex difficulties**

---

## The Structure of Fascicle 4

Miao-lo organizes this fascicle around **three main themes**:

| Theme | Focus | Purpose |
|---|---|---|
| **Deepening Object Contemplation** | Detailed work with Objects 2-6 | Master conditional objects |
| **Refining the Ten Modes** | Subtle applications and adjustments | Perfect the methodology |
| **Resolving Advanced Obstacles** | Complex difficulties and their remedies | Navigate advanced practice |

---

## Why Advanced Practice Is Necessary

After mastering the basics (Fascicle 3), practitioners encounter **three challenges**:

### 1. Conditional Objects Arise
