<!-- FASCICLE 06 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 6: Opening — Breaking Through Thought-Delusion
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 6  
**Translator**: [Your Name]  
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Introduction to Breaking Through Thought-Delusion (破思假)

### The Nature of Thought-Delusion

The text now turns to the second major category of delusion: **thought-delusion** (思惑, *sīhuò*), also called the **three correct poisons** (正三毒, *zhèng sān dú*). This designation requires explanation.

Among the thought-delusions, there are four fundamental afflictions: greed, anger, delusion, and pride. However, pride is subsumed under delusion, leaving three primary categories. These are termed "correct" (正, *zhèng*) for two reasons:

1. They are **not background afflictions** (非背使, *fēi bèi shǐ*) — they arise directly in consciousness
2. They are **not habitual tendencies** (非習氣, *fēi xíqì*) — they are the primary afflictions themselves

### The Structure of Thought-Delusion

**Vertical enumeration** (歷三, *lì sān*): These afflictions pervade the three realms (desire, form, formless).

**Horizontal enumeration** (九地, *jiǔ dì*): Within each realm, they are classified into nine grades or levels of intensity.

The distribution across the three realms:
- **Desire realm**: Four types (greed, anger, delusion, pride) × 3 grades = 12 categories
- **Form realm**: Three types (no anger) × 3 grades = 9 categories  
- **Formless realm**: Three types (no anger) × 3 grades = 9 categories

**Total**: 81 categories of thought-delusion

### Why Nine Grades?

The division into nine grades reflects the progressive nature of wisdom and the gradual elimination of delusion. In reality, the distinctions are infinite — wisdom becomes clearer by degrees, and delusion is exhausted gradually. However, the teaching establishes nine grades as a pedagogical framework.

This ninefold classification serves to:
1. Distinguish levels of practice
2. Determine stages of fruition
3. Provide a map for practitioners

Just as the classification of rebirth in the Pure Land into nine grades is a provisional teaching to accommodate different capacities, so too the nine grades of thought-delusion represent a skillful means rather than an absolute division.

### The Function of Thought-Delusion

**Before elimination**: Each grade of thought-delusion, in its unbroken state, possesses the power to:
- Provide energy for continued existence
- Moisten the seeds of rebirth
- Sustain the cycle of birth and death

**After elimination**: Once a particular fruition is attained and the corresponding delusions are severed, rebirth in that realm ceases.

---

## The Stream-Enterer's Seven Returns

### The Doctrine

The **Stream-Enterer** (初果, *chūguǒ*; Skt. *sotāpanna*) is distinguished from those who have merely broken through view-delusion by the specific limitation: **at most seven returns** (七反, *qī fǎn*).

This means:
- Maximum of seven more rebirths in the desire realm
- Definite liberation within seven lifetimes
- No possibility of an eighth rebirth

Not all Stream-Enterers require the full seven returns. As the *Laṅkāvat*āra Sūtra* states:

> "The lowest grade requires seven returns; the middle grade, three to five; the highest grade attains *parinirvāṇa* in this very life."

### Explanations from the Treatises

**From the *Satyasiddhi Śāstra* (*Chéng Lùn*, 成論)**:

"In seven lifetimes, the wisdom of the uncontaminated matures. This is like:
- Taking medicine for seven days until the illness is cured
- The *kalala* embryo transforming every seven days
- Family relationships extending to seven generations
- The seven-step snake: due to the power of the four elements, it walks seven steps; due to the power of its venom, it cannot take an eighth"

The power of delusion extends to seven returns; the power of the Path prevents an eighth.

**From the *Abhidharma Mahāvibhāṣā Śāstra* (*Pó Shā*, 婆沙)**:

*Question*: Why is it called "seven" and not "fourteen"?

*Answer*: Counting both intermediate existence (*antarābhava*) and actual existence (*bhava*), the number does not exceed seven, hence "seven returns."

Moreover:
- Seven places of birth: human realm plus six desire-realm heavens
- Seven paths cultivated
- Seven afflictions severed

The seven afflictions are:
1. Desire-realm craving (欲愛, *yù ài*)
2. Anger (恚, *huì*)
3. Form-realm craving (有愛, *yǒu ài*)
4. Pride (慢, *màn*)
5. Ignorance (無明, *wúmíng*)
6. Views (見, *jiàn*)
7. Doubt (疑, *yí*)

If we count comprehensively, there should be seven human births, seven heavenly births, and fourteen intermediate existences — totaling twenty-eight births. However, following the primary explanation, we speak of "seven returns."

---

## The Lamp Metaphor: Extinction at Peak Brightness

**As stated in the *Chū Yào Jīng* (出曜經)**:

"Like a lamp that is extinguished precisely when it burns brightest."

This metaphor illustrates that the Stream-Enterer, though still possessing desire and other afflictions, has fundamentally transformed their nature.

### The Transformation of Desire

**Although desire remains**, the Stream-Enterer is protected by the power of the **moral precepts arising from the Path** (道共戒, *dào gòng jiè*):

- Naturally refrains from sexual misconduct
- Avoids improper conduct even with one's own spouse
- Observes proper times and places
- Maintains purity through the power of realization, not mere willpower

This is likened to how insects naturally stay four inches away from a blade — not through conscious effort, but through inherent nature.

### The Distinction from View-Delusion

Having severed view-delusion and attained the wisdom of personal emptiness (*pudgala-śūnyatā*), the Stream-Enterer:

- Still experiences the **three poisons** in isolated, specific instances
- Understands that all dharmas arise from conditions
- Does not grasp at inherent existence
- Maintains correctness regarding the three poisons — hence "correct poisons"

**Though these afflictions can still moisten rebirth**, they no longer create the causes for rebirth in the four evil destinies (hell, hungry ghost, animal, asura realms).

This differs fundamentally from view-delusion, which:
- Involves grasping and attachment
- Follows objects and generates clinging
- Creates causes for rebirth in the four evil destinies
- Operates indiscriminately and pervasively

Therefore, the **correct three poisons** exist in:
- Those with complete bondage (ordinary beings who have not yet attained the Path)
- Noble ones (Stream-Enterers and above)

In noble ones, these afflictions:
- Do not create causes for the four evil destinies
- Arise with discrimination (knowing when to engage and when to refrain)
- Can be deliberately employed or set aside
- Differ entirely from the indiscriminate arising of view-delusion

---

## The Name "Thought-Delusion"

This category is called **thought-delusion** (思惑, *sīhuò*) because:

1. It arises through **repeated contemplation** (思惟, *sīwéi*)
2. It requires **sustained meditation** to eliminate
3. It is severed through the **contemplative path** (修道, *xiūdào*), not the path of seeing

Unlike view-delusion, which can be eliminated suddenly upon seeing the truth, thought-delusion must be worn away gradually through practice.

---

**[End of Opening Section]**

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Key Terms

- **思惑** (*sīhuò*): Thought-delusion; afflictions eliminated through cultivation
- **正三毒** (*zhèng sān dú*): The three correct poisons (greed, anger, delusion)
- **九品** (*jiǔ pǐn*): Nine grades of intensity
- **七反** (*qī fǎn*): Seven returns (maximum rebirths for a Stream-Enterer)
- **道共戒** (*dào gòng jiè*): Moral precepts arising together with realization

### Sources Cited

1. *Laṅkāvat*āra Sūtra* (楞伽經)
2. *Satyasiddhi Śāstra* (成實論)
3. *Abhidharma Mahāvibhāṣā Śāstra* (阿毘達磨大毘婆沙論)
4. *Chū Yào Jīng* (出曜經)

### Cross-References

- See Fascicle 5 for the completion of the Ten Objects
- See Fascicle 3 for the initial treatment of the Aggregates Object
- Compare with the treatment of view-delusion in Fascicle 4


---



<!-- FASCICLE 06 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 6: Section 1 — Contemplating Thought-Delusion
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 6  
**Translator**: [Your Name]  
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Methods of Contemplation: Analytical and Substantial

### The Two Approaches

In contemplating thought-delusion, there are two fundamental methods:

1. **Analytical Contemplation** (析觀, *xī guān*)
2. **Substantial Contemplation** (體觀, *tǐ guān*)

Both methods aim at the same goal — the elimination of thought-delusion and entry into emptiness. However, they differ in approach and are suited to different capacities.

### Why Distinguish These Methods?

The text states: "**Space encompasses both analytical and substantial methods, therefore both must be indicated**" (空兼析體故須標列).

This means:
- The realization of emptiness can be approached through either method
- Both are valid paths within the Hīnayāna tradition
- The distinction helps practitioners choose the appropriate method for their capacity

The same distinction applies to breaking through view-delusion. Now, in the context of the Mahāyāna (*yǎn mén*, 衍門), we revisit this distinction to clarify the proper use of analytical contemplation.

---

## Analytical Contemplation in Context

### The Nature of Analytical Method

The text states: "**In explaining the proper contemplative method, we again clarify the analytical approach**" (正明觀法中初標).

**Key point**: The analytical method, from beginning to end, operates through analysis (*xī*, 析). This distinguishes it from the substantial method.

**Purpose of this discussion**: Not to advocate for the analytical method as the primary approach, but to:
1. Distinguish it clearly from the substantial method
2. Show its limitations compared to the Mahāyāna approach
3. Demonstrate why it is not the main practice (*fēi zhèng yòng yě*, 非正用也)

### Contemplating Desire-Realm Delusions

**Initial framework**: We begin with the delusions of the desire realm, specifically examining one grade of thought-delusion and its three aspects (*sān jiǎ*, 三假):

#### 1. Cause-and-Effect Provisional Designation (因成假)

**External conditions**: The text references the **six objects of desire** (六欲境):

These six objects, as explained in the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* (大論, *Dà Lùn*), Volume 21, represent different aspects of sensory attraction:

1. **Color/Appearance** (色) — attraction to physical beauty
2. **Form/Figure** (形容) — attraction to bodily shape and movement
3. **Demeanor** (威儀) — attraction to graceful conduct
4. **Speech** (言語) — attraction to pleasant voice and words
5. **Smoothness** (細滑) — attraction to tactile sensation
6. **Human Form** (人相) — attraction to the general human appearance

**The contemplative analysis**:

When these six external objects meet the internal faculty of mind (*yì gēn*, 意根), they produce:
- **Cause**: The external object
- **Condition**: The internal faculty
- **Effect**: The arising desire (*suǒ shēng fǎ*, 所生法)

This arising desire is what we call the **desire-thought** (欲想). It is a provisional designation based on causes and conditions.

**The six objects in detail** (from *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*, Vol. 21):

The treatise explains that practitioners may be attached to different aspects:

1. Some are染著 (attached) to **color** — blue, yellow, red, white
2. Some, though not attached to color, are attached to **form** — delicate skin, slender fingers, beautiful eyes, arched eyebrows
3. Some are not attached to form but to **demeanor** — walking, standing, sitting, lying down; bowing, looking, extending and contracting limbs; raising eyebrows, glancing; approaching, touching
4. Some are attached to **speech** — soft, skillful words that accord with one's wishes
5. Some are attached to **smoothness** — soft flesh, warm in winter, cool in summer
6. Some are attached to the general **human form** — simply the fact of being male or female

**Treatment**: The **nine contemplations** (九想) are prescribed to counter these six desires. (Details in the Meditation Manual, *Chán Mén*, 禪門)

**Manifestation of desire**:

When these six objects trigger internal responses, the **desire-mark** (貪相) manifests externally. This is the arising of desire based on the six objects meeting the mind-faculty.

**Comparison with attainment**:

Even a **Stream-Enterer** (初果, *chūguǒ*) still has these desires arise, though they are transformed. How much more so for ordinary practitioners!

Even an **Arhat** (無學, *wúxué*) may have residual desire-habits (*xí*, 習), as exemplified by **Nanda** (難陀), whose desire-habits were so strong they manifested even after attaining Arhatship.

**Scriptural reference**: The *Lotus Sūtra* teaches that one should not even give rise to **desire-thought** (欲想), let alone engage in physical union. The text further distinguishes **desire-marks** (欲相) from desire-thought.

**The six desire-marks** (according to the *Abhidharmakośa*, 俱舍):

Different realm-beings experience desire differently:

1. **Earth-dwelling beings** (地居, *dì jū*) — physical union
2. **Trāyastriṃśa Heaven** (忉利天) — embracing
3. **Yāma Heaven** (夜摩天) — holding hands  
4. **Tuṣita Heaven** (兜率天) — gazing and smiling
5. **Nirmāṇarati Heaven** (化樂天) — just gazing
6. **Paranirmitavaśavartin Heaven** (他化天) — just looking

The teaching is: One should not even give rise to the thought, let alone engage in the looking, smiling, or physical contact.

**Additional context** (*Abhidharma Mahāvibhāṣā Śāstra*, 婆沙):

The four continents also differ in desire:

- **Jambudvīpa** (this continent): Countless acts of desire
- **Pūrvavideha** (Eastern continent): Up to 12 times, down to 10
- **Aparagodānīya** (Western continent): Many as 7-8, few as 4-5  
- **Uttarakuru** (Northern continent): Up to 5, down to 3-4; some practice celibacy

Thus, those with excessive desire are worse than animals. Yet this continent also has those with little desire, even practicing pure conduct (*fàn xíng*, 梵行).

**The range of desire**: From the grossest (more than animals) to the subtlest (celibate practice), desire manifests in countless gradations.

#### Applying Contemplation to Cause-and-Effect

**The method**: "**If one grasps at the six marks and allows the mind to move internally, this is the arising of the provisional**" (若取前六相內動意根即是所生).

When desire arises based on these conditions, one applies contemplation:

**The fourfold analysis**:

1. Does desire arise from the **external object** alone?
2. Does it arise from the **internal faculty** alone?
3. Does it arise from **both together**?
4. Does it arise from **neither** (without cause)?

**Result of analysis**:

Through this fourfold examination, one discovers:
- No inherent **nature of desire** (欲性) can be found
- The **four propositions** (四句) are empty
- This is **emptiness of inherent nature** (性空)
- This is **emptiness of characteristics** (相空)

**For sharp faculties**: After breaking through the four propositions regarding existence, they immediately attain realization. This is the **direct path** (利根二行).

**For dull faculties**: If realization does not occur, they must employ the **sixty-four examinations** (六十四番). This involves examining each of the four propositions through sixteen perspectives, totaling sixty-four analytical approaches.

#### 2. Continuous Provisional Designation (相續假)

**The principle**: The *Vinaya* states, "**One should prevent continuous desire from leading to action**" (制內相續).

This means:
- Preventing the continuity of desire
- Not allowing desire to develop into physical action
- Recognizing that even continuous thought, without action, must be contemplated

**Contemplative approach**: Similar to the cause-and-effect analysis, one examines the continuous arising of desire through the fourfold analysis, discovering its emptiness.

#### 3. Relative Provisional Designation (相待假)

**The principle**: Understanding that all designations are relative and lack inherent reality.

**Example contemplations**:
- Desire is designated in relation to non-desire
- Purity is designated in relation to impurity  
- These are merely conceptual constructs

**Result**: Through examining the relative nature of all designations, one realizes:
- **Emptiness of inherent nature** (性空)
- **Emptiness of characteristics** (相空)

---

## Gradual Elimination Through the Nine Grades

**The process**: 

"**The first grade also has three provisional designations. The remaining grades should be understood by analogy**" (初品下明餘品亦有三假).

Each of the nine grades of desire-realm thought-delusion contains these three provisional designations. By contemplating each grade through these three aspects, one progressively eliminates thought-delusion.

**Extension to other afflictions**:

"**Breaking through greed, the other afflictions follow the same pattern**" (破貪下餘使亦例).

The same contemplative method applies to:
- Anger (瞋)
- Delusion (癡)  
- Pride (慢)

**Completion**: 

"**Through the nine grades, one achieves the breaking through of non-arising**" (九品下結成無生破遍也).

When all nine grades of all afflictions are broken through, one achieves:
- **Non-arising** (無生) — the cessation of rebirth
- **Complete breaking through** (破遍) — thorough elimination

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Key Terms

- **析觀** (*xī guān*): Analytical contemplation; breaking down phenomena into components
- **體觀** (*tǐ guān*): Substantial contemplation; directly perceiving emptiness
- **因成假** (*yīn chéng jiǎ*): Cause-and-effect provisional designation
- **相續假** (*xiāng xù jiǎ*): Continuous provisional designation
- **相待假** (*xiāng dài jiǎ*): Relative provisional designation
- **六欲境** (*liù yù jìng*): Six objects of desire
- **九品** (*jiǔ pǐn*): Nine grades of intensity

### Sources Cited

1. *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* (大智度論), Vol. 21
2. *Abhidharmakośa* (俱舍論)
3. *Abhidharma Mahāvibhāṣā Śāstra* (阿毘達磨大毘婆沙論)
4. *Lotus Sūtra* (法華經)
5. *Vinaya* (律藏)

### Cross-References

- See Fascicle 3, Section 2 for the initial treatment of the three provisional designations
- See Fascicle 4 for the detailed treatment of the afflictions object
- Compare with the sixty-four examinations in the breaking of view-delusion


---



<!-- FASCICLE 06 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 6: Section 2 — The Four Gates
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 6  
**Translator**: [Your Name]  
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## The Four Gates: Methods of Entry

### Introduction to the Four Gates

Having established the methods of contemplation (analytical and substantial), the text now addresses the **four gates** (四門, *sì mén*) — four distinct approaches to entering the truth.

**The fundamental question**: Why must we distinguish these four gates when examining the breaking through of delusions?

**The answer**: "**To penetrate the principle, therefore the sixteen gates all break through view and thought**" (為通理故故十六門並破見思).

The principle (*lǐ*, 理) is singular, but the gates of entry are multiple. The **sixteen gates** refer to:
- Four gates for each of the four teachings (Tripiṭaka, Shared, Distinct, Perfect)
- 4 × 4 = 16 total approaches

All sixteen gates serve the same ultimate purpose: breaking through view-delusion and thought-delusion to realize emptiness.

---

## The Four Gates of the Tripiṭaka Teaching

### 1. The Gate of Existence (有門)

**Doctrinal foundation**: This gate is based on the *Abhidharma* teachings that establish the existence of dharmas.

**Initial contemplation**: 

The text cites the *Abhidharma* treatises: "**Contemplate that all conditioned dharmas arise from causes and conditions**" (觀一切有為法從因緣生).

This means:
- All phenomena arise through causation
- Nothing exists independently
- Everything is interdependent

**Application to breaking through**:

"**Contemplating in this way breaks through delusions**" (如此下用觀破惑).

By understanding that all dharmas arise from causes and conditions, one realizes:
- No permanent self
- No inherent existence
- All is empty of self-nature

**Historical example**:

"**The five bhikṣus at Deer Park**" (鹿苑下明得益之人).

The Buddha's first five disciples — Ājñāta-Kauṇḍinya (阿若憍陳如), Aśvajit (頞鞞), Bhadrika (跋提), Mahānāman (十力迦葉), and Daśabala-Kāśyapa (拘利太子) — all attained realization through this gate.

**The encounter with Śāriputra**:

When Aśvajit (頞鞞) met Śāriputra, he spoke the famous verse:

> "All dharmas arise from conditions,  
> The Tathāgata has explained their causes,  
> And also their cessation —  
> This is what the Great Śramaṇa teaches."

This verse contains three of the Four Noble Truths:
- "Arise from conditions" = **Suffering** (苦諦)
- "Explained their causes" = **Origin** (集諦)  
- "Their cessation" = **Cessation** (滅諦)

Upon hearing this, Śāriputra attained the **fruit of Stream-Entry**.

**The twelve hundred disciples**:

The text mentions "**one thousand two hundred**" (千二百人) — this refers to:
- The three Kāśyapa brothers and their 1,000 disciples
- Śāriputra and Maudgalyāyana with their 200 disciples

(Note: The text is missing fifty to make the traditional 1,250, but this is a common textual variation.)

**The necessity of skillful means**:

The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* warns: "**The great physician must employ skillful means**" (大論下明用門者善須方便).

Even though this gate leads to realization, one must not become attached to it. The *Prajñāpāramitā* teaches non-attachment (*wú zhuó*, 無著) as its essence.

**If one uses existence, one falls into the trap of existence**.

The same applies to all four gates — each can become an obstacle if grasped.

**Evidence from the *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra***:

The text cites examples of people in the Buddha's time who attained realization through the gate of existence, demonstrating its efficacy when properly applied.

### 2. The Gate of Emptiness (空門)

**Doctrinal foundation**:

"**Therefore it is said: 'Is it old age and death? Or who experiences old age and death?'**" (故彼下證門體).

This refers to the contemplation of the **twelve links of dependent origination** (十二因緣) from the perspective of emptiness.

**The two emptinesses**:

The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* explains:

1. **Emptiness of persons** (生空, *shēng kōng*): 
   - "Who experiences old age and death?" — There is no person
   - This refutes the notion of a permanent self

2. **Emptiness of dharmas** (法空, *fǎ kōng*):
   - "Is it old age and death?" — There is no inherent old age and death
   - This refutes the inherent existence of phenomena

**The Great Emptiness Sūtra**:

The Buddha taught this in the village of Gavāṃpati (調牛聚落), calling it the "**Great Emptiness Sūtra**" (大空經) because it contains both emptinesses.

**Clarification on "dharma-emptiness"**:

Some claim that Hīnayāna only teaches person-emptiness, not dharma-emptiness. However, this is a misunderstanding.

**The text clarifies**:

In the Hīnayāna context:
- **Person-emptiness** = emptiness of self and possessions
- **Dharma-emptiness** = emptiness of the five aggregates as inherently existing entities

In the Mahāyāna context:
- **Person-emptiness** = same as above
- **Dharma-emptiness** = emptiness of all phenomena, including nirvāṇa and Buddha-dharmas

**Therefore**: When scriptures say that śrāvakas only attain person-emptiness, this is a **comparative statement** (與奪之言) — giving to one side while taking from another. It does not mean they have no realization of dharma-emptiness at all.

**Subhūti's specialization**:

"**Subhūti's wisdom of emptiness was particularly clear**" (須菩提空智偏明等者).

Though Subhūti was an Arhat like others, his particular strength was in the contemplation of emptiness.

**The stone chamber contemplation**:

When the Buddha descended from Trāyastriṃśa Heaven, all disciples rushed to see him. Subhūti remained in his stone chamber, thinking:

> "Should I go to see the Buddha, or should I not? The Buddha constantly teaches that if one observes the Dharma-body through wisdom, this is the supreme way of seeing the Buddha."

He then contemplated: "This great assembly, though magnificent, is impermanent. All conditioned things return to extinction."

Through this contemplation of impermanence as a gateway, he realized that **all dharmas are empty and without reality**.

At that moment, he attained realization.

Meanwhile, the bhikṣuṇī Utpalavarṇā (蓮華色比丘尼) transformed herself into a wheel-turning king to be first to see the Buddha. But the Buddha said:

> "You were not first to see me. Subhūti was first to see me. Why? Because Subhūti contemplated the emptiness of all dharmas and saw the Dharma-body. This is true offering — the supreme offering. It is not offering to the physical body that constitutes true offering."

**Empowerment in the *Prajñāpāramitā***:

"**The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Sūtra* records his empowerment**" (大品被加等者).

The Buddha empowered Subhūti to teach the *Prajñāpāramitā* because:
- Subhūti's realization of emptiness accorded with the *Prajñāpāramitā*
- The Buddha also empowered Śāriputra for his wisdom

The text states: "**Desiring to use the great emptiness to parallel the small emptiness**" (故云欲以大空並小空等).

This means the *Prajñāpāramitā* extensively teaches emptiness, so the Buddha empowered these two disciples — one for emptiness, one for wisdom.

**The necessity of skillful means**:

As with the gate of existence, the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* warns that one must employ skillful means when using this gate, lest attachment to emptiness become an obstacle.

### 3. The Gate of Both Existence and Emptiness (亦有亦空門)

**The third gate** combines the insights of the first two gates, recognizing both the provisional existence of phenomena and their ultimate emptiness.

**Textual note**: The text briefly mentions this gate with the notation "**斥非中盪**" — indicating that this gate sweeps away the extremes of the previous two.

**The principle**: 
- Phenomena provisionally exist (有)
- Yet they are ultimately empty (空)
- Both perspectives are necessary

### 4. The Gate of Neither Existence nor Emptiness (非有非空門)

**The fourth gate** transcends both existence and emptiness, entering the realm of the inexpressible.

**Historical example**:

"**Chāṇakya**" (車匿) — one of the Buddha's attendants.

Before his *parinirvāṇa*, the Buddha instructed Ānanda on four matters, including how to deal with the ill-tempered Chāṇakya:

> "Treat him according to the Brahma-method. If his mind becomes gentle and compliant, teach him the *Nādakātyāyana Sūtra*."

**The teaching**: "**Separate from existence and non-existence, one can attain the Way**" (離有離無乃可得道).

This sūtra teaches:
- Not grasping at existence
- Not grasping at non-existence  
- Transcending both extremes

**Contemplation of the境**:

When contemplating phenomena (*jìng*, 境):
- "**Provisional non-existence is the same as before**" (假無同前)
- One recognizes that both existence and non-existence are provisional designations
- Both are ultimately empty

**The observation method**:

"**Making this contemplation**" (作此觀者) — one applies the fourfold analysis to this gate as well, discovering that neither existence nor non-existence can be found.

**Result**:

"**Contemplating in this way**" (如此下破惑結成) — through this contemplation, one breaks through delusions and achieves realization.

---

## The Unity of the Four Gates

**The principle**: "**The four gates, though different, all lead to the same principle**" (四門下明門異理同).

**Explanation**:

Though there are four distinct approaches:
- They all aim at the same truth
- They all break through the same delusions
- They all lead to the same realization

**Metaphor**: Like four doors to a single room — the doors are different, but the room is one.

**Scriptural evidence**: The text provides法譬證 (dharma, metaphor, and scriptural proof) to establish this point.

**Resolving disputes**:

"**Relying on principle to resolve disputes**" (依理通諍中) — when different schools argue about which gate is superior, one must return to the principle: all gates lead to the same truth.

**Example of Bhadanta Dharmatrāta**:

Bhadanta Dharmatrāta (跋摩, *Bá Mó*) — a master who came to China during the Liu Song dynasty and resided at Jetavana Monastery.

Before his death, he left a letter describing his realization, stating he had attained the second fruit. He wrote forty-six verses, including:

> "That Abhidharma speaks of the five-cause dharma,  
> The true meaning cultivates wisdom,  
> The name-giver cannot see it...  
> Various treatises have different approaches,  
> But the principle of cultivation is not two."

**The point**: Though Bhadanta Dharmatrāta followed the *Abhidharma* (which emphasizes the gate of existence), and others followed the *Satyasiddhi Śāstra* (which emphasizes emptiness), both attained realization.

**The error**: Some criticized those who followed different gates, saying they could not attain the Way. This is mistaken.

**The truth**: "**True principle is silent and empty**" (真諦寂寥等非偈文也) — the ultimate truth transcends all conceptual frameworks. Different gates are merely skillful means.

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Key Terms

- **四門** (*sì mén*): Four gates — four methods of entry
- **有門** (*yǒu mén*): Gate of existence
- **空門** (*kōng mén*): Gate of emptiness  
- **亦有亦空門** (*yì yǒu yì kōng mén*): Gate of both existence and emptiness
- **非有非空門** (*fēi yǒu fēi kōng mén*): Gate of neither existence nor emptiness
- **十六門** (*shí liù mén*): Sixteen gates (four gates × four teachings)

### Sources Cited

1. *Abhidharma* treatises (阿毘曇)
2. *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* (大智度論)
3. *Mahāsaṃnipāta Sūtra* (大集經)
4. *Nādakātyāyana Sūtra* (那陀迦旃延經)

### Historical Figures

- The five bhikṣus at Deer Park
- Śāriputra and Maudgalyāyana
- Subhūti
- Bhadanta Dharmatrāta (跋摩)
- Chāṇakya (車匿)

### Cross-References

- See Fascicle 3 for the initial treatment of the four gates in breaking view-delusion
- Compare with the Perfect Teaching's four gates in later fascicles


---



<!-- FASCICLE 06 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 6: Closing Summary
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 6  
**Translator**: [Your Name]  
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Summary of Fascicle 6

### Core Teachings

This fascicle has presented the essential methods for **breaking through thought-delusion** (破思假, *pò sī jiǎ*), the second major category of afflictions that must be eliminated on the path to liberation.

### Key Doctrinal Points

**1. The Nature of Thought-Delusion**

Unlike view-delusion, which can be eliminated suddenly through insight, thought-delusion must be worn away gradually through sustained practice. These are the **three correct poisons** (正三毒):
- Greed (貪)
- Anger (瞋)
- Delusion (癡)

They are termed "correct" because, though still afflictions, they no longer create causes for rebirth in the four evil destinies.

**2. The Structure: Nine Grades Across Three Realms**

The systematic classification into **81 categories** provides a map for practitioners:
- Desire realm: 4 afflictions × 9 grades = 36 categories
- Form realm: 3 afflictions × 9 grades = 27 categories
- Formless realm: 3 afflictions × 9 grades = 18 categories

**3. The Stream-Enterer's Path**

The doctrine of **seven returns** establishes:
- Maximum of seven more rebirths
- Progressive elimination of thought-delusion
- Definite liberation within seven lifetimes

**4. Two Methods of Contemplation**

**Analytical Contemplation** (析觀):
- Breaking down phenomena into components
- Examining causes and conditions
- Suited to those of duller faculties
- Requires sustained, methodical practice

**Substantial Contemplation** (體觀):
- Direct perception of emptiness
- Immediate realization of non-inherent existence
- Suited to those of sharper faculties
- Can achieve realization more swiftly

**5. The Three Provisional Designations**

Applied to each grade of affliction:
- **Cause-and-Effect** (因成假): Understanding how afflictions arise from conditions
- **Continuous** (相續假): Recognizing the stream-like nature of mental states
- **Relative** (相待假): Seeing how all designations are interdependent

**6. The Four Gates**

Four distinct approaches to entering the truth:

**Gate of Existence** (有門):
- Contemplating how all dharmas arise from causes and conditions
- Realizing non-self through understanding interdependence
- Exemplified by the Buddha's first five disciples

**Gate of Emptiness** (空門):
- Contemplating the absence of inherent existence
- Realizing both person-emptiness and dharma-emptiness
- Exemplified by Subhūti's realization

**Gate of Both** (亦有亦空門):
- Recognizing provisional existence and ultimate emptiness
- Avoiding the extremes of reification and nihilism
- Integrating both perspectives

**Gate of Neither** (非有非空門):
- Transcending both existence and non-existence
- Entering the inexpressible truth
- Beyond all conceptual frameworks

### Methodological Principles

**1. The Unity of Diverse Methods**

Though the gates differ, they all lead to the same realization. The principle is one; the approaches are many. This prevents sectarian attachment while honoring diverse capacities.

**2. The Necessity of Skillful Means**

Each method, while effective, can become an obstacle if grasped. The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* repeatedly warns against attachment to any particular approach.

**3. Progressive Elimination**

The nine grades represent a gradual process:
- Each grade contains the three provisional designations
- Each must be contemplated and broken through
- The process extends to all afflictions
- Complete elimination results in non-arising (無生)

### Integration with Previous Fascicles

**Building on Fascicle 5**:
- Completed the treatment of the Ten Objects
- Established the foundation for breaking through delusions
- Prepared practitioners for the systematic elimination of afflictions

**Preparing for Fascicle 7**:
- Further refinement of contemplative methods
- Deeper exploration of the Middle Way
- Advanced applications of the principles established here

### Practical Implications

**For Practitioners**:

1. **Recognize the nature of remaining afflictions**: Though view-delusion is broken, thought-delusion persists and must be addressed systematically.

2. **Choose an appropriate method**: Whether analytical or substantial, the method should accord with one's capacity and temperament.

3. **Apply the three provisional designations**: Each affliction must be examined through cause-and-effect, continuity, and relativity.

4. **Work through the grades progressively**: From upper-upper to lower-lower, each grade must be contemplated and eliminated.

5. **Avoid attachment to method**: The gate is a means, not an end. Do not grasp at the approach itself.

### The Path Forward

Having established these methods for breaking through thought-delusion, the practitioner is equipped to:
- Systematically eliminate the 81 categories of affliction
- Progress through the stages of realization
- Ultimately achieve liberation from cyclic existence

The work is gradual, requiring sustained effort over potentially multiple lifetimes. Yet the path is clear, the methods are proven, and the goal is certain.

---

## Concluding Reflection

Miao-lo's commentary illuminates the practical application of Zhiyi's teachings. By distinguishing methods, clarifying approaches, and providing systematic frameworks, this fascicle serves as an indispensable guide for serious practitioners.

The emphasis on both precision (in the classification of afflictions) and flexibility (in the choice of methods) reflects the Tiantai tradition's characteristic balance of rigor and adaptability.

As we conclude this fascicle, we are reminded that the path to liberation, though demanding, is accessible to all who apply themselves with diligence, wisdom, and skillful means.

---

**[End of Fascicle 6]**

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Key Concepts Reviewed

- **破思假** (*pò sī jiǎ*): Breaking through thought-delusion
- **正三毒** (*zhèng sān dú*): The three correct poisons
- **九品** (*jiǔ pǐn*): Nine grades
- **七反** (*qī fǎn*): Seven returns
- **析觀** (*xī guān*): Analytical contemplation
- **體觀** (*tǐ guān*): Substantial contemplation
- **三假** (*sān jiǎ*): Three provisional designations
- **四門** (*sì mén*): Four gates

### Cross-References

- Previous: Fascicle 5 — Completion of the Ten Objects
- Next: Fascicle 7 — Further refinement of contemplative methods
- Related: Fascicle 3 — Initial treatment of the Aggregates Object
- Related: Fascicle 4 — Detailed treatment of the Afflictions Object

<!-- FASCICLE 07 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 7: Opening — The Latter Six Modes of Contemplation
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7 (止觀輔行傳弘決卷第七)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Overview

Fascicle 7 of the *Zhiguan Fuxing Zhuan Hongjue* (止觀輔行傳弘決) continues Miao-lo's commentary on Zhiyi's *Mohe Zhiguan*, addressing the latter six of the Ten Modes of Contemplation (十乘觀法). While Fascicles 5-6 treated the first four modes—establishing the foundational contemplation—this fascicle provides the diagnostic, therapeutic, and culminating practices necessary for complete realization.

---

## The Ten Modes: Structural Overview

The Ten Modes (十乘觀法) constitute Zhiyi's comprehensive methodology for contemplative practice. They function as a progressive yet interpenetrating system:

### Modes 1-4: Foundational Practice
1. **Contemplating the Inconceivable Object** (觀不思議境) — Ichinen Sanzen
2. **Arousing the True Bodhi-Mind** (發真正菩提心)
3. **Skillfully Settling the Mind** (善巧安心)
4. **Breaking Through All Dharmas** (破法遍)

### Modes 5-10: Diagnostic and Culminating Practice (This Fascicle)
5. **Discerning Openness and Obstruction** (通塞)
6. **Adjusting the Path Factors** (道品調適)
7. **Counteractive Aids for Opening** (對治助開)
8. **Knowing the Sequential Positions** (知次位)
9. **Patient Endurance** (安忍)
10. **Freedom from Dharma-Attachment** (無法愛)

---

## Mode 5: Discerning Openness and Obstruction (通塞)

### Doctrinal Context

The term 通塞 (tōng sè) denotes the practitioner's ability to discern whether the contemplative path is "open" (通) or "obstructed" (塞). This mode addresses a critical juncture in practice: having employed the breaking-through contemplation of Mode 4, the practitioner must now assess whether genuine progress has occurred or whether new obstructions have arisen.

Miao-lo states:
> 恐於通中翻起於塞
> "There is concern that within openness, obstruction may instead arise."

This indicates that even correct practice can generate attachment to the practice itself, thereby creating new obstacles.

### The Four-Sentence Examination (四句檢校)

Miao-lo presents a fourfold diagnostic:

1. **塞中得通** — Obtaining openness within obstruction
2. **通中有塞** — Finding obstruction within openness
3. **塞自是塞** — Obstruction that is simply obstruction
4. **通自是通** — Openness that is simply openness

This tetralemma ensures comprehensive examination of the practitioner's condition.

### The Metaphors

**Removing the Film, Nurturing the Pearl (除膜養珠)**
The eye covered by a film cannot see clearly. Removing the film reveals what was always present—the pearl of Buddha-nature. Practice does not create Buddha-nature but removes what obscures it.

**Destroying Bandits, Protecting Generals (破賊護將)**
If a general becomes a bandit, that general must also be destroyed. This metaphor indicates that no practice—however beneficial—is exempt from examination. Even the "general" of contemplation can become the "bandit" of attachment.

### The 500 Yojanas (五百由旬)

Miao-lo connects this mode to the *Lotus Sūtra*'s parable of the guide who leads travelers through a dangerous wilderness of 500 yojanas. The guide "knows openness and obstruction" (導師善知通塞). Miao-lo critiques six earlier interpretations of this passage, demonstrating that each captured only partial meaning.

### Four Types of Openness and Obstruction

1. **Horizontal (橫通塞)** — Viewing all afflictions simultaneously
2. **Vertical (竪通塞)** — Progressing through stages sequentially
3. **Horizontal-Distinct (橫別通塞)** — Different practitioners entering through different gates
4. **One-Mind (一心通塞)** — All three truths complete in a single moment of thought

The fourth—one-mind openness and obstruction—represents the distinctively Tiantai approach, wherein horizontal and vertical are integrated in non-dual contemplation.

---

## Mode 6: Adjusting the Path Factors (道品調適)

### The Thirty-Seven Factors of Awakening

This mode addresses the proper application of the traditional thirty-seven factors (三十七道品):

- Four Foundations of Mindfulness (四念處)
- Four Right Efforts (四正勤)
- Four Bases of Supernatural Power (四如意足)
- Five Faculties (五根)
- Five Powers (五力)
- Seven Factors of Awakening (七覺支)
- Noble Eightfold Path (八正道)

### The Tree Metaphor (道樹)

Following the *Mahāvibhāṣā* and *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*, Miao-lo presents the path factors as an organic structure:

- **Roots**: Four Foundations of Mindfulness
- **Trunk**: Four Right Efforts
- **Branches**: Four Bases of Power
- **Leaves**: Five Faculties and Powers
- **Flowers**: Seven Factors of Awakening
- **Fruit**: Noble Eightfold Path

This organic metaphor indicates that the factors support one another in systematic development.

### The Three Gates of Liberation (三脫門)

Miao-lo discusses how the path factors lead to the three liberative gates:

1. **Gate of Emptiness (空門)** — Realization of the emptiness of self and dharmas
2. **Gate of Signlessness (無相門)** — Freedom from grasping at characteristics
3. **Gate of Wishlessness (無作門)** — Cessation of craving for results

In the Perfect Teaching, these three are simultaneous aspects of a single liberative insight.

---

## Mode 7: Counteractive Aids for Opening (對治助開)

### The Rationale

When standard contemplation proves insufficient due to dull faculties (鈍根) or karmic obstructions (有遮), targeted treatment becomes necessary. Miao-lo specifies that practitioners with sharp faculties and no obstructions need not employ these aids, but most practitioners require them.

### The Six Obstructions and Six Perfections

| Obstruction (蔽) | Perfection (度) |
|------------------|-----------------|
| Stinginess (慳) | Generosity (布施) |
| Immorality (破戒) | Moral Discipline (持戒) |
| Anger (瞋) | Patience (忍辱) |
| Laziness (懈怠) | Effort (精進) |
| Distraction (散亂) | Concentration (禪定) |
| Ignorance (愚癡) | Wisdom (般若) |

### The Four Followings (四隨)

When direct treatment fails, Miao-lo provides a protocol for adjustment:

1. **Following the Treatment (隨對治)** — Apply the corresponding perfection directly
2. **Following with Turning (隨轉)** — If ineffective, turn to a different perfection
3. **Following with Combination (隨兼)** — Apply multiple perfections simultaneously
4. **Following the Ultimate (隨第一義)** — Abandon specific treatments; observe the mind directly

---

## Mode 8: Knowing the Sequential Positions (知次位)

### The Danger of Misidentification

Miao-lo emphasizes the grave consequences of claiming attainments not yet realized:
> 未入位未得謂得。恐極下根生重罪故。
> "Not having entered the position yet claiming to have attained—this may cause those of the dullest faculties to generate grave offenses."

### The Four Foundations of Mindfulness (四念處)

Before accurate self-assessment is possible, the practitioner must establish mindfulness through the four foundations:

1. **Mindfulness of Body (身念處)** — Contemplating the five impurities
2. **Mindfulness of Feelings (受念處)** — Contemplating the three sufferings
3. **Mindfulness of Mind (心念處)** — Contemplating impermanence
4. **Mindfulness of Dharmas (法念處)** — Contemplating non-self

### The Three Animals Metaphor

Miao-lo employs a metaphor from the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* to distinguish the three vehicles:

- **麞 (Small Deer)** — The Śrāvaka flees alone, seeking only personal escape
- **鹿 (Larger Deer)** — The Pratyekabuddha flees with backward glances of compassion
- **大象 (Great Elephant)** — The Bodhisattva remains to lead all beings to safety

### The Ten Methods for Harmonizing Teachings (十意融通)

Miao-lo presents ten hermeneutical principles for reconciling apparent contradictions among scriptures and treatises:

1. Clarifying the Principle (道理)
2. Understanding the Framework (綱格)
3. Resolving Names (釋名)
4. Distinguishing Functions (明用)
5. Clarifying the Goal (明宗)
6. Opening and Expanding (開拓)
7. Generating Structure (開章)
8. Textual Commentary (帖釋)
9. Translation Issues (翻譯)
10. Contemplation Practice (印心)

---

## Mode 9: Patient Endurance (安忍)

### Definition

安忍 combines 安 (settled, peaceful) with 忍 (endurance, patience). This mode addresses the practitioner's capacity to maintain stability when facing obstacles—both internal (doubt, fear, impatience) and external (criticism, hardship, temptation).

### The Five Repentances (五悔)

As a preparatory practice for the higher stages, Miao-lo elaborates the five repentances:

1. **懺悔** (Confession) — Acknowledging and revealing past wrongdoing
2. **勸請** (Entreaty) — Requesting Buddhas to remain in the world and teach
3. **隨喜** (Rejoicing) — Taking joy in the merit of others
4. **迴向** (Transfer of Merit) — Directing merit toward all beings and supreme awakening
5. **發願** (Vow) — Establishing the four great vows

---

## Mode 10: Freedom from Dharma-Attachment (無法愛)

### The Final Obstacle

Having traversed the previous nine modes, the practitioner faces the subtlest obstruction: attachment to the Dharma itself. Miao-lo indicates that even love of the teaching constitutes a form of grasping that must be released.

### The Raft Metaphor

The traditional metaphor applies: the Dharma is a raft for crossing to the other shore. Upon arrival, the raft must be abandoned. Continued attachment to the raft—however useful it was during the crossing—becomes an impediment on solid ground.

### Non-Attachment to Non-Attachment

This mode does not advocate rejection of the Dharma but rather freedom from fixation upon it. The teaching remains valid and beneficial; the practitioner simply ceases to grasp it as an object of craving.

---

## Structure of This Fascicle

Fascicle 7 is divided into four parts in the CBETA edition:

- **Part 1 (卷第七之一)**: Mode 5 — Openness and Obstruction
- **Part 2 (卷第七之二)**: Modes 6-7 — Path Factors and Counteractive Aids
- **Part 3 (卷第七之三)**: Mode 8 (partial) — Four Foundations, Three Animals
- **Part 4 (卷第七之四)**: Modes 8-10 — Stages, Endurance, Freedom from Attachment

---

## Significance

Fascicle 7 completes the exposition of the Ten Modes, providing the diagnostic and therapeutic apparatus necessary for addressing obstacles in practice. The modes presented here are not merely supplementary but integral to the Tiantai contemplative system. Without the capacity to diagnose obstruction (Mode 5), adjust method (Modes 6-7), accurately assess progress (Mode 8), endure difficulty (Mode 9), and release attachment to practice itself (Mode 10), the earlier modes remain incomplete.

The fascicle thus represents the maturation of contemplative methodology—the transition from establishing practice to perfecting it.

---

## Bibliography

- T46n1912: 止觀輔行傳弘決 (Zhiguan Fuxing Zhuan Hongjue)
- T46n1911: 摩訶止觀 (Mohe Zhiguan)

- T27n1545: 阿毘達磨大毘婆沙論 (Mahāvibhāṣā)
- T09n262: 妙法蓮華經 (Lotus Sūtra)
- T12n374: 大般涅槃經 (Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra)
