<!-- FASCICLE 07 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 7: Section 1 — Discerning Openness and Obstruction
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7, Part 1 (卷第七之一)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Mode 5: Discerning Openness and Obstruction (通塞)

### I. Introduction and Purpose (來意)

Miao-lo opens his treatment of Mode 5 by establishing its relationship to the preceding mode:

> 次釋通塞中先列異名。次明來意有法譬合。

"Next, in explaining openness and obstruction, we first list the alternative names, then clarify the purpose through dharma, metaphor, and their combination."

The fundamental purpose of this mode is diagnostic: having employed the breaking-through contemplation (破法遍) of Mode 4, the practitioner must now assess whether genuine progress has occurred. Miao-lo identifies a critical danger:

> 恐於通中翻起於塞

"There is concern that within openness, obstruction may instead arise."

This indicates that even correct contemplation can generate new obstacles—specifically, attachment to the contemplation itself. When the practitioner becomes proud of "breaking through," this very pride becomes a new obstruction.

### II. The Rationale for Four-Sentence Examination

Miao-lo explains why the four-sentence examination is necessary:

> 故知前破遍中但用能破破於所破。猶未悟者必於能所心生塞著。

"Therefore, in the previous breaking-through, one simply used the breaking capacity to break what is to be broken. Those who have not yet awakened will certainly generate obstruction and attachment regarding the capacity and its object."

The four sentences (四句) address this problem systematically:

1. **通途通塞** — General openness and obstruction: the object of breaking is obstruction; the capacity to break is openness.

2. **別相通塞** — Specific openness and obstruction: attachment arising regarding the capacity itself becomes obstruction; breaking that attachment without generating new attachment becomes openness.

### III. The Four Meanings (四義)

Miao-lo identifies four dimensions of openness and obstruction:

> 然今此中總有四義。一橫通塞。二竪通塞。三橫別通塞。四一心通塞。

"Now, in this context there are altogether four meanings: (1) horizontal openness-obstruction, (2) vertical openness-obstruction, (3) horizontal-distinct openness-obstruction, and (4) one-mind openness-obstruction."

#### 1. Horizontal (橫通塞)
All afflictions—view-delusions, thought-delusions, dust-and-sand delusions, and ignorance—are examined simultaneously. Openness consists in perceiving all as empty; obstruction consists in becoming fixed on any particular one.

#### 2. Vertical (竪通塞)
The practitioner progresses through stages sequentially: first breaking view-delusions, then thought-delusions, and so forth. Openness consists in advancing from stage to stage; obstruction consists in stalling at any level.

#### 3. Horizontal-Distinct (橫別通塞)
Different practitioners enter realization through different gates (emptiness, provisional existence, middle). Openness consists in finding one's appropriate gate; obstruction consists in asserting that one's gate is the only valid approach.

#### 4. One-Mind (一心通塞)
This represents the distinctively Tiantai integration. All three truths are present in a single moment of thought, with no temporal sequence or division among practitioners. This mode subsumes and perfects the previous three.

> 文四義二。前三為成一心故也。

"The text has four meanings but two [categories]: the first three exist for the purpose of accomplishing one-mind."

### IV. The Four-Sentence Diagnostic (四句檢校)

The examination proceeds through four sentences:

> 一者塞中得通。二者通中有塞。三者塞自是塞。四者通自是通。

1. **塞中得通** (Obtaining openness within obstruction): What appears to be blockage may contain the seed of breakthrough. The very recognition of obstruction can become the occasion for liberation.

2. **通中有塞** (Finding obstruction within openness): What appears to be progress may conceal subtle attachment. Pride in one's contemplation creates new bondage.

3. **塞自是塞** (Obstruction that is simply obstruction): Some obstacles are straightforward impediments requiring direct dismantling.

4. **通自是通** (Openness that is simply openness): Sometimes the path is genuinely clear, and second-guessing constitutes its own form of obstruction.

### V. The Metaphors

#### A. Removing the Film, Nurturing the Pearl (除膜養珠)

> 除膜養珠但成分喻

"Removing the film and nurturing the pearl constitutes only a partial metaphor."

The eye covered by a cataract-like film (膜) cannot see clearly. Removing the film reveals what was always present. This metaphor illustrates that practice does not create Buddha-nature but removes what obscures it.

However, Miao-lo notes this metaphor is incomplete because it does not address the possibility that "the pearl might become the film"—that is, what functions as openness might subsequently function as obstruction.

#### B. Destroying Bandits, Protecting Generals (破賊護將)

> 若破賊護將便成遍喻

"If [we use] destroying bandits and protecting generals, it becomes a complete metaphor."

This metaphor addresses the limitation of the first:

> 將若為賊此賊亦破。賊若為將此將亦護。如是展轉將皆為賊。節節破之。

"If a general becomes a bandit, that bandit must also be destroyed. If a bandit becomes a general, that general should also be protected. Thus, in succession, all generals may become bandits. Section by section, they must be destroyed."

No practice—however beneficial—is exempt from scrutiny. The contemplation that once served as "general" (openness) may become "bandit" (obstruction) through attachment.

### VI. The 500 Yojanas (五百由旬)

Miao-lo connects this mode to the *Lotus Sūtra*'s parable in Chapter 7 (化城喻品):

> 經云導師善知通塞。故用法華將導譬也。

"The sūtra states that the guide 'knows well openness and obstruction.' Therefore we use the *Lotus* metaphor of the guide."

#### Critique of Six Masters

Miao-lo examines and critiques six earlier interpretations of the 500 yojanas:

**First Master**: Identified 400 yojanas with the two vehicles (seventh and eighth grounds of the Shared Teaching).

*Miao-lo's critique*: This confuses the stages. The *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* correlates the two vehicles with 400 yojanas differently.

**Second Master** (攝論師 - Shèlùn school): Proposed seven kinds of birth-and-death corresponding to 500 yojanas.

*Miao-lo's critique*: This interpretation omits the final two kinds (有後, 無後 - "with remainder" and "without remainder").

**Third Master** (地論師 - Dìlùn school): Correlated the positions (住位) with 200 yojanas.

*Miao-lo's critique*: This places the "magic city" (化城) within the three realms, which contradicts the sūtra's intent.

**Fourth Master**: Identified the two vehicles as completing all 500 yojanas.

*Miao-lo's critique*: This has the two vehicles "usurping authority" (擅行) over territory they should not traverse.

**Fifth and Sixth Masters**: Various correlations with afflictions.

*Miao-lo's critique*: Each captures only partial meaning.

#### The Tiantai Interpretation

> 今明下今家正解。正顯今家依法華明通塞義也。

Miao-lo presents the Tiantai interpretation based on three correlations:

1. **Correlation with Birth-and-Death (約生死)**: The 500 yojanas represent the complete cycle of saṃsāra.

2. **Correlation with Afflictions (約煩惱)**:
   - View-delusions across three realms = 100
   - Lower fetters (下分結) = 100
   - Upper fetters (上分結) = 100
   - Dust-and-sand delusions = 100
   - Ignorance = 100

3. **Correlation with Contemplative Wisdom (約觀智)**: Through contemplation, one "passes through" (過) these 500 yojanas by knowing them as empty.

### VII. One-Mind Openness and Obstruction

The culminating teaching integrates all dimensions:

> 若一心下正明一心。即是檢校一心破遍。

"One-mind [openness-obstruction] precisely refers to examining one-mind breaking-through."

#### Breaking Vertical Obstruction

> 一心三觀破竪通塞

"One-mind three contemplations break vertical openness-obstruction."

The three contemplations (of emptiness, provisional existence, and the middle) are not sequential but simultaneous. This breaks the vertical arrangement wherein one proceeds from stage to stage.

#### Breaking Horizontal Obstruction

> 三觀一心破橫通塞

"Three contemplations, one mind, break horizontal openness-obstruction."

The three contemplations are not divided among different practitioners but are all present in a single mind. This breaks the horizontal arrangement wherein different people approach through different gates.

#### The Integration

> 空即三觀等者。次圓三觀。觀觀具三以破橫別。

"Emptiness is precisely the three contemplations, etc. Next, the Perfect three contemplations—each contemplation contains all three, breaking horizontal-distinct [obstruction]."

In the Perfect Teaching:
- Contemplation of emptiness contains provisional and middle
- Contemplation of provisional contains emptiness and middle
- Contemplation of middle contains emptiness and provisional

This mutual inclusion (圓融) characterizes the Tiantai approach.

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | Definition |
|---------|--------|------------|
| 通塞 | tōng sè | Openness and obstruction |
| 四句檢校 | sì jù jiǎn jiào | Four-sentence examination |
| 除膜養珠 | chú mó yǎng zhū | Removing film, nurturing pearl |
| 破賊護將 | pò zéi hù jiàng | Destroying bandits, protecting generals |
| 五百由旬 | wǔ bǎi yóu xún | 500 yojanas |
| 橫通塞 | héng tōng sè | Horizontal openness-obstruction |
| 竪通塞 | shù tōng sè | Vertical openness-obstruction |
| 一心通塞 | yī xīn tōng sè | One-mind openness-obstruction |
| 一心三觀 | yī xīn sān guān | One-mind three contemplations |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Mode 5 serves as the pivotal diagnostic function within the Ten Modes. Without the capacity to discern openness and obstruction, the practitioner cannot:

1. Recognize when correct practice has generated new attachment
2. Adjust methodology appropriately
3. Avoid the error of claiming attainment not yet realized
4. Progress from the foundational modes (1-4) to the culminating modes (6-10)

The one-mind approach to openness and obstruction distinguishes Tiantai from gradual approaches that treat stages sequentially and from approaches that assign different gates to different practitioners. In Tiantai, all dimensions are integrated in the single moment of contemplative awareness.



---



<!-- FASCICLE 07 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 7: Section 2 — Path Factors and Counteractive Aids
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7, Part 2 (卷第七之二)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Mode 6: Adjusting the Path Factors (道品調適)

### I. The Tree of the Path (道樹)

Miao-lo opens his treatment of Mode 6 by presenting the traditional metaphor of the path factors as a tree:

> 次舉譬者。婆沙大論並依樹譬道品。故名道樹。

"Next, presenting the metaphor: both the *[Mahā]vibhāṣā* and the *Great Treatise* use the tree metaphor for the path factors. Therefore it is called 'the tree of the path.'"

The organic structure is as follows:

| Component | Path Factors |
|-----------|--------------|
| Roots (根) | Four Foundations of Mindfulness (四念處) |
| Trunk (幹) | Four Right Efforts (四正勤) |
| Branches (枝) | Four Bases of Supernatural Power (四如意足) |
| Leaves (葉) | Five Faculties and Five Powers (五根五力) |
| Flowers (華) | Seven Factors of Awakening (七覺支) |
| Fruit (果) | Noble Eightfold Path (八正道) |

Miao-lo notes that both the *Mahāvibhāṣā* and the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra* employ this metaphor, though with slight variations:

> 二論並與今文大同。但今結果位在初住。與婆沙異。

"Both treatises largely agree with the present text. However, the present text places the fruit-position at the first abiding [stage], differing from the *Vibhāṣā*."

This indicates that while Miao-lo draws on Abhidharma sources, he interprets them through the Tiantai lens of the Six Identities and progressive positions.

### II. The Three Kinds of Spiritual Guides (三善知識)

The path factors function as spiritual guides in three capacities:

> 道品善知識下結前合譬。準方便中有三知識。

"The path factors as spiritual guides—this concludes and combines the previous metaphor. According to the expedient [section], there are three kinds of guides."

1. **同行善知識** (Companion Guide): The path factors accompany the practitioner, walking the same path.

2. **教授善知識** (Teaching Guide): The path factors instruct by showing what to cultivate next.
   > 由是道品令行無誤
   > "Through these path factors, practice proceeds without error."

3. **外護善知識** (Protective Guide): The path factors guard against deviation.
   > 諸品防護妄誤不侵
   > "The various factors protect and guard; error and delusion do not intrude."

### III. The Three Gates of Liberation (三脫門)

Miao-lo transitions to discuss how the path factors culminate in the three liberative gates:

> 次明三脫門者。明道品功能。

"Next, explaining the three gates of liberation: this clarifies the function of the path factors."

#### Definition from the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*

> 大論二十一問。涅槃唯一門何故三耶。答。法一義三。三秖是一。

"The *Great Treatise* [chapter] twenty-one asks: 'Nirvāṇa has only one gate—why then three?' Answer: 'The dharma is one, the meanings are three. The three are precisely one.'"

The three gates are:

1. **空門** (Gate of Emptiness): Realization that self and dharmas lack inherent existence
2. **無相門** (Gate of Signlessness): Freedom from grasping fixed characteristics
3. **無作門** (Gate of Wishlessness): Cessation of craving for results

#### Differentiation by Teaching

Miao-lo distinguishes how each teaching approaches the three gates:

**Tripiṭaka Teaching (三藏教)**:
> 初三藏教約十六行以分三門
"The Tripiṭaka Teaching uses the sixteen aspects [of the Four Noble Truths] to differentiate the three gates."

**Shared Teaching (通教)**:
> 通教從初但觀幻化。是故不復依十六行。
"The Shared Teaching from the beginning only contemplates illusion. Therefore it does not rely on the sixteen aspects."

**Distinct Teaching (別教)**:
> 次明別教先通約三觀。次別約出假。
"Next, explaining the Distinct Teaching: first generally in terms of the three contemplations, then specifically in terms of emerging into the provisional."

**Perfect Teaching (圓教)**:
> 故圓教中空即無相無作故也
"Therefore in the Perfect Teaching, emptiness is precisely signlessness and wishlessness."

In the Perfect Teaching, the three gates are not sequential stages but simultaneous aspects of a single liberative insight.

---

## Mode 7: Counteractive Aids for Opening (對治助開)

### I. The Rationale for Counteractive Aids

Miao-lo explains when and why these aids become necessary:

> 次釋對治助開。初明來意中。先剋出所助之體。故云為一切三昧作本。

"Next, explaining counteractive aids for opening. In first clarifying the purpose, we initially specify the entity being aided. Therefore [the text] states: '[these aids] serve as the foundation for all samādhis.'"

The fundamental condition requiring counteractive aids:

> 根利下正約根遮以簡助意。四句之中三句不須。唯根鈍有遮用此對治。

"From 'sharp faculties' onward, we precisely use faculties and obstructions to differentiate the meaning of aids. Among the four sentences, three do not require [aids]. Only the dull-facultied with obstructions employ this counteractive [approach]."

The four combinations are:

1. **Sharp faculties, no obstructions (利根無遮)**: No aids needed
2. **Sharp faculties, with obstructions (利根有遮)**: Some aids may help
3. **Dull faculties, no obstructions (鈍根無遮)**: Standard practice suffices
4. **Dull faculties, with obstructions (鈍根有遮)**: Counteractive aids essential

### II. The Six Obstructions (六蔽)

Miao-lo presents the six obstructions that impede practice:

> 助道無量下明用治所依。助道無量但用六者為對六蔽。

"'Aids to the path are immeasurable'—this clarifies the basis for applying treatment. Though aids are immeasurable, we use [only] six because they counter the six obstructions."

| Chinese | Sanskrit equivalent | Meaning |
|---------|---------------------|---------|
| 慳 | mātsarya | Stinginess |
| 破戒 | dauḥśīlya | Immorality |
| 瞋 | pratigha | Anger |
| 懈怠 | kausīdya | Laziness |
| 散亂 | vikṣepa | Distraction |
| 愚癡 | moha | Ignorance |

### III. The Six Perfections as Treatment (六度)

Each obstruction has its corresponding perfection as antidote:

| Obstruction | Perfection | Treatment Logic |
|-------------|------------|-----------------|
| Stinginess (慳) | Generosity (布施) | Opens the closed fist |
| Immorality (破戒) | Moral Discipline (持戒) | Restores ethical foundation |
| Anger (瞋) | Patience (忍辱) | Cools the burning |
| Laziness (懈怠) | Effort (精進) | Energizes practice |
| Distraction (散亂) | Concentration (禪定) | Settles the scattered mind |
| Ignorance (愚癡) | Wisdom (般若) | Illuminates darkness |

### IV. The Four Followings (四隨)

When direct treatment proves ineffective, Miao-lo provides a protocol for adjustment:

> 若用下具辯四隨迴轉治相。前之對治已用四中一對治竟。忽非其宜。應須轉用。

"From 'if using' onward, we thoroughly explain the characteristics of treatment through the four followings and their turning. The previous counteractive [approach] has already employed one of the four treatments. If suddenly it proves unsuitable, one should turn to use [another]."

#### 1. Following the Treatment (隨對治)

> 如治一慳者。略於一蔽示四隨相。

"For example, treating one [case of] stinginess: briefly, for one obstruction, we show the four following-characteristics."

The direct approach: apply the corresponding perfection. Stinginess → practice generosity.

#### 2. Following with Turning (隨轉)

If direct treatment fails:

> 所言轉者。或轉用尸乃至般若。或次或超超一至四。

"What is meant by 'turning': perhaps turn to use moral discipline, and so on up to wisdom. Either sequentially or by skipping—skipping from one to four."

The practitioner tries a different perfection. Perhaps the stinginess is rooted in fear (use moral discipline for security) or in confusion (use wisdom).

#### 3. Following with Combination (隨兼)

> 若至五度名轉名具

"If reaching [all] five perfections, it is called 'turning' [and] called 'complete.'"

Multiple perfections may be applied simultaneously: generosity + effort + wisdom, for instance.

#### 4. Following the Ultimate (隨第一義)

> 言第一義者。唯觀無相真如寂理。

"What is meant by 'ultimate truth': only contemplating signless true suchness, quiescent principle."

Abandoning specific treatments, the practitioner directly observes the mind. The obstruction is seen to lack inherent nature; treatment and obstruction are both empty.

### V. Treatment Across the Four Teachings

Miao-lo extends the counteractive framework across all four teachings:

> 若以四教展轉為治以前助後者。還以前六轉望後教六蔽之相。

"If using the four teachings to treat in succession, using the earlier to aid the later: we turn to use the earlier six [perfections] in relation to the later teaching's six-obstruction characteristics."

- **Shared Teaching practitioners** may use Tripiṭaka perfections
- **Distinct Teaching practitioners** may use both Tripiṭaka and Shared perfections
- **Perfect Teaching practitioners** may use all three previous sets

This creates a comprehensive therapeutic arsenal.

### VI. The Five Tastes (五味)

Miao-lo connects the path factors to the five periods of Buddhist teaching using the milk metaphor from the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*:

> 又華嚴下約五味判

"Furthermore, from 'Huayan' onward, we judge according to the five tastes."

| Period | Taste | Characteristic |
|--------|-------|----------------|
| Huayan | Fresh milk (乳) | Rich but difficult to digest |
| Āgama | Cream (酪) | More accessible |
| Vaipulya | Curds (生酥) | Transitional |
| Prajñā | Butter (熟酥) | Refined |
| Lotus/Nirvāṇa | Ghee (醍醐) | Ultimate refinement |

#### The Twin Trees (枯榮)

The *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*'s image of twin trees—one withered (枯), one flourishing (榮)—represents:

> 初華嚴中雖具二義文多明別。故云偏多四榮。

"In the Huayan [period], though both meanings are present, the text mostly clarifies the distinct [teaching]. Therefore we say it 'leans toward the four flourishing.'"

- **Withered trees**: Represent the two vehicles (śrāvaka, pratyekabuddha)
- **Flourishing trees**: Represent the bodhisattva path

In the Lotus and Nirvāṇa sūtras, the withered become flourishing—all paths are revealed as leading to the one vehicle.

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | Definition |
|---------|--------|------------|
| 道品調適 | dào pǐn tiáo shì | Adjusting the path factors |
| 三十七道品 | sān shí qī dào pǐn | Thirty-seven factors of awakening |
| 道樹 | dào shù | Tree of the path |
| 三善知識 | sān shàn zhī shí | Three kinds of spiritual guides |
| 三脫門 | sān tuō mén | Three gates of liberation |
| 對治助開 | duì zhì zhù kāi | Counteractive aids for opening |
| 六蔽 | liù bì | Six obstructions |
| 六度 | liù dù | Six perfections |
| 四隨 | sì suí | Four followings |
| 五味 | wǔ wèi | Five tastes |
| 枯榮 | kū róng | Withered and flourishing |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Modes 6 and 7 constitute the therapeutic dimension of the Ten Modes. Having diagnosed obstruction in Mode 5, the practitioner now adjusts methodology (Mode 6) and applies targeted treatment (Mode 7).

The integration of Abhidharma path-factor analysis with Tiantai three-truth contemplation represents a distinctive synthesis. The 37 factors are not merely graduated practices but are understood through the lens of the three truths—empty, provisional, and middle—allowing for flexible application across all four teachings.

The four followings provide a practical protocol for when standard approaches fail, moving from direct treatment through various adjustments to ultimate contemplation. This ensures that no practitioner remains stuck due to rigid adherence to a single method.



---



<!-- FASCICLE 07 SECTION 03 -->

# Fascicle 7: Section 3 — Knowing the Stages (Part 1)
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7, Part 3 (卷第七之三)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Mode 8: Knowing the Sequential Positions (知次位) — Part 1

### I. The Purpose of Knowing Positions

Miao-lo opens with a discussion of wisdom (般若) and its relation to the four foundations of mindfulness, then transitions to the critical importance of knowing one's stage:

> 次明知次位者。先明來意。既用前七以為所修。二世善發入位不定。或未入位未得謂得。恐極下根生重罪故。故須明位令行者識之。

"Next, explaining knowing the sequential positions. First clarifying the purpose: having used the previous seven [modes] as what is cultivated, wholesome [states] from both lives may arise, but entering the position is uncertain. Perhaps one has not entered the position yet claims to have attained. Fearing that those of the dullest faculties may generate grave offenses, therefore we must clarify the positions to enable practitioners to recognize them."

This passage establishes the critical danger of misidentification. Claiming attainments not yet realized constitutes a grave offense (重罪), potentially leading to deterioration of practice and misleading of others.

### II. The Structure of Positions

Miao-lo identifies two categories of positions across the four teachings:

> 四教各有真似二位。在賢名似入聖名真。

"Each of the four teachings has two [types of] positions: true and resemblance. Being in the [stage of] worthies is called 'resemblance'; entering sagehood is called 'true.'"

- **Resemblance positions (似位)**: The preparatory stages where realization is approximated but not yet complete
- **True positions (真位)**: The stages of genuine attainment

### III. The Metaphor of Purple and Vermillion

> 次舉喻云朱紫等者。朱正色如真位。紫間色如似位。如赤黑合以成於紫。真妄尚雜故名為似。

"Next, the metaphor 'vermillion and purple, etc.': vermillion, a primary color, is like the true position. Purple, an intermediate color, is like the resemblance position. Just as red and black combine to produce purple, the true and false are still mixed—therefore it is called 'resemblance.'"

This metaphor from traditional Chinese color theory illustrates how resemblance positions contain elements of both realization and delusion, unlike the pure "vermillion" of true attainment.

### IV. The Four Foundations of Mindfulness (四念處)

Miao-lo presents an extended treatment of the four foundations as the basis for accurate self-assessment.

#### A. Mindfulness of Body: The Five Impurities (五種不淨)

**1. Seed Impurity (種子不淨)**

> 初言種子者。如論偈曰。是身種不淨非是妙寶物。不從白淨生。父母邪想憶念風吹淫欲之火。

"First, regarding 'seed': as the treatise verse states, 'This body's seed is impure, not a wondrous treasure. It does not arise from the white and pure. Parents' wrong thoughts, recollection, wind-blown fire of desire...'"

The body originates from parental sexual union—described as "grasping another's leavings" (攬他遺體). The red and white drops (赤白精) are the biological materials from which the embryo develops.

**2. Place Impurity (住處不淨)**

> 生處不淨者。如論偈曰。是身為臭穢不從華開生。亦不從薝蔔又不出寶山。

"The birthplace is impure: as the treatise verse states, 'This body is foul and filthy; it does not arise from flowers blooming, nor from champaka blossoms, nor does it emerge from treasure mountains.'"

The womb environment is characterized as "vile and crowded" (猥者雜惡也).

**3. Characteristic Impurity (自相不淨)**

> 自相不淨者。衣服澡浴種種華香。飴以上饌眾妙美味。經宿之間皆為不淨。

"Self-characteristic impurity: clothing and bathing, various flowers and fragrances, fed with superior dishes and many wonderful flavors—after a night's passage, all becomes impure."

Even with the finest adornments and foods, the body's nature produces waste:

> 假令衣天衣食天食以身性故。亦成不淨。

"Even if clothed in heavenly garments and fed heavenly food, due to the body's nature, it also becomes impure."

**4. Self-Nature Impurity (自性不淨)**

> 自性不淨者。大小便道目淚涕睡常流不息。如論偈云。種種不淨物充滿於其身。常流出不止如漏囊盛物。

"Self-nature impurity: the paths of urine and feces, tears, nasal discharge, saliva—constantly flowing without cease. As the treatise verse states, 'Various impure substances fill this body, constantly flowing out without stopping, like a leaky bag containing things.'"

The body continuously produces and excretes impure substances.

**5. Ultimate Impurity (究竟不淨)**

> 究竟不淨者。如論偈曰。審諦觀此身終必歸死處。

"Ultimate impurity: as the treatise verse states, 'Carefully and truly contemplate this body—in the end it certainly returns to death.'"

The body's final destination is decomposition and dissolution.

#### B. Mindfulness of Feelings: The Three Sufferings (三苦)

Miao-lo presents the three sufferings through the *Abhidharmakośa* metaphor:

> 故俱舍云。如以一睫毛置掌而不覺。若置眼睛上為損及不安。凡夫如手掌不覺行苦睫。智者如眼睛緣極生厭怖。

"Therefore the *Kośa* states: 'Like placing a single eyelash on the palm—one does not perceive it. If placed on the eyeball, it causes harm and disturbance. Ordinary beings are like the palm, not perceiving the eyelash of the suffering of conditioning. The wise are like the eye, and through contact with the extreme, aversion and fear arise.'"

| Type | Chinese | Characteristic |
|------|---------|----------------|
| Suffering of suffering | 苦苦 | Obvious pain: illness, injury |
| Suffering of change | 壞苦 | Pleasure that ends |
| Suffering of conditioning | 行苦 | Pervasive unsatisfactoriness |

#### C. Mindfulness of Mind: Impermanence

**The Cage and Bird Metaphor**

> 如繫鳥等者。如論偈云。鳥來入瓶中羅𣪺揜瓶口。縠穿鳥飛去神明隨業走。

"Regarding 'like a caged bird, etc.': as the treatise verse states, 'The bird enters the bottle, the net-cover seals the opening. When the silk tears, the bird flies away; the spirit follows karma and departs.'"

- The body (bottle/cage) = the physical form
- Consciousness (bird) = the mental continuum
- Karma (net) = the binding force
- Death = the breaking of the container

**The Wax Seal Metaphor**

From the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*:

> 印壞文成等者。重顯籠瓶文仍少異。籠瓶二喻但喻因果。此蠟印喻更加中陰。

"'The seal is destroyed, the pattern appears'—this again reveals [the meaning of] cage and bottle, though the text differs slightly. The two metaphors of cage and bottle only illustrate cause and effect. This wax-seal metaphor additionally includes the intermediate state."

> 如蠟印印泥印與泥合印滅文成。

"Like a wax seal pressed into clay: the seal combines with the clay, the seal is destroyed, the pattern appears."

This illustrates the relationship between:
- Present life (the wax seal)
- Intermediate state (the pressing)
- Future life (the pattern in clay)

#### D. Mindfulness of Dharmas: Non-Self

**The Ghost Room Story (鬼房)**

Miao-lo recounts from the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*:

> 如一山中有一佛圖。中有別房常有鬼來。故諸道人皆捨此房。

"In a certain mountain there was a Buddhist temple. Within it was a particular room where ghosts constantly came. Therefore all the monks abandoned this room."

Two monks, each unaware of the other, take residence in the room. Each mistakes the other for a ghost. They struggle throughout the night, only to discover at dawn that they were old friends fighting phantoms.

> 眾生亦爾。五陰本來無人無我。空取其相生於鬪諍。

"Sentient beings are likewise. The five aggregates originally have no person, no self. Vainly grasping their characteristics, they generate conflict."

**The Two Ghosts and the Corpse (二鬼爭屍)**

From the *Great Treatise*:

> 如人遠行獨宿空亭。夜中有鬼擔一死屍。來著其前。復有一鬼從後而來。瞋罵前鬼云。是我屍何以擔來。

"A person traveling far lodged alone in an empty pavilion. In the night, a ghost came carrying a corpse and placed it before him. Another ghost came from behind, angrily cursing the first: 'This is my corpse! Why did you carry it here?'"

The traveler, asked to adjudicate, tells the truth and is torn apart. But the first ghost replaces each limb with parts from the corpse. By morning, the traveler's entire body has been replaced:

> 其人思惟。此之二鬼皆有大力。實語虛語皆不免死。我今不應妄語答鬼。

"The person thought: 'These two ghosts both have great power. Whether I speak truth or falsehood, I cannot avoid death. I should not answer the ghosts with false words.'"

The traveler wanders to a monastery, where monks teach him:

> 此人自知己身無我易可化度。

"This person himself knew his body has no self—he is easily transformed and liberated."

### V. The Three Animals Metaphor

Miao-lo employs the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*'s metaphor to distinguish the three vehicles:

#### The Small Deer (麞) — Śrāvaka

> 聲聞觀法義之如麞。生老病死猶如獵圍。三界五欲猶如水草。不遑視聽如不暇飲噉。希離二縛如志在免脫。

"The śrāvaka's contemplation is like the small deer. Birth, aging, sickness, and death are like the hunter's encirclement. The three realms and five desires are like water and grass. Not having leisure to look or listen, like not having time to drink or eat. Hoping to escape the two bonds, intent on liberation."

The śrāvaka flees danger single-mindedly, without looking back or helping others.

#### The Larger Deer (鹿) — Pratyekabuddha

> 部行說法名為悲鳴。但讚出苦名為呦咽。

"Teaching the Dharma to groups is called 'mournful crying.' Simply praising escape from suffering is called 'plaintive sound.'"

The pratyekabuddha flees but looks back with compassion:

> 有少悲心似住生死。利物事微名為何益。說已入般。

"Having some compassion, seemingly abiding in birth-and-death. Benefiting beings in small matters—what benefit is this called? Having taught, [the pratyekabuddha] enters parinirvāṇa."

#### The Great Elephant (大象) — Bodhisattva

> 大象合菩薩... 以智去至俱安者。合令群安隱。

"The great elephant corresponds to the bodhisattva... 'With wisdom departing until all are secure' corresponds to 'causing the herd to be safe and hidden.'"

The bodhisattva:
- Knows its own great power
- Endures the dangers of birth-and-death
- Leads all beings across to safety

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | Definition |
|---------|--------|------------|
| 知次位 | zhī cì wèi | Knowing the sequential positions |
| 真位 | zhēn wèi | True position |
| 似位 | sì wèi | Resemblance position |
| 四念處 | sì niàn chù | Four foundations of mindfulness |
| 五種不淨 | wǔ zhǒng bù jìng | Five kinds of impurity |
| 三苦 | sān kǔ | Three sufferings |
| 籠瓶 | lóng píng | Cage and bottle |
| 蠟印印泥 | là yìn yìn ní | Wax seal [pressed] in clay |
| 鬼房 | guǐ fáng | Ghost room |
| 二鬼爭屍 | èr guǐ zhēng shī | Two ghosts [arguing over] a corpse |
| 麞 | zhāng | Small deer (śrāvaka) |
| 鹿 | lù | Deer (pratyekabuddha) |
| 大象 | dà xiàng | Great elephant (bodhisattva) |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Mode 8 addresses the danger of premature claims to attainment—a persistent problem in contemplative traditions. By establishing the four foundations of mindfulness as prerequisites for accurate self-assessment, Miao-lo ensures that practitioners develop the discernment necessary to evaluate their own progress.

The three animals metaphor provides a framework for understanding different levels of aspiration and capacity. The Tiantai ideal is the elephant—the bodhisattva who leads all beings to liberation—but practitioners must honestly assess whether they currently function as deer or elephant.

The extended treatment of impermanence through the cage-and-bird and wax-seal metaphors emphasizes the urgency of practice while undermining attachment to the physical body as the locus of self.



---



<!-- FASCICLE 07 SECTION 04 -->

# Fascicle 7: Section 4 — Stages, Endurance, and Freedom
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7, Part 4 (卷第七之四)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Mode 8: Knowing the Sequential Positions (知次位) — Part 2

### I. The Ten Methods for Harmonizing Teachings (十意融通)

Miao-lo presents ten hermeneutical principles for reconciling apparent contradictions among Buddhist scriptures and treatises:

> 次以十意融通經論者。

"Next, using the ten meanings to harmonize sūtras and treatises."

He notes that these should not be confused with the Ten Modes of contemplation:

> 人見十意之初有諦理之言。便將十乘以對十意。深不便也。

"People see that at the beginning of the ten meanings there is discussion of 'truth and principle.' They then correlate the Ten Modes with the ten meanings. This is deeply inconvenient [i.e., mistaken]."

#### The Ten Methods

1. **道理** (Clarifying the Principle): Different teachings describe different aspects of the same reality.

2. **綱格** (Understanding the Framework): Using the eight teachings (頓漸祕密不定 + 藏通別圓) to classify and sort doctrinal statements.
   > 以教判釋得教遠度。故云綱格。
   > "Through doctrinal classification and interpretation, one obtains the far-reaching scope of the teaching. Therefore it is called 'framework.'"

3. **釋名** (Resolving Names): The same word may carry different meanings in different contexts.
   > 名雖有八用必不俱
   > "Though names number eight, their uses are certainly not simultaneous."

4. **明用** (Distinguishing Functions): Different methods serve different purposes.
   > 祛執遣迷。有執咸破。
   > "Dispelling attachment and removing delusion. All attachments are broken."

5. **明宗** (Clarifying the Goal): Despite different methods, the ultimate aim is one.
   > 位是所階行是能修。能所相帶則方便與證因果不混。
   > "Position is what is ascended; practice is what cultivates. Capacity and object mutually accompany, so expedient [means] and realization, cause and effect, are not confused."

6. **開拓** (Opening and Expanding): Not rigidly boxing the teachings; allowing interpenetration.
   > 開拓法門使佛意不壅
   > "Opening and expanding the Dharma-gate so that the Buddha's intention is not obstructed."

7. **開章** (Generating Structure): Seeing how sections build upon one another.
   > 如諸義疏凡開章段。無不生起如鉤鎖也。
   > "Like all commentaries that divide into sections—none fail to arise [from previous sections] like linked hooks."

8. **帖釋** (Textual Commentary): Careful reading adhering closely to the actual words.
   > 復須附文次第帖釋。不廣不略有事有理。
   > "One must also follow the text and explain in sequence. Neither too broad nor too brief, with both phenomena and principle."

9. **翻譯** (Translation Issues): Recognizing that some problems arise from translation rather than doctrine.
   > 方音不同非關義理不可卒備。
   > "Regional pronunciations differ—this is unrelated to meaning and principle; [such matters] cannot be suddenly prepared."

10. **印心** (Contemplation Practice): The teachings are for practice, not merely theoretical understanding.
    > 附文成觀文不虛設。觀與文合名為印心。
    > "Following the text to accomplish contemplation—the text is not established in vain. When contemplation and text unite, it is called 'sealing the mind.'"

### II. The Five Repentances (五悔)

Miao-lo presents the five repentances as essential preparation for entering higher stages:

> 先知下明五悔意為入觀之方。是故重牒前文觀法。但心理下明用向觀法不能發真。是故應須更加五悔。

"From 'first knowing' onward, we clarify that the five repentances serve as the method for entering contemplation. Therefore we repeat the previous contemplative method. From 'only mind-principle' onward, we clarify that using the previous contemplative method cannot yet manifest the true [realization]. Therefore one must additionally employ the five repentances."

#### 1. Confession (懺悔)

> 懺名下次正明五悔。雖有勸請等四不同。莫非悔罪。故名五悔。

"Though there are [the other] four [practices] such as entreaty, etc., none are other than repenting wrongdoing. Therefore they are called 'five repentances.'"

Definition:
> 陳露者。陳列也首也。悔者伏也。

"陳露 means 'to display and reveal.' 悔 means 'to submit' [i.e., acknowledge and reform]."

The practice involves:
- Recalling wrongdoing from this and previous lives
- Not justifying or minimizing
- Acknowledging the harm caused
- Resolving not to repeat

#### 2. Entreaty (勸請)

Two aspects:
> 一者請住於世。二者請轉法輪。

"First, requesting [Buddhas] to remain in the world. Second, requesting [them] to turn the Dharma-wheel."

Miao-lo quotes the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*:

> 佛雖必說而不待請。請者得福何得不請。猶如大王雖多美膳若有請者必得恩福。

"Though the Buddha will certainly teach without waiting to be asked, those who request obtain merit—how could one not request? Just as a great king, though having abundant fine food, certainly bestows grace and blessing on those who request."

#### 3. Rejoicing (隨喜)

> 佛轉法輪眾生得三世益。我助彼喜者。喜前勸請也。

"The Buddha turns the Dharma-wheel; sentient beings obtain benefit in the three times. 'I assist that joy'—this rejoices in the previous entreaty."

From the *Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*:

> 隨喜者有二種。一者世間。二者出世。若人無有出世善根。我今隨喜眾生世福。

"Rejoicing is of two kinds: worldly and world-transcending. If a person lacks world-transcending wholesome roots, I now rejoice in sentient beings' worldly merit."

The *Lotus Sūtra* connection:
> 法華隨喜法等者。隨喜品中從法座起。至於餘處為他人說。

"The *Lotus* [chapter on] 'Rejoicing in the Dharma': In the Rejoicing chapter, [practitioners] rise from the Dharma-seat, go to other places, and teach others."

#### 4. Transfer of Merit (迴向)

> 以隨喜福與眾生共及向菩提。

"Through the merit of rejoicing, [one shares] with sentient beings together and [directs merit] toward bodhi."

Two directions of transfer:
1. **To all beings**: May all beings benefit
2. **To awakening**: May this merit lead to supreme enlightenment

The seeming paradox:
> 施眾生者。然修因福不可得共。若得果時則以此福施於眾生。

"Giving to sentient beings: the merit of cultivating causes cannot actually be shared. But when obtaining the fruit, one gives this merit to sentient beings."

#### 5. Vow (發願)

> 迴向為大利等。故法華梵天施宮殿時。皆發願云。願以此功德等

"Transfer [of merit] is great benefit, etc. Therefore in the *Lotus [Sūtra]*, when Brahmā offers palaces, all make vows saying, 'May with this merit...' etc."

The four great vows provide direction for practice.

---

## Mode 9: Patient Endurance (安忍)

### Definition and Context

Mode 9 addresses the practitioner's capacity to maintain stability when facing obstacles. The term 安忍 combines:

- **安** (ān): settled, peaceful, stable
- **忍** (rěn): endurance, patience, forbearance

### The Function of Endurance

Having established the five repentances as preparatory practice, the practitioner must now develop the capacity to endure:

- **Internal obstacles**: doubt, fear, impatience, boredom
- **External obstacles**: criticism, hardship, temptation

Miao-lo emphasizes that endurance is not passive resignation but active stability—maintaining the contemplative orientation regardless of circumstances.

---

## Mode 10: Freedom from Dharma-Attachment (無法愛)

### The Final Obstacle

> 即使別教詮於不空。非初心觀何須比決。故並不與圓教空同。

"Even if the Distinct Teaching expresses 'not-empty,' it is not the initial mind's contemplation—why would it need comparative determination? Therefore [such teachings] are entirely not the same as the Perfect Teaching's emptiness."

Having traversed the previous nine modes, the practitioner faces the subtlest obstruction: attachment to the Dharma itself.

### The Logic of Release

The raft metaphor applies: the Dharma is an expedient for crossing to the other shore. Upon arrival, continued attachment to the raft impedes progress on solid ground.

This mode does not advocate:
- Rejection of the Dharma
- Antinomian disregard for practice
- Premature claims of transcendence

Rather, it advocates:
- Freedom from fixation
- Non-grasping relationship with methods
- Continued use without attachment

### Integration with Previous Modes

Mode 10 completes the Ten Modes by preventing the entire system from becoming a new form of bondage. Each mode—from the Hologram (一念三千) through Patient Endurance (安忍)—remains valid and beneficial. The practitioner simply ceases to grasp these methods as objects of craving.

---

## The Complete Ten Modes: Summary

| Mode | Chinese | Function |
|------|---------|----------|
| 1 | 觀不思議境 | Contemplating the inconceivable object |
| 2 | 發真正菩提心 | Arousing the true bodhi-mind |
| 3 | 善巧安心 | Skillfully settling the mind |
| 4 | 破法遍 | Breaking through all dharmas |
| 5 | 通塞 | Discerning openness and obstruction |
| 6 | 道品調適 | Adjusting the path factors |
| 7 | 對治助開 | Counteractive aids for opening |
| 8 | 知次位 | Knowing the sequential positions |
| 9 | 安忍 | Patient endurance |
| 10 | 無法愛 | Freedom from Dharma-attachment |

### Structural Analysis

- **Modes 1-4**: Establish the foundational contemplation
- **Modes 5-7**: Provide diagnostic and therapeutic apparatus
- **Mode 8**: Ensures accurate self-assessment
- **Mode 9**: Develops capacity to endure difficulties
- **Mode 10**: Prevents attachment to the system itself

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | Definition |
|---------|--------|------------|
| 十意融通 | shí yì róng tōng | Ten methods for harmonizing [teachings] |
| 五悔 | wǔ huǐ | Five repentances |
| 懺悔 | chàn huǐ | Confession |
| 勸請 | quàn qǐng | Entreaty |
| 隨喜 | suí xǐ | Rejoicing |
| 迴向 | huí xiàng | Transfer of merit |
| 發願 | fā yuàn | Making vows |
| 安忍 | ān rěn | Patient endurance |
| 無法愛 | wú fǎ ài | Freedom from Dharma-attachment |
| 印心 | yìn xīn | Sealing the mind |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Fascicle 7 completes the exposition of the Ten Modes by presenting Modes 5-10. These latter modes address what happens when foundational practice encounters obstacles, how to assess progress accurately, and how to avoid turning the path itself into a new form of bondage.

The ten methods for harmonizing teachings provide hermeneutical tools for resolving apparent contradictions among scriptures—a persistent challenge in Buddhist scholasticism. Miao-lo's approach allows practitioners to benefit from diverse textual traditions without becoming confused by surface-level disagreements.

The five repentances establish a practical entry point for serious practice. They are not merely devotional exercises but functional tools for clearing karmic obstacles and establishing the proper orientation for contemplation.

Modes 9 and 10 address the final challenges: maintaining stability through difficulty (安忍) and releasing attachment to the very methods that brought one to realization (無法愛). Together, they ensure that the practitioner neither abandons the path prematurely nor clings to it beyond its usefulness.



---



<!-- FASCICLE 07 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 7: Closing Summary — The Latter Six Modes
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 7 (止觀輔行傳弘決卷第七)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Overview

Fascicle 7 of Miao-lo's *Zhiguan Fuxing Zhuan Hongjue* completes the exposition of the Ten Modes of Contemplation (十乘觀法) by presenting Modes 5-10. While the earlier fascicles established the foundational contemplative methodology through Modes 1-4, this fascicle provides the diagnostic, therapeutic, positional, endurance, and liberative dimensions necessary for the completion of practice.

---

## The Six Modes Treated in This Fascicle

### Mode 5: Discerning Openness and Obstruction (通塞)

**Function**: Diagnostic assessment of whether the contemplative path is clear or blocked.

**Key Teaching**: Even correct practice can generate new obstructions through attachment to the practice itself. The four-sentence examination (四句檢校) provides a comprehensive diagnostic:

1. Openness within obstruction (塞中得通)
2. Obstruction within openness (通中有塞)
3. Obstruction as simply obstruction (塞自是塞)
4. Openness as simply openness (通自是通)

**Distinctive Tiantai Contribution**: The one-mind (一心) approach integrates horizontal, vertical, and distinct dimensions of openness-obstruction in a single moment of contemplative awareness.

---

### Mode 6: Adjusting the Path Factors (道品調適)

**Function**: Appropriate application of the thirty-seven factors of awakening (三十七道品).

**Key Teaching**: The path factors form an organic structure—from roots (four foundations of mindfulness) through trunk (four right efforts) to fruit (noble eightfold path). The practitioner adjusts which factors to emphasize based on current conditions.

**The Three Gates of Liberation**: Emptiness (空門), signlessness (無相門), and wishlessness (無作門) represent the culmination of path-factor practice. In the Perfect Teaching, these three are simultaneous aspects of a single liberative insight.

---

### Mode 7: Counteractive Aids for Opening (對治助開)

**Function**: Targeted treatment for specific obstacles when standard practice proves insufficient.

**Key Teaching**: Six obstructions (六蔽) require six perfections (六度) as antidotes:

| Obstruction | Perfection |
|-------------|------------|
| Stinginess (慳) | Generosity (布施) |
| Immorality (破戒) | Moral Discipline (持戒) |
| Anger (瞋) | Patience (忍辱) |
| Laziness (懈怠) | Effort (精進) |
| Distraction (散亂) | Concentration (禪定) |
| Ignorance (愚癡) | Wisdom (般若) |

**The Four Followings (四隨)**: When direct treatment fails, the practitioner may turn to alternative perfections, combine multiple perfections, or proceed directly to ultimate contemplation.

---

### Mode 8: Knowing the Sequential Positions (知次位)

**Function**: Accurate self-assessment of one's stage on the path.

**Key Teaching**: Claiming attainments not yet realized constitutes a grave offense. The four foundations of mindfulness (四念處) establish the discernment necessary for honest evaluation.

**The Three Animals**: Different levels of aspiration:
- Small deer (麞) — Śrāvaka: escapes alone
- Larger deer (鹿) — Pratyekabuddha: escapes with backward glance
- Great elephant (大象) — Bodhisattva: leads all beings to safety

**The Ten Methods for Harmonizing Teachings (十意融通)**: Hermeneutical principles for reconciling apparent contradictions among scriptures.

**The Five Repentances (五悔)**: Preparatory practices for entering higher stages:
1. Confession (懺悔)
2. Entreaty (勸請)
3. Rejoicing (隨喜)
4. Transfer of Merit (迴向)
5. Vow (發願)

---

### Mode 9: Patient Endurance (安忍)

**Function**: Maintaining stability when facing internal and external obstacles.

**Key Teaching**: 安忍 combines settled peace (安) with endurance (忍). The practitioner develops capacity to maintain contemplative orientation regardless of circumstances—neither fighting obstacles nor fleeing from them, but steadily continuing practice.

---

### Mode 10: Freedom from Dharma-Attachment (無法愛)

**Function**: Releasing attachment to the practice itself.

**Key Teaching**: Even love of the Dharma constitutes a form of grasping that must ultimately be released. The teaching remains valid and beneficial; the practitioner simply ceases to cling to it as an object of craving.

**The Raft Metaphor**: The Dharma is for crossing to the other shore. Upon arrival, continued attachment to the raft becomes an impediment.

---

## Structural Analysis of the Ten Modes

| Category | Modes | Function |
|----------|-------|----------|
| Foundation | 1-4 | Establish the core contemplation |
| Diagnostic | 5 | Assess openness and obstruction |
| Therapeutic | 6-7 | Adjust method and apply treatment |
| Positional | 8 | Know one's stage accurately |
| Endurance | 9 | Maintain stability through difficulty |
| Liberation | 10 | Release attachment to the path itself |

---

## Major Doctrinal Contributions

### 1. Integration of Abhidharma and Tiantai

Miao-lo draws extensively on Abhidharma sources (*Mahāvibhāṣā*, *Abhidharmakośa*) and Mādhyamika treatises (*Mahāprajñāpāramitā Śāstra*) while interpreting them through the Tiantai lens of the three truths and mutual inclusion.

### 2. The One-Mind Principle

Throughout the fascicle, Miao-lo emphasizes that horizontal and vertical dimensions of practice are integrated in the single moment of contemplative awareness. This prevents both sequential gradualism and mere abstraction.

### 3. The Therapeutic Framework

Modes 5-7 constitute a sophisticated therapeutic apparatus for addressing obstacles in practice. The four-sentence examination, the thirty-seven factors, and the six perfections provide comprehensive tools for diagnosis and treatment.

### 4. Hermeneutical Method

The ten methods for harmonizing teachings offer a principled approach to reconciling apparent contradictions among Buddhist scriptures—a persistent challenge in scholastic Buddhism.

### 5. Practical Psychology

The five repentances, the three animals metaphor, and the discussions of endurance and non-attachment provide practical psychological guidance for practitioners at various stages.

---

## Key Technical Vocabulary

| Chinese | Pinyin | English |
|---------|--------|---------|
| 十乘觀法 | shí chéng guān fǎ | Ten Modes of Contemplation |
| 通塞 | tōng sè | Openness and Obstruction |
| 四句檢校 | sì jù jiǎn jiào | Four-Sentence Examination |
| 一心通塞 | yī xīn tōng sè | One-Mind Openness-Obstruction |
| 道品調適 | dào pǐn tiáo shì | Adjusting the Path Factors |
| 三十七道品 | sān shí qī dào pǐn | Thirty-Seven Factors of Awakening |
| 三脫門 | sān tuō mén | Three Gates of Liberation |
| 對治助開 | duì zhì zhù kāi | Counteractive Aids for Opening |
| 六蔽 | liù bì | Six Obstructions |
| 六度 | liù dù | Six Perfections |
| 四隨 | sì suí | Four Followings |
| 知次位 | zhī cì wèi | Knowing the Sequential Positions |
| 四念處 | sì niàn chù | Four Foundations of Mindfulness |
| 十意融通 | shí yì róng tōng | Ten Methods for Harmonizing Teachings |
| 五悔 | wǔ huǐ | Five Repentances |
| 安忍 | ān rěn | Patient Endurance |
| 無法愛 | wú fǎ ài | Freedom from Dharma-Attachment |

---

## Relation to Other Fascicles

**Preceding Fascicles (5-6)**: Established Modes 1-4, the foundational contemplative methodology.

**Following Fascicle (8)**: Will address 歷緣對境 (encountering objects in daily life)—the application of contemplation to ordinary experience.

---

## Significance for Tiantai Practice

Fascicle 7 represents the maturation of contemplative methodology. Having established the foundation (Modes 1-4), the practitioner now possesses:

1. **Diagnostic capacity** (Mode 5) to recognize when practice is blocked
2. **Adjustment skills** (Mode 6) to apply appropriate path factors
3. **Therapeutic tools** (Mode 7) for targeted treatment of obstacles
4. **Positional awareness** (Mode 8) to accurately assess progress
5. **Endurance capacity** (Mode 9) to maintain stability through difficulty
6. **Liberative freedom** (Mode 10) from attachment to the path itself

These six modes ensure that practice does not stall at the foundational level but proceeds toward complete realization.

---

- T46n1911: 摩訶止觀 (Mohe Zhiguan)

- T27n1545: 阿毘達磨大毘婆沙論 (Mahāvibhāṣā)

<!-- FASCICLE 08 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 8: Opening — The Ten Objects of Contemplation
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 8 (止觀輔行傳弘決卷第八)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

**Five Flavors Assessment**: Ghee (法華涅槃) — Fully integrated; everything is the Path
**Six Stages Location**: Practice (修行即) — Active processing of source material

---

## Overview: From Method to Application

Fascicle 8 marks a critical transition in Miao-lo's commentary. Having completed the exposition of the Ten Modes of Contemplation (十乘觀法) in Fascicles 5-7, Miao-lo now addresses what happens when the practitioner **encounters objects** (歷緣對境) in the course of practice.

The Ten Modes provided the foundational methodology. But practice does not occur in a vacuum. The meditator encounters:

- **Internal disturbances**: Afflictions (煩惱), illness (病患), karmic manifestations (業)
- **External phenomena**: Demonic interference (魔事), meditative states (禪定), wrong views (邪見)
- **Advanced stages**: The two vehicles (二乘), bodhisattva practice (菩薩), and Buddhahood (佛)

These are the **Ten Objects** (十境) — phenomena that arise during contemplation and require skilled response.

---

## The Ten Objects (十境)

Miao-lo provides this structural overview:

| # | Object | Chinese | Function |
|---|--------|---------|----------|
| 1 | Aggregates | 陰入境 | Foundation: contemplate the five aggregates |
| 2 | Afflictions | 煩惱境 | Obstacle: recognize and transform defilements |
| 3 | Illness | 病患境 | Obstacle: work with physical disease |
| 4 | Karma | 業境 | Obstacle: address past actions manifesting |
| 5 | Demons | 魔事境 | Obstacle: discern and resist demonic interference |
| 6 | Meditative States | 禪定境 | Attainment: navigate dhyāna without attachment |
| 7 | Wrong Views | 邪見境 | Obstacle: correct mistaken understanding |
| 8 | Arrogance | 慢境 | Obstacle: overcome spiritual pride |
| 9 | Two Vehicles | 二乘境 | Attainment: transcend limited aspiration |
| 10 | Bodhisattva | 菩薩境 | Attainment: fulfill the bodhisattva path |

**Fascicle 8 covers Objects 2-4**: Afflictions, Illness, and Karma.

---

## The Relationship Between Objects and Modes

Miao-lo clarifies a crucial structural point:

> 自此以下現文六境及略三境。咸是發得。

"From this point onward, the text presents six objects in detail and three objects briefly. All are 'arising and obtaining' [phenomena]."

The Ten Objects are not separate from the Ten Modes. Rather:

- The **Ten Modes** are the contemplative methodology
- The **Ten Objects** are what arises during contemplation
- The practitioner applies the Ten Modes **to** each Object

> 此九境外所起煩惱。乃至四弘但屬陰境三陰所攝。

"Afflictions arising outside these nine objects, and even the four great vows, belong to the aggregate-object and are subsumed under the three aggregates."

This establishes that all phenomena ultimately reduce to the first object (aggregates), but the Buddha distinguished nine additional objects for pedagogical purposes.

---

## The Three Obstacles (三障)

Miao-lo connects the Ten Objects to the traditional classification of three obstacles:

> 如前七隻分別中。云此之十境是三障攝。

"As in the previous sevenfold distinction, these ten objects are subsumed under the three obstacles."

From the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*:

| Obstacle | Chinese | Contents |
|----------|---------|----------|
| Affliction Obstacle | 煩惱障 | Three poisons, five coverings, eight kinds of arrogance, four perversities |
| Karma Obstacle | 業障 | Five heinous acts |
| Retribution Obstacle | 報障 | Three evil paths, slandering the Dharma, icchantika |

> 故知舉惡能障。故名為障。

"Therefore we know that citing evils that can obstruct is why they are called 'obstacles.'"

---

## The Transition: From Foundation to Application

### What Came Before (Fascicles 5-7)

The Ten Modes established:
1. **Mode 1**: Contemplating the inconceivable object (一念三千)
2. **Mode 2**: Arousing the bodhi-mind
3. **Mode 3**: Skillfully settling the mind
4. **Mode 4**: Breaking through all dharmas
5. **Mode 5**: Discerning openness and obstruction
6. **Mode 6**: Adjusting the path factors
7. **Mode 7**: Counteractive aids for opening
8. **Mode 8**: Knowing the sequential positions
9. **Mode 9**: Patient endurance
10. **Mode 10**: Freedom from Dharma-attachment

### What Comes Now (Fascicle 8)

The practitioner, equipped with these ten tools, now encounters specific phenomena that require their application:

1. **Afflictions** (煩惱境): When the three poisons intensify during practice
2. **Illness** (病患境): When physical disease arises
3. **Karma** (業境): When past actions manifest as visions or obstacles

Each of these objects receives:
- Analysis of causes and conditions
- Classification of types
- Treatment methods (both gradual and sudden)
- Application of the ten contemplative modes

---

## Scope of Fascicle 8

### Part 1 (之一): The Affliction Object (煩惱境)
- Purpose and context
- Relationship to previous practices (訶欲棄蓋, 陰入)
- The three causes: habitual karma, intentional karma, demonic activity
- Treatment methods: gradual and sudden

### Part 2 (之二): The Affliction Object (continued) and Illness Object (病患境)
- Ten contemplative modes applied to afflictions
- The 36-sentence framework
- Introduction to illness as object
- Causes of illness: four elements, diet, meditation imbalance, spirits, karma

### Part 3 (之三): Illness Object (continued) and Karma Object (業境)
- Six treatment methods for illness
- Ten contemplative modes applied to illness
- Introduction to karma as object
- Manifestation of karmic signs during meditation
- Ten contemplative modes applied to karma

---

## Key Technical Terms for Fascicle 8

| Chinese | Pinyin | English |
|---------|--------|---------|
| 十境 | shí jìng | Ten Objects |
| 歷緣對境 | lì yuán duì jìng | Encountering objects through conditions |
| 煩惱境 | fán nǎo jìng | Affliction Object |
| 病患境 | bìng huàn jìng | Illness Object |
| 業境 | yè jìng | Karma Object |
| 三障 | sān zhàng | Three Obstacles |
| 習因 | xí yīn | Habitual cause |
| 報果 | bào guǒ | Retributive effect |
| 四大 | sì dà | Four great elements |
| 六氣 | liù qì | Six healing breaths |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Fascicle 8 represents the integration of contemplative theory with practical application. The Ten Modes are not merely conceptual frameworks but living tools to be applied when the practitioner encounters obstacles.

Miao-lo's approach is distinctive in several ways:

1. **Comprehensive Classification**: Every phenomenon that can arise during practice is categorized and addressed.

2. **Integration of Medical Knowledge**: The illness section incorporates traditional Chinese medicine (pulse diagnosis, five elements, organ theory) within a Buddhist contemplative framework.

3. **Karmic Psychology**: The karma section addresses how past actions manifest as visions and obstacles during meditation, providing both diagnostic and therapeutic guidance.

4. **Gradual and Sudden Methods**: Each object receives both gradual (思議) and sudden (不思議) treatment approaches, accommodating practitioners of different capacities.

---

## Reading Strategy

Given the density of Fascicle 8, the scholarly translation is organized as follows:

- **Section 1**: Affliction Object (煩惱境) — causes, types, treatment
- **Section 2**: Illness Object (病患境) — causes, types, treatment
- **Section 3**: Karma Object (業境) — causes, manifestations, treatment
- **Closing Summary**: Integration and cross-references

Each section includes:
- Direct translation of Miao-lo's commentary
- Embedded Chinese for key terms
- Footnotes for doctrinal context
- Technical vocabulary tables

---

## Source Text Reference

**CBETA**: T46n1912, Fascicle 8

| Part | Chinese Title | Content |
|------|---------------|---------|
| 之一 | 卷第八之一 | Affliction Object: causes, three conditions, treatment |
| 之二 | 卷第八之二 | Affliction Object (continued), Illness Object |
| 之三 | 卷第八之三 | Illness treatment, Karma Object |

---

**END OF OPENING**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 08 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 8: Section 1 — The Affliction Object (煩惱境)
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 8, Part 1 (卷第八之一)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

**Five Flavors Assessment**: Ghee (法華涅槃) — Fully integrated; afflictions as Buddha-nature
**Six Stages Location**: Practice (修行即) — Active processing of source material

---

## Object 2: The Affliction Object (煩惱境)

### I. Introduction: From Method to Encounter

Having established the Ten Modes of contemplation (十乘觀法) in Fascicles 5-7, Miao-lo now addresses what happens when the practitioner **encounters phenomena** (歷緣對境) during practice. The Affliction Object is the second of the Ten Objects, arising immediately after the practitioner begins contemplating the aggregates (陰入境).

> 自此以下現文六境及略三境。咸是發得。

"From this point onward, the text presents six objects in detail and three objects briefly. All are 'arising and obtaining' [phenomena]."

This indicates that Objects 2-7 receive full treatment while Objects 8-10 receive condensed treatment. The Affliction Object initiates this sequence.

### II. The Nature of Affliction Arising

Miao-lo identifies three causes for affliction arising during contemplation:

#### A. Habitual Karma (習因)

> 若見思未窮但是習因

"If view-delusions and thought-delusions have not been exhausted, this is simply habitual cause."

The practitioner who has not yet severed the roots of defilement will naturally experience afflictions arising from accumulated habitual patterns. This is not pathological but expected.

#### B. Intentional Karma (作意)

> 若起現行故生煩惱名作意業

"If one generates afflictions through present activity, this is called intentional karma."

This refers to afflictions generated in the present moment through incorrect mental operation—what the Indian tradition calls "unwise attention" (ayoniśomanaskāra, 非如理作意).

#### C. Demonic Activity (魔事)

> 或魔所為令發煩惱

"Or demons may cause afflictions to arise."

Miao-lo includes the possibility that external forces (魔, māra) may intensify afflictions beyond what the practitioner's own karma would produce. This receives full treatment in Object 5 (魔事境).

### III. The Three Poisons and Their Derivatives

Miao-lo organizes afflictions according to the traditional classification:

#### The Root Three (三毒)

| Affliction | Chinese | Function |
|------------|---------|----------|
| Greed | 貪 | Attachment to agreeable objects |
| Hatred | 瞋 | Aversion to disagreeable objects |
| Delusion | 癡 | Confusion regarding neutral objects |

> 遇順境故生貪。遇違境故生瞋。遇非違非順境故生癡。

"Encountering agreeable conditions, greed arises. Encountering disagreeable conditions, hatred arises. Encountering conditions neither disagreeable nor agreeable, delusion arises."

This aligns with Śāntideva's analysis in the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*: when the mind moves toward an agreeable object, passion arises; toward a disagreeable object, hatred arises; toward a neutral object, delusion arises.

#### The Five Coverings (五蓋)

| Covering | Chinese | Characteristic |
|----------|---------|----------------|
| Desire | 貪欲蓋 | Craving for sensory pleasure |
| Ill-will | 瞋恚蓋 | Hostility and resentment |
| Sloth-torpor | 睡眠蓋 | Mental dullness and drowsiness |
| Restlessness-remorse | 掉悔蓋 | Agitation and regret |
| Doubt | 疑蓋 | Hesitation regarding the path |

### IV. The Relationship to Prior Practices

Miao-lo addresses why afflictions arise even after the practitioner has completed the preliminary practices of "rejecting desires and abandoning coverings" (訶欲棄蓋):

> 如前訶欲棄蓋。與此何異。

"How does this differ from the previous [teaching on] rejecting desires and abandoning coverings?"

His answer establishes a critical distinction:

> 前是助道方便。今是發得正觀。前是未發。今是已發。

"The previous was a preparatory aid to the path. Now is the arising and obtaining of correct contemplation. The previous was before arising; now is after arising."

The preliminary practices (訶欲棄蓋) prepare the mind for contemplation but do not address what happens **during** contemplation when afflictions intensify due to practice itself. The Affliction Object addresses this second, more subtle level.

### V. The 36-Sentence Framework

Miao-lo introduces the comprehensive framework for analyzing afflictions:

> 六根對六塵生六識。識之所對名事煩惱。三六十八。由觀成二。故成三十六。

"The six sense faculties confronting six sense objects generate six consciousnesses. What consciousness confronts is called 'affair-affliction.' Three times six is eighteen. Because contemplation creates two [categories], it becomes thirty-six."

This produces the 36-sentence structure:

| Category | Sense Organs | Objects | Consciousnesses | Total |
|----------|--------------|---------|-----------------|-------|
| Contact | 6 | 6 | 6 | 18 |
| × Observing/Observed | — | — | — | ×2 |
| **Total** | — | — | — | **36** |

Each of the 18 contact-points is examined from two perspectives:
1. **As observer** (能觀): How the practitioner's contemplation relates to the affliction
2. **As observed** (所觀): How the affliction appears as object of contemplation

### VI. Treatment Methods: Gradual and Sudden

#### A. Gradual Treatment (思議)

The gradual approach applies specific antidotes (pratipakṣa, 對治) to specific afflictions:

| Affliction | Antidote | Chinese |
|------------|----------|---------|
| Greed | Impurity contemplation | 不淨觀 |
| Hatred | Loving-kindness contemplation | 慈悲觀 |
| Delusion | Dependent origination contemplation | 因緣觀 |
| Distraction | Breath-counting | 數息觀 |
| Arrogance | Element-contemplation | 界分別觀 |

This corresponds to the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*'s prescription: impurity (aśubha) for passion, loving-kindness (maitrī) for hatred, dependent origination (pratītyasamutpāda) for delusion.

#### B. Sudden Treatment (不思議)

The sudden approach does not apply antidotes but directly perceives the empty nature of afflictions:

> 煩惱即菩提。不須更斷。

"Afflictions are precisely bodhi. There is no need for further severance."

This accords with the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*'s ultimate teaching: "By the very understanding of these afflictions, there is bodhi. Whatever is the nature of afflictions, that is the nature of bodhi."

Miao-lo clarifies that this is not license for indulgence:

> 非謂縱任。乃是了達煩惱性空。

"This does not mean indulgence. It means thoroughly penetrating that the nature of afflictions is empty."

### VII. Applying the Ten Modes to Afflictions

Miao-lo demonstrates how each of the Ten Modes applies to the Affliction Object:

#### Mode 1: Contemplating the Inconceivable Object (觀不思議境)

> 觀煩惱即是法界。一念三千。無非佛法。

"Contemplate that afflictions are precisely the dharma-realm. In one thought, three thousand [realms]. Nothing is not Buddha-dharma."

The affliction is not observed as something to be eliminated but as the very manifestation of the three thousand realms in a single moment of thought. Greed, when contemplated correctly, reveals the entire structure of reality.

#### Mode 2: Arousing the Bodhi-Mind (發菩提心)

> 因煩惱發大誓願。眾生無邊煩惱無盡。

"Because of afflictions, generate the great vow. Sentient beings are limitless; afflictions are inexhaustible."

The Four Great Vows are aroused precisely because afflictions exist. Without afflictions, there would be no need for the bodhisattva path.

#### Mode 3: Skillfully Settling the Mind (善巧安心)

> 安心於煩惱。不動不轉。

"Settle the mind upon afflictions. Neither moving nor turning."

Rather than fleeing from afflictions, the practitioner settles the mind directly upon them, using them as the very object of stabilization.

#### Mode 4: Breaking Through All Dharmas (破法遍)

> 破煩惱相。見煩惱空。

"Break through the characteristics of afflictions. See that afflictions are empty."

This applies the breaking-through contemplation specifically to afflictions, dismantling their appearance of solidity.

#### Mode 5: Discerning Openness and Obstruction (識通塞)

> 煩惱即塞。了煩惱空即通。通塞不二。

"Afflictions are precisely obstruction. Understanding afflictions as empty is precisely openness. Openness and obstruction are non-dual."

The diagnostic function of Mode 5 determines whether the practitioner is blocked by or liberated through afflictions.

#### Modes 6-10: Complete Application

The remaining modes (調道品, 助道對治, 識次位, 能安忍, 無法愛) are applied similarly, with afflictions serving as the specific object to which each mode addresses itself.

### VIII. The Four Perversities (四顛倒)

Miao-lo connects afflictions to the fundamental perversities:

| Perversity | Takes | For | Chinese |
|------------|-------|-----|---------|
| 1 | Impermanent | Permanent | 常顛倒 |
| 2 | Suffering | Happiness | 樂顛倒 |
| 3 | Non-self | Self | 我顛倒 |
| 4 | Impure | Pure | 淨顛倒 |

> 此四顛倒是煩惱之根本。

"These four perversities are the root of afflictions."

All afflictions ultimately derive from these four fundamental misperceptions. The treatment of afflictions therefore requires addressing these root causes.

### IX. The Eight Kinds of Arrogance (八慢)

Miao-lo lists the eight forms of pride that constitute subtle afflictions:

1. **慢** (māna) — Basic pride: thinking oneself superior
2. **過慢** (atimāna) — Excessive pride: thinking oneself equal to superiors
3. **慢過慢** (mānātimāna) — Pride upon pride: thinking oneself superior to superiors
4. **我慢** (asmimāna) — Self-pride: attachment to the five aggregates as "I"
5. **增上慢** (adhimāna) — Presumptuous pride: claiming attainments not realized
6. **卑慢** (ūnamāna) — Pride of inferiority: thinking "I am only slightly inferior"
7. **邪慢** (mithyāmāna) — Wrong pride: pride in wrong accomplishments
8. **傲慢** — Arrogance: general condescension toward others

### X. Doctrinal Significance

The Affliction Object (煩惱境) establishes a foundational principle for Tiantai practice: afflictions are not merely obstacles to be eliminated but **objects of contemplation** that reveal the nature of reality when correctly approached.

This differs from gradual paths that treat afflictions solely as enemies:

> 若但斷煩惱。未見煩惱性。雖似離煩惱。還復起煩惱。

"If one merely severs afflictions without seeing the nature of afflictions, although one seems to leave afflictions behind, they will arise again."

The Tiantai approach—grounded in the Perfect Teaching (圓教)—perceives afflictions as the very material through which enlightenment manifests. This does not excuse indulgence but requires the deepest penetration of affliction's empty nature.

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | Definition |
|---------|--------|------------|
| 煩惱境 | fán nǎo jìng | Affliction Object |
| 習因 | xí yīn | Habitual cause |
| 作意業 | zuò yì yè | Intentional karma |
| 三毒 | sān dú | Three poisons (greed, hatred, delusion) |
| 五蓋 | wǔ gài | Five coverings |
| 訶欲棄蓋 | hē yù qì gài | Rejecting desires, abandoning coverings |
| 對治 | duì zhì | Antidote (pratipakṣa) |
| 不淨觀 | bù jìng guān | Impurity contemplation |
| 慈悲觀 | cí bēi guān | Loving-kindness contemplation |
| 因緣觀 | yīn yuán guān | Dependent origination contemplation |
| 四顛倒 | sì diān dǎo | Four perversities |
| 八慢 | bā màn | Eight kinds of arrogance |
| 煩惱即菩提 | fán nǎo jí pú tí | Afflictions are precisely bodhi |

---

## Cross-References

- **Indian Sources**: The antidote framework aligns with *Śikṣāsamuccaya* Ch. 9 (Kṣāntipāramitā) and Ch. 13-14 (Smṛtyupasthāna, Ātmabhāvapariśuddhi)
- **Tiantai Context**: This section presupposes the Ten Modes from Fascicles 5-7, especially Mode 4 (Breaking Through) and Mode 5 (Openness/Obstruction)
- **Lotus Sūtra Connection**: The "afflictions as bodhi" teaching derives from the One Vehicle doctrine wherein nothing is excluded from the path

---

**END OF SECTION 1**
