<!-- FASCICLE 09 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 9: Opening — The Meditative States Object
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 9 (止觀輔行傳弘決卷第九)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

**Five Flavors Assessment**: Ghee (法華涅槃) — Integration of all meditative states into the One Vehicle
**Six Stages Location**: Practice (修行即) — Deepening the cultivation through specific states

---

## Overview: The Realm of Dhyāna (禪境)

Fascicle 9 is entirely dedicated to the **Sixth Object**: **Meditative States** (禪定境, *chán dìng jìng*).

Having addressed the obstacles of Afflictions, Illness, and Karma in the previous fascicle (and presumably Mara, though Mara often overlaps with these), Miao-lo now turns to the **attainments** that arise during practice. These states are "objects" (境) because they can become:
1.  **Obstacles**: If the practitioner clings to them or mistakes them for liberation.
2.  **Vehicles**: If the practitioner contemplates them with the Ten Modes.

This fascicle provides a comprehensive encyclopedic treatment of Buddhist meditation states, ranging from ordinary worldly dhyānas to the most advanced Mahāyāna samādhis.

---

## The Necessity of Contemplating Dhyāna

Miao-lo explains why this object must be contemplated:

> 若入流竟者。初住已去無功用故。若此位兼發一切事禪。任運即理不須更觀。今令觀者在於假名五品位中。

"If one has 'entered the stream' (entered the First Abode), there is no [deliberate] effort. If in this position one concurrently develops all phenomenal dhyānas, they naturally accord with principle and need not be further contemplated. The instruction to contemplate now lies within the **proficience-in-names** (five grades) position."

Practitioners in the early stages (Five Grades) may develop deep meditative states due to past karma or present practice. Without correct contemplation, these states can lead to:
- **Pride** (慢)
- **Wrong Views** (邪見)
- **Rebirth in the Form/Formless Realms** (preventing liberation)
- **Demonic Interference** (魔事)

Therefore, Dhyāna must be treated as an **object of contemplation**, not just a resting place.

---

## The Classification of Dhyānas

Miao-lo follows the classification system of the *Ci Di Chan Men* (Sequential Dhyāna Gates), dividing all meditative states into four categories:

1.  **Worldly Dhyānas** (世間禪):
    - The Four Dhyānas (四禪)
    - The Four Undefiled/Immeasurable Minds (四無量心)
    - The Four Formless Absorptions (四無色定)
    - *Characteristic*: Leaking (有漏), bound to samsara.

2.  **Also-Worldly-Also-Transcendental Dhyānas** (亦世間亦出世間禪):
    - The Six Wondrous Gates (六妙門)
    - The Sixteen Special Excellences (十六特勝)
    - The Transparent Light Dhyāna (通明禪)
    - *Characteristic*: Can be used for both worldly attainment and liberation.

3.  **Transcendental Dhyānas** (出世間禪):
    - The Nine Thoughts (九想)
    - The Eight Recollections (八念)
    - The Ten Thoughts (十想)
    - The Eight Back-Sheddings (八背捨) - Vimokṣa
    - The Eight Victory Places (八勝處)
    - The Ten All-Places (十一切處)
    - The Nine Sequential Dhyānas (九次第定)
    - The Lion Sprint Samādhi (師子奮迅三昧)
    - The Transcendence Samādhi (超越三昧)
    - *Characteristic*: Non-leaking (無漏), leading to Arhatship or Pratyekabuddhahood.

4.  **Neither-Worldly-Nor-Transcendental Dhyānas** (非世間非出世間禪):
    - The Great Vehicle Dhyānas (e.g., The Self-Nature Zen, etc.) - though often these are realized *through* the previous forms when viewed with the Perfect Mind.
    - Included here:
        - Compassion Samādhi (慈心定) - in its Mahāyāna form
        - Causes and Conditions Samādhi (因緣定)
        - Buddha-Recollection Samādhi (念佛三昧)
        - Superknowledges (神通) - as function

---

## Structure of Fascicle 9

### Part 1 (卷第九之一): Worldly and Transitional Zens
- **Causes and Conditions**: Why dhyāna arises (past seeds + present practice + external protection).
- **The Worldly Zens**: Detailed analysis of the Four Dhyānas (triggering, signs, benefits).
- **The Also-Worldly-Also-Transcendental Zens**: The Sixteen Special Excellences and Transparent Light.

### Part 2 (卷第九之二): Transcendental Zens
- **The Impurity Meditations**: Nine Thoughts, etc.
- **The Eight Back-Sheddings (Vimokṣa)**: The core of the Transcendental system.
- **Interconnection**: How these states trigger one another.

### Part 3 (卷第九之三): Great Vehicle Applications
- **Compassion and Causes/Conditions**: Deepening the practice.
- **Buddha-Recollection**: Using the image/name as a gateway.
- **Superknowledges**: The fruits of power.
- **Applying the Ten Modes**: The specific method for contemplating dhyāna as the Inconceivable Object.

---

## Key Technical Terms

| Chinese | Pinyin | English |
|---------|--------|---------|
| 禪定境 | chán dìng jìng | Meditative States Object |
| 根本禪 | gēn běn chán | Root/Fundamental Dhyāna (Worldly) |
| 十六特勝 | shí liù tè shèng | Sixteen Special Excellences |
| 通明禪 | tōng míng chán | Transparent Light Dhyāna |
| 背捨 | bèi shě | Back-Shedding (Vimokṣa/Liberation) |
| 九次第定 | jiǔ cì dì dìng | Nine Sequential Dhyānas |
| 師子奮迅 | shī zi fèn xùn | Lion Sprint Samādhi |
| 超越三昧 | chāo yuè sān mèi | Transcendence Samādhi |
| 歷緣對境 | lì yuán duì jìng | Encountering objects through conditions |

---

## Doctrinal Significance

Fascicle 9 demonstrates the **encyclopedic scope** of the Tiantai system. Zhiyi and Miao-lo do not reject any form of meditation found in the sutras. Instead, they:
1.  **Classify** it (Worldly, Transcendent, etc.).
2.  **Diagnose** it (Is it a trap? A tool?).
3.  **Integrate** it (How does it fit into the Perfect Teaching?).

By applying the **Ten Modes** (especially Mode 1: Inconceivable Object) to these states, even the most mundane "Root Dhyāna" can be transformed into a gateway to the **Three Thousand Realms in a Single Thought**.

---

**[End of Opening]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 09 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 9: Section 1 — The Stock Gear (Worldly & Transitional Zens)
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 9, Part 1 (卷第九之一)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## I. Causes and Conditions for the Arising of Dhyāna

Before detailing the states, Miao-lo explains **why** they arise.

### 1. The Internal Cause (內因)
All sentient beings possess the potential for these states.
> "Just as all beings have the potential for Buddhahood, they also possess the seeds of the fundamental dhyānas."

These seeds are "moistened" by past practice. Even if one did not master them in a past life, the imprint remains.

### 2. The External Condition (外緣)
The triggering factors:
- **Teaching**: Hearing the method.
- **Protection**: The aid of Buddhas and spirits.
- **Practice**: The immediate application of methods (like the 25 Preparations).

---

## II. The Root Dhyānas (根本禪) — Worldly Zen

These are called "Root" (根本) because they are the foundation for all other meditations, and "Flavor" (味) because beings become attached to the taste of bliss within them.

### The Four Dhyānas (四禪)

**1. First Dhyāna (初禪)**
- **Trigger**: Through the "Eight Touches" (八觸) - movement, itch, lightness, heaviness, cold, heat, roughness, smoothness.
- **Characteristics**: Separation from desire (離欲). Five branches:
  1.  **Vitarka** (覺 - Investigation/Coarse thought)
  2.  **Vicara** (觀 - Analysis/Fine thought)
  3.  **Piti** (喜 - Joy)
  4.  **Sukha** (樂 - Bliss)
  5.  **Ekaggata** (一心 - One-pointedness)
- **The Danger**: Attachment to the Eight Touches. Mistaking the sensation for realization.

**2. Second Dhyāna (二禪)**
- **Trigger**: Cessation of coarse and fine thought.
- **Characteristics**: Internal purity (內淨).
  1.  **Internal Purity** (內淨)
  2.  **Joy** (喜)
  3.  **Bliss** (樂)
  4.  **One-pointedness** (一心)
- **The Shift**: The mind is like a still pool. No waves of discursive thought.

**3. Third Dhyāna (三禪)**
- **Characteristics**:
  1.  **Equanimity** (捨)
  2.  **Mindfulness** (念)
  3.  **Wisdom** (慧)
  4.  **Bliss** (樂)
  5.  **One-pointedness** (一心)
- **The Note**: This contains the highest worldly bliss. The danger of attachment is highest here.

**4. Fourth Dhyāna (四禪)**
- **Characteristics**:
  1.  **Neither-pain-nor-pleasure** (不苦不樂)
  2.  **Equanimity** (捨)
  3.  **Mindfulness-purity** (念清淨)
  4.  **One-pointedness** (一心)
- **The State**: Breath ceases. The mind is immobile like a lamp in a windless room.

---

### The Four Formless Absorptions (四無色定)

When the practitioner turns away from matter (form) completely:

1.  **Infinite Space** (空無邊處): Removing the limits of matter.
2.  **Infinite Consciousness** (識無邊處): Focusing on the mind that perceives space.
3.  **Nothingness** (無所有處): Dropping even consciousness. "There is nothing."
4.  **Neither-Perception-Nor-Non-Perception** (非想非非想處): The limit of worldly existence. The mind is so subtle it can hardly be said to exist, yet it is not non-existent.

**Miao-lo's Critique**:
> "These are the peak of samsara. But they are still samsara. Like an arrow shot into the sky, eventually it must fall."

---

## III. The Also-Worldly-Also-Transcendental Zens (亦有漏亦無漏禪)

These practices bridge the gap. They use worldly methods but can lead to transcendental results if directed by wisdom.

### 1. The Six Wondrous Gates (六妙門)
1.  **Counting** (數): Regulating the breath.
2.  **Following** (隨): Unification with the breath.
3.  **Stopping** (止): Quiescence.
4.  **Contemplating** (觀): Insight.
5.  **Returning** (還): Turning back to the source of mind.
6.  **Purifying** (淨): No marks.

### 2. The Sixteen Special Excellences (十六特勝)
An expansion of breath mindfulness (Anapanasati). Unlike the Root Dhyānas, these maintain **insight/wisdom** throughout the levels of dhyāna. They prevent "flavor" (attachment).

- Includes: Knowing breath short/long, experiencing joy/bliss, calming mental formations, gladdening the mind, concentrating the mind, liberating the mind, contemplating impermanence, fading away, cessation, relinquishment.

### 3. The Transparent Light Dhyāna (通明禪)
A practice that produces "Transparent Light" (通明) - realizing the non-substantiality of the breath and the mind. It illuminates the internal organs and the nature of the body without dissection. It is distinct from the Nine Thoughts (which focus on impurity).

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Key Terms
- **八觸** (*bā chù*): Eight Touches (physical sensations of dhyāna arising).
- **支林** (*zhī lín*): Branch-forest (the specific factors/characteristics of each dhyāna).
- **動** (*dòng*): Movement (one of the eight touches).
- **味禪** (*wèi chán*): Flavor Zen (meditation corrupted by attachment/craving).

### Cross-Reference
- See Fascicle 2 for the **25 Preparations**, which are prerequisites for these states.
- See *Abhidharmakośa* for parallel definitions of the dhyāna factors.

---

**[End of Section 1]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 09 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 9: Section 2 — The Pro Rig (Transcendental Zens)
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 9, Part 2 (卷第九之二)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## The Realm of No-Leakage (無漏)

Miao-lo now introduces the **Transcendental Zens**. Unlike the Root Dhyānas (which "leak" back into samsara), these practices sever the bonds of attachment. They are the tools of the Arhats and Pratyekabuddhas, but Bodhisattvas must master them too.

---

## I. The Impurity Meditations (不淨觀)

Designed to break the most fundamental attachment: lust and body-identification.

### The Nine Thoughts (九想)
1.  **Swelling** (脹想): The corpse bloats.
2.  **Discoloration** (壞想): Turns blue/purple.
3.  **Bloody Flesh** (血塗想).
4.  **Festering** (膿爛想).
5.  **Eaten** (噉想): Birds and beasts consume it.
6.  **Scattering** (散想): Limbs torn apart.
7.  **Bone** (骨想): Only the skeleton remains.
8.  **Burning** (燒想).
9.  **Ash/Dust** (壞滅想).

Miao-lo notes:
> "This is strong medicine. Like using fire to fight fire. It is not the 'True' contemplation of the Middle Way, but without it, the coarse obstacles cannot be removed."

---

## II. The Eight Back-Sheddings (八背捨 - Aṣṭa Vimokṣāḥ)

Also known as the **Eight Liberations**. "Back" (背) means turning the back on desire; "Shedding" (捨) means removing the attachment.

1.  **Internal forms exist, seeing external forms** (內有色相觀外色): Retaining the perception of one's own body while viewing external impurity.
2.  **Internal forms nonexistent, seeing external forms** (內無色相觀外色): Eliminating the perception of one's own body (skeleton visualization) while viewing external impurity.
3.  **Purified Body leading to the Witnessing of the Pure** (淨身作證): Seeing pure colors/lights. (Transition to formless).
4.  **Infinite Space Absorption**.
5.  **Infinite Consciousness Absorption**.
6.  **Nothingness Absorption**.
7.  **Neither-Perception-Nor-Non-Perception**.
8.  **Extinction of Cessation** (滅受想定): The cessation of feeling and perception. The hallmark of the Arhat.

---

## III. The Eight Victory Places (八勝處 - Abhibhāyatana)

Mastery over the objects of visualization. The practitioner is not just "shedding" attachment but "conquering" the object—able to make it appear, disappear, or change at will.

1.  **Internal form, external small forms**.
2.  **Internal form, external large forms**.
(And so on, matching the Back-Sheddings but with "Victory" - mastery).

---

## IV. The Ten All-Places (十一切處 - Kṛtsna)

Total pervasion. The practitioner can make the entire universe appear as a single element.

- **Earth, Water, Fire, Wind, Blue, Yellow, Red, White, Space, Consciousness.**

Miao-lo explains:
> "If one envisions Water, the entire ten directions become water. There is no shore. This breaks the boundaries of rigid perception."

---

## V. The Nine Sequential Dhyānas (九次第定)

Entering the dhyānas in strict order (1→2→3→4→Space→...→Cessation) without skipping or wavering. This proves the stability of the "Pro Rig."

---

## VI. The Lion Sprint Samādhi (師子奮迅三昧)

The ability to enter and exit these states rapidly, forward and backward. Like a lion shaking off dust, the practitioner shakes off the fixation on any specific state.

---

## VII. The Transcendence Samādhi (超越三昧)

The ultimate mastery of the "Pro Rig." The practitioner can jump from the First Dhyāna directly to the Extinction Samādhi, or from the Earth element directly to Nothingness. This flexibility prepares the mind for the non-dual contemplation of the Bodhisattva.

---

**[End of Section 2]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 09 SECTION 03 -->

# Fascicle 9: Section 3 — The Master Class (Great Vehicle Zens) & Application
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 9, Part 3 (卷第九之三)
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran, 湛然, 711-782 CE)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## I. The Great Vehicle Dhyānas

These are the practices that define the Bodhisattva path. They are not merely for suppression of defilement but for the cultivation of positive power to aid beings.

### 1. Compassion Samādhi (慈心定)
Also known as the **Four Immeasurables** (四無量心).
-   **Loving-Kindness**: Giving happiness.
-   **Compassion**: Removing suffering.
-   **Joy**: Rejoicing in others' merit.
-   **Equanimity**: Equality towards friends and enemies.

**Miao-lo's Insight**: "In the Hīnayāna, this is just a visualization. In the Mahāyāna, it is the **function of the Essence**. The 'Unconditioned Compassion' (無緣大慈) arises from seeing the emptiness of the self and the emptiness of beings, yet still responding."

### 2. Causes and Conditions Samādhi (因緣定)
Contemplating the **Twelve Links of Dependent Origination**.
- Past causes → Present effects.
- Present causes → Future effects.
- **The Insight**: Seeing the links breaks the chain. In the Perfect Teaching, one sees that **Ignorance IS Buddha-nature** (when transformed).

### 3. Buddha-Recollection Samādhi (念佛三昧)
Focusing on the Buddha.
- **Form Body**: The 32 marks.
- **Dharma Body**: The wisdom and merit.
- **Real Mark**: The nature of the Buddha as non-dual.
- **The Result**: "Seeing the Buddhas of the ten directions standing before one."

### 4. Superknowledges (神通)
- The **Six Superknowledges** (Divine Eye, Ear, etc.) are natural byproducts of the Fourth Dhyāna when directed by wisdom.
- **The Bodhisattva's Use**: They are not magic tricks. They are **skillful means** (upaya) to remove obstacles for beings. Example: Using the Divine Eye to see where a being has been reborn to help them.

---

## II. Applying the Ten Modes to the Zen Object (正明修觀)

Miao-lo now integrates the entire fascicle by applying the **Ten Modes of Contemplation** to the **Meditative States Object**.

### Mode 1: Contemplating the Inconceivable Object
**The Method**:
When a dhyāna state arises (e.g., the joy of the First Dhyāna), do not rest in it. **Observe it**.
-   Where does this joy come from?
-   It is not the self. It is not other.
-   It is **Empty** (no fixed nature).
-   It is **Provisional** (the sensation exists).
-   It is the **Middle** (the Dhyāna itself is the Dharma-Realm).

**The 3000 Realms**:
This single moment of "Dhyāna Joy" contains the Ten Worlds.
-   If you cling to it → **Human/Heavenly Realm**.
-   If you use it to escape → **Two Vehicles Realm**.
-   If you engage it with Bodhicitta → **Bodhisattva Realm**.
-   If you see its true nature → **Buddha Realm**.

### Mode 2: Generating Bodhicitta
"I will use this concentration not for my own bliss, but to stabilize my mind for the liberation of all beings."

### Mode 3: Skillful Stabilization
If the mind sinks into the bliss (too much stopping), raise it with inspection (contemplation). If the mind scatters in analysis, settle it with the dhyāna factor.

### Mode 10: Release Dharma-Attachment
**Crucial**: You must release the attachment to the Dhyāna state itself.
> "The Dhyāna is a boat. Do not build a house on the boat. Cross the river."

---

## III. The Ultimate Integration

Miao-lo concludes by noting that all these Zens—Worldly, Transcendent, and Great Vehicle—are ultimately **One Zen** in the Perfect Teaching.
- The **Root Dhyāna** is the **Dharma-body** (when seen as Middle).
- The **Impurity** is the **Empty** aspect.
- The **Compassion** is the **Provisional** aspect.

Practicing one allows access to all, if the View (Mode 1) is correct.

---

**[End of Fascicle 9]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 09 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 9: Closing Summary
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 9
**Author**: Miao-lo (Zhanran)
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Overview

Fascicle 9 has provided an exhaustive analysis of the **Meditative States Object** (禪定境). This object encompasses the entire range of Buddhist meditative attainments, from the fundamental worldly absorptions to the advanced samādhis of the Bodhisattva.

## Key Teachings

### 1. The Dangers of Dhyāna
Miao-lo repeatedly emphasizes that while dhyāna is essential for stability, it is fraught with danger:
- **Attachment**: "Flavor Zen" (味禪) binds the practitioner to samsara.
- **Stagnation**: Resting in the "Pro Rig" (Transcendental Zen) leads to the Two Vehicles, cutting off the Bodhisattva path.
- **Pride**: Mistaking the Fourth Dhyāna for Nirvana is a common error (exemplified by the Bhikshu who was reborn in hell).

### 2. The Necessity of Wisdom
Dhyāna alone is "blind." It must be illuminated by **Prajñā** (Wisdom).
- The **Ten Modes** provide this illumination.
- Mode 1 (Inconceivable Object) transforms the "dead water" of absorption into the "living ocean" of the Dharma-realm.

### 3. The Integration of Methods
Miao-lo demonstrates that the Tiantai system does not reject "inferior" methods (like the Four Dhyānas or Nine Thoughts). Instead, it **repurposes** them.
- A Perfect Teaching practitioner can use the **Nine Thoughts** not just to disgust the mind, but to see the emptiness of the body and the non-duality of purity and impurity.
- The **Six Wondrous Gates** become a method for tuning the mind to the Middle Way.

### 4. The Supremacy of the Perfect View
Ultimately, all Zens are seen as **Dharma-Nature**.
- The bliss of the First Dhyāna *is* the bliss of Nirvana, if seen correctly.
- The silence of the Fourth Dhyāna *is* the cessation of the Middle Way.
- There is no need to discard the worldly to find the transcendental.

---

## Final Instruction

The practitioner is urged to:
1.  **Cultivate** dhyāna to stabilize the mind.
2.  **Apply** the Ten Modes to prevent attachment.
3.  **Use** the power of concentration to fuel Compassion and the Six Paramitas.

Fascicle 10 will conclude the work with the remaining objects (Views, Pride, Two Vehicles, Bodhisattva) and the final fruition.

**[End of Fascicle 9]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 10 OPENING -->

# Fascicle 10: Opening — External Obstacles and Final Integration
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 10
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## Introduction

Having completed the treatment of internal obstacles (煩惱境, 病患境, 業境) in Fascicle 8 and meditative states (禪定境) in Fascicle 9, this fascicle addresses the final major category of obstacles: **external interference** (魔事境).

The Māra Object represents the culmination of the conditional objects. While afflictions, illness, and karma arise from within, and meditative states can become traps through attachment, the Māra Object addresses obstacles that appear to come from outside—though Miao-lo will demonstrate that even these are ultimately rooted in mind.

---

## The Structure of Fascicle 10

### Part 1: The Māra Object (魔事境)

The fifth of the Ten Objects addresses demonic interference during practice. Miao-lo follows Zhiyi's treatment in the *Mohe Zhiguan*, which distinguishes:

1. **The Four Types of Māra** (四魔)
   - Affliction-Māra (煩惱魔)
   - Aggregate-Māra (陰魔/五陰魔)
   - Death-Māra (死魔)
   - Heavenly-Māra (天魔/他化自在天魔)

2. **The Twelve Categories of Demonic Activity** (十二種魔事)
   - Manifestations ranging from subtle psychological interference to dramatic visionary encounters

3. **Methods for Recognizing and Counteracting Māra** (識魔對治)
   - Distinguishing true from false
   - Applying the Ten Modes

### Part 2: Integration of the Ten Objects (十境總論)

Having covered all ten objects across the previous fascicles, Miao-lo provides a synthetic overview:

- How the objects interrelate
- The order of their arising
- Why the Aggregates Object is primary
- How each object contains all ten

### Part 3: The Final Three Chapters (後三章略明)

The *Mohe Zhiguan* has ten chapters. The seventh chapter on Correct Contemplation (正觀) has occupied the bulk of the commentary. This fascicle briefly addresses the final three:

- **Fruit and Result** (果報) — What arises from correct practice
- **Arising of Teaching** (起教) — How realization gives rise to teaching others
- **Ultimate Return** (旨歸) — The final destination of the path

---

## The Māra Object in Context

### Position Among the Ten Objects

| # | Object | Category | Nature |
|---|--------|----------|--------|
| 1 | Aggregates | Universal | Always present |
| 2 | Afflictions | Internal | Arises from within |
| 3 | Illness | Internal | Body-based |
| 4 | Karma | Internal | Past actions surfacing |
| **5** | **Māra** | **External** | **Interference from outside** |
| 6 | Dhyāna States | Conditional | Arises in practice |
| 7 | Wrong Views | Cognitive | Mistaken understanding |
| 8 | Pride | Emotional | Attachment to attainment |
| 9 | Two Vehicles | Aspirational | Limited goal |
| 10 | Bodhisattva | Aspirational | Advanced but incomplete |

### Why Māra Comes Fifth

Miao-lo explains:

> "After internal obstacles are addressed, the practitioner gains some stability. This very stability can attract external interference. Māra does not trouble those who are not practicing. It is precisely because one is making progress that Māra takes interest."

This is both a warning and an encouragement: encountering Māra is a sign that practice is working.

---

## The Four Types of Māra (四魔)

### 1. Affliction-Māra (煩惱魔)

**Definition**: The three poisons (greed, anger, delusion) and their derivatives intensifying during practice.

**How it manifests**: Strong emotional states arising seemingly from nowhere. Desire, anger, or confusion more intense than usual.

**The insight**: This is not truly "external"—it is internal affliction wearing a mask. Miao-lo includes it under Māra because it can feel like an attack from outside.

### 2. Aggregate-Māra (陰魔/五陰魔)

**Definition**: Extreme attachment to or fear of the body-mind complex.

**How it manifests**:
- Obsessive concern for physical comfort
- Fear of losing the self
- Identification with mental states as "me"

**The insight**: The aggregates themselves are not demonic. The māra is the clinging.

### 3. Death-Māra (死魔)

**Definition**: Fear of death or impermanence disrupting practice.

**How it manifests**:
- Anxiety about dying before attaining realization
- Resistance to impermanence teachings
- Urgency that creates tension rather than diligence

**The insight**: Death is certain; the timing is uncertain. This māra uses natural facts to create suffering.

### 4. Heavenly-Māra (天魔)

**Definition**: Actual interference from Māra or his retinue in the Heaven of Free Enjoyment of Others' Emanations (他化自在天).

**How it manifests**:
- Visions of deities offering rewards for stopping practice
- Apparent "success" that is actually a trap
- External circumstances that consistently obstruct practice
- Beings appearing to offer teachings that lead astray

**The insight**: This is the only truly "external" māra, yet even this operates through the practitioner's mind. Without internal susceptibility, external māra cannot gain purchase.

---

## The Core Teaching on Māra

### The Fundamental Principle

Miao-lo quotes the *Mohe Zhiguan*:

> "魔界如，佛界如，一如無二如。"
> "The māra-realm is suchness; the Buddha-realm is suchness. One suchness, not two suchnesses."

This is the essential teaching: **Māra and Buddha are not ultimately separate**. Both arise within the same reality. The difference lies in how mind relates to phenomena.

### Application of the First Mode

When the Māra Object arises, apply the first mode of contemplation:

> "Contemplate this demonic appearance as itself containing the 3,000 worlds in one thought-moment. This very māra is empty, provisional, and middle. Do not flee it, do not fight it, do not grasp it."

### The Four Sentences Applied to Māra

1. **Is there Buddha within Māra?** — Yes. Even demonic manifestation is Buddha-nature appearing in distorted form.

2. **Is there Māra within Buddha?** — Potentially. Without vigilance, even genuine realization can be corrupted.

3. **Are Māra and Buddha neither one nor different?** — Correct view. They are not identical (functional difference) nor separate (same nature).

4. **Are they both affirmed and both negated?** — The tetralemma reveals that all positions are provisional.

---

## Recognizing Māra vs. Genuine Experience

### The Twelve Categories of Demonic Activity (十二種魔事)

Miao-lo elaborates Zhiyi's classification:

**Category 1-3: Physical Manifestations**
1. Unusual bodily sensations (pain, pleasure, heat, cold without cause)
2. Illness arising suddenly during practice and vanishing when practice stops
3. Inexplicable physical restlessness or paralysis

**Category 4-6: Emotional Manifestations**
4. Extreme sadness or grief without object
5. Intense fear or terror
6. Uncharacteristic anger or hatred

**Category 7-9: Cognitive Manifestations**
7. Confusion about teachings previously understood
8. False insights that feel profound but lead astray
9. Inability to concentrate despite effort

**Category 10-12: Visionary Manifestations**
10. Seeing figures (sometimes beautiful, sometimes terrifying)
11. Hearing voices (offering advice, making threats)
12. Dreams that disturb or mislead

### The Test of Authenticity

How to distinguish māra from genuine spiritual experience:

| Genuine Experience | Māra's Counterfeit |
|-------------------|-------------------|
| Increases humility | Increases pride |
| Deepens compassion | Narrows concern to self |
| Aligns with Three Truths | Contradicts or distorts doctrine |
| Produces lasting peace | Produces temporary euphoria followed by disturbance |
| Leads toward liberation | Creates new attachments |
| Can be discussed with qualified teachers | Resists examination |

---

## Methods for Counteracting Māra

### The Gradual Approach

1. **Recognition** (識魔): Know that this is māra activity, not reality
2. **Non-engagement** (不取): Do not follow the vision, voice, or feeling
3. **Non-rejection** (不捨): Do not flee or fight
4. **Observation** (觀察): Watch the phenomenon arise and cease
5. **Return** (還): Return to the primary practice

### The Sudden Approach

Apply the contemplation directly:

> "This māra appearance is empty of inherent existence. It arises from conditions. It has no power that I do not give it. Seeing its empty nature, I am not moved."

### The Perfect Approach

> "Māra is Bodhi. This very obstacle is the path. Without this appearance, where would I practice? The māra is showing me my own mind."

---

## What's Ahead

This fascicle will cover:

1. **Section 1**: Detailed treatment of the Four Māras and Twelve Categories
2. **Section 2**: Integration of all Ten Objects
3. **Section 3**: The Final Three Chapters (Fruit, Teaching, Return)

With this fascicle, the commentary on the *Mohe Zhiguan*'s Correct Contemplation chapter reaches completion.

---

**[End of Opening]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 10 SECTION 01 -->

# Fascicle 10: Section 1 — The Māra Object
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 10, Part 1
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## The Fifth Object: Demonic Interference (魔事境)

### The Definition

The character 魔 (*mó*) is a Chinese transliteration of the Sanskrit *māra*, meaning "killer" or "destroyer." Māra is that which kills wisdom, destroys virtue, and obstructs liberation.

Miao-lo clarifies:

> "Māra is not necessarily an external being. The term refers to any phenomenon that obstructs the path. Whether it arises from within or without, if it kills progress, it is māra."

---

## The Four Types of Māra (四魔) — Detailed Treatment

### 1. Affliction-Māra (煩惱魔) — The Internal Saboteur

**The Classic Definition**: The 84,000 afflictions in their manifest forms.

**Miao-lo's Commentary**:

The three poisons serve as the roots:

| Poison | Sanskrit | Function | Manifestation During Practice |
|--------|----------|----------|-------------------------------|
| 貪 Greed | *rāga* | Grasping | Attachment to blissful states, desire for quick results |
| 瞋 Anger | *dveṣa* | Aversion | Frustration with slow progress, hatred of obstacles |
| 癡 Delusion | *moha* | Confusion | Mistaking false insights for true, losing the method |

**The Ten Bonds** (十結) manifest as:

1. **Greed for sensory pleasure** (欲貪) — Drawn back to worldly enjoyments
2. **Greed for existence** (有貪) — Clinging to continued becoming
3. **Anger** (瞋) — Irritation at practice difficulties
4. **Pride** (慢) — "I am making progress"
5. **Ignorance** (無明) — Fundamental not-knowing
6. **Wrong views** (見) — Mistaken understanding
7. **Doubt** (疑) — "Is this really working?"
8. **Restlessness** (掉舉) — Mental agitation
9. **Sloth** (昏沈) — Mental dullness
10. **Shamelessness** (無慚) — Loss of moral sensitivity

**The Treatment**:

Apply the contemplation:

> "These afflictions arise from conditions. They have no inherent nature. This very greed is empty. This very anger is the dharma-realm. Seeing their nature, I am not moved."

When arising is intense:
1. Return to breath awareness
2. Apply the specific antidote (generosity for greed, patience for anger, analysis for delusion)
3. When stable, return to direct contemplation

---

### 2. Aggregate-Māra (陰魔/五陰魔) — The Body-Mind Trap

**The Classic Definition**: The five aggregates as the basis for suffering.

**Miao-lo's Commentary**:

This māra operates through identification:

| Aggregate | Identification Pattern | Māra's Hook |
|-----------|----------------------|-------------|
| Form (色) | "This body is me" | Fear of bodily harm, craving for physical comfort |
| Feeling (受) | "These sensations are me" | Chasing pleasant, fleeing unpleasant |
| Perception (想) | "My thoughts define me" | Attachment to views |
| Formations (行) | "My intentions are me" | Pride in effort |
| Consciousness (識) | "This knowing is me" | Subtle self-grasping |

**The Specific Trap for Practitioners**:

At advanced stages, practitioners may experience:
- **Aggregate dissolution** — The sense of body-mind beginning to break apart
- **Fear response** — Clinging arises: "I'm losing myself!"
- **Retreat** — Practice is abandoned to preserve the sense of self

This is the aggregate-māra at work.

**The Treatment**:

> "The aggregates have always been empty. There is no self to lose. What dissolves is what was never solid. Let go into the dissolution."

For those with strong fear:
1. Ground in the body (feel the weight, the breath)
2. Remember: This is natural
3. Return to contemplation when fear subsides

---

### 3. Death-Māra (死魔) — The Ultimate Deadline

**The Classic Definition**: Death as the termination of opportunity for practice.

**Miao-lo's Commentary**:

Death-māra operates in two ways:

**A. Fear of Dying Before Attainment**

The practitioner thinks:
- "I haven't attained stream-entry. What if I die tonight?"
- "I need to practice harder, faster!"
- "There's no time!"

This urgency, while understandable, creates tension that obstructs the very attainment sought.

**B. Using Death as Excuse**

The practitioner thinks:
- "I'm going to die anyway. What's the point?"
- "I'll practice more seriously when I'm older"
- "Life is short—better enjoy it now"

This resignation abandons the path.

**The Treatment**:

> "Death is certain; only the time is uncertain. This very uncertainty is the teaching. Practice now—not from panic, but from clarity. This moment is the only moment."

The Buddha's teaching on the four thoughts that turn the mind:
1. Precious human birth
2. Impermanence and death
3. Karma and its effects
4. The suffering of saṃsāra

---

### 4. Heavenly-Māra (天魔) — The External Adversary

**The Classic Definition**: Māra-pāpīyān, the "Evil One," ruler of the highest heaven of the desire realm (他化自在天, *Paranirmita-vaśavartin*).

**Miao-lo's Commentary**:

This is the māra that explicitly opposes liberation. His domain is the desire realm; every being who leaves this realm diminishes his subjects.

**The Hierarchy of Māra's Forces**:

| Level | Agent | Method |
|-------|-------|--------|
| Highest | Māra himself | Rarely intervenes personally; reserves for advanced practitioners |
| Middle | Māra's daughters | Temptation through desire |
| Lower | Māra's armies | Fear, violence, obstruction |
| Common | Minor spirits | Petty disturbances |

**How Heavenly-Māra Operates**:

1. **Observation**: Māra watches for practitioners making genuine progress
2. **Assessment**: Determines the practitioner's vulnerabilities
3. **Approach**: Uses the appropriate method (desire for some, fear for others, doubt for yet others)
4. **Escalation**: If initial methods fail, escalates to more dramatic interventions

**The Classic Forms of Attack**:

From the *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*:

**The Ten Armies of Māra** (十軍):
1. Sensual desire
2. Discontent
3. Hunger and thirst
4. Craving
5. Sloth and torpor
6. Fear
7. Doubt
8. Obstinacy and ingratitude
9. Gain, praise, honor, false glory
10. Self-exaltation and disparaging others

**The Treatment**:

> "Māra has no power I do not give him. His arrows are my own projections. Recognizing this, I sit unmoved."

The story of the Buddha's enlightenment night:
- Māra's armies attacked—the Buddha was unmoved
- Māra's daughters tempted—the Buddha was unmoved
- Māra claimed the seat of enlightenment—the Buddha touched the earth
- The earth testified: "This is his rightful seat"

---

## The Twelve Categories of Demonic Activity (十二種魔事)

### Category 1-4: Physical Manifestations (身分魔事)

**1. Spontaneous Pain (無故痛)**

During meditation, sudden sharp pain arises—in the heart, the head, the joints—with no physical cause. When practice stops, the pain vanishes.

**Recognition**: Pain that has no medical cause, appears only during practice, and vanishes when practice stops.

**Treatment**: Recognize it as māra. Continue practice. If unbearable, reduce intensity temporarily but do not abandon the session.

**2. Spontaneous Pleasure (無故樂)**

Waves of bliss, sexual arousal, or physical pleasure arise without cause. This is more dangerous than pain because practitioners may chase it.

**Recognition**: Pleasure without object that arises specifically during practice.

**Treatment**: Neither grasp nor reject. Note "pleasure" and return to contemplation. If sexual arousal is strong, apply impurity contemplation.

**3. Abnormal Physical States (身相異常)**

The body feels too heavy to move, or too light (floating sensation). Uncontrollable movements (shaking, swaying, jumping) occur.

**Recognition**: Physical states that are dramatic, do not correspond to the practice being done, and distract from contemplation.

**Treatment**: Ground awareness in the body's weight. Breathe deeply. Do not follow the phenomena. Return to the method.

**4. Illness That Follows Practice (隨修生病)**

Sickness arises precisely when practice intensifies. It clears when practice stops. This is distinct from ordinary illness.

**Recognition**: The timing correlation is too precise to be coincidental.

**Treatment**: Recognize it as māra. If possible, continue practice. If the body genuinely needs rest, rest—but understand the cause.

---

### Category 5-8: Emotional Manifestations (情緒魔事)

**5. Unaccountable Grief (無故悲)**

Overwhelming sadness, tears, a sense of loss—with no object. "I don't know why I'm crying."

**Recognition**: Grief without cause that arises during practice.

**Treatment**: Allow the emotion without feeding it. Do not create stories to explain it. Let it pass. Return to method.

**6. Terror (恐怖)**

Intense fear—of death, of madness, of dissolution, of the dark, of unseen presences.

**Recognition**: Fear disproportionate to any real threat.

**Treatment**: Ground in the body. Remember: "This is māra. I am safe. The fear is empty." If severe, open eyes, move body, then return.

**7. Uncharacteristic Rage (瞋恚現前)**

Anger toward the teacher, the teaching, fellow practitioners, oneself—arising without adequate cause.

**Recognition**: Hostility that appears during practice and does not match one's normal disposition.

**Treatment**: Recognize it as māra. Do not act on it. Apply loving-kindness toward the apparent object. Return to method.

**8. False Devotion (虛妄信)**

Sudden intense "faith" accompanied by grandiosity: "I have seen the truth! I am special! I must tell everyone!"

**Recognition**: Inflation rather than humility. Urgency rather than peace.

**Treatment**: The most dangerous because it feels positive. Wait. Do not act on the "insight" for at least three days. Consult a teacher. If genuine, it will remain; if māra, it will fade.

---

### Category 9-12: Cognitive and Visionary Manifestations (心識魔事)

**9. Confusion About Teaching (法義迷悶)**

Teachings previously clear now seem contradictory or meaningless. The practitioner cannot remember basic instructions.

**Recognition**: Sudden intellectual confusion specifically about the dharma.

**Treatment**: Do not try to think through it. Return to the simplest practice (breath, mantra). Let clarity return naturally.

**10. False Insight (邪慧現前)**

"Realizations" that feel profound but contradict correct teaching. "Form is not really emptiness—I see now that everything is actually real!"

**Recognition**: Insights that cannot be reconciled with the Three Truths.

**Treatment**: Test against scripture and teacher. If it contradicts, reject it regardless of how "certain" it feels.

**11. Visions of Beings (天神鬼魅現)**

Buddhas, bodhisattvas, deities, demons, ghosts, ancestors—appearing during meditation.

**Recognition**: Visual appearances, whether beautiful or terrifying.

**Treatment**:
- Do not engage in dialogue
- Do not follow instructions from the vision
- Say: "If you are genuine, you will wait until I consult my teacher. If you cannot wait, you are not genuine."
- Return to practice

**12. Prophetic Dreams (夢中授記)**

Dreams in which beings predict the practitioner's attainment, give secret teachings, or assign special missions.

**Recognition**: Dreams that inflate the practitioner's sense of importance.

**Treatment**: Dreams are mental activity. Even genuine prophetic dreams do not change what needs to be done: practice. Ignore the content; continue practice.

---

## The Ultimate Teaching on Māra

### From the *Mohe Zhiguan*:

> "魔界如即佛界如，佛界如即魔界如。"
> "The suchness of the māra-realm is identical to the suchness of the Buddha-realm."

This is not saying māra is good or that demons should be welcomed. It is saying:

1. Māra and Buddha arise within the same reality
2. The difference is in the mind's relationship to phenomena
3. Fighting māra strengthens māra; seeing māra's nature liberates

### The Paradox

- If you grasp at Buddha, Buddha becomes māra
- If you see through māra, māra becomes Buddha
- Neither grasping nor rejecting, the practitioner is free

---

## Summary

The Māra Object is the test of practice. Everything encountered before—afflictions, illness, karma, meditative states—prepared for this. Here the practitioner faces opposition that appears absolute.

The teaching: **Nothing is absolute**. Even Māra is empty. Even this opposition is Buddha-nature in disguise.

This is not comfort. It is method. See the nature of what arises, and it cannot bind you.

---

**[End of Section 1]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 10 SECTION 02 -->

# Fascicle 10: Section 2 — Integration of the Ten Objects
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 10, Part 2
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## The Ten Objects Complete (十境總論)

Having covered all ten objects across the preceding fascicles, Miao-lo now provides a synthetic overview. This section addresses:

1. Why there are ten objects
2. Their order and interrelationship
3. How each contains all ten
4. The practical implications for practice

---

## Why Ten Objects?

### The Logic of the Classification

The ten objects arise from analyzing all possible phenomena that can appear during contemplation:

| Category | Objects | Logic |
|----------|---------|-------|
| **Universal** | 1. Aggregates | Always present; the basis of experience |
| **Internal** | 2. Afflictions, 3. Illness, 4. Karma | Arise from within the practitioner |
| **External** | 5. Māra | Appears to come from outside |
| **Practice-Induced** | 6. Dhyāna States | Arises specifically from meditation |
| **Cognitive** | 7. Wrong Views, 8. Pride | Errors in understanding or attitude |
| **Aspirational** | 9. Two Vehicles, 10. Bodhisattva | Incomplete goals |

Miao-lo clarifies:

> "These ten exhaust the possibilities. Whatever arises during practice falls into one of these categories. The practitioner who knows these ten is prepared for anything."

---

## The Order of the Ten Objects

### Why This Sequence?

**Object 1: Aggregates (陰入界境)**

The aggregates come first because they are always present. Before any specific obstacle arises, there is already body-mind. This is the universal ground.

> "Even when sitting peacefully with no disturbance, the aggregates are present. Therefore, contemplation begins here."

**Objects 2-4: Internal Obstacles**

After establishing contemplation on the aggregates, internal disturbances naturally surface:

- **Afflictions** (煩惱): The three poisons stir
- **Illness** (病患): Physical discomfort intensifies
- **Karma** (業相): Past actions manifest as visions, feelings, memories

These arise from within. They are "mine" in the conventional sense.

**Object 5: External Obstacle**

Once internal obstacles are somewhat stabilized, external interference may begin:

- **Māra** (魔事): Opposition from outside

This marks the transition from internal to external work.

**Object 6: Practice State**

As practice deepens, meditative states arise:

- **Dhyāna** (禪定): Jhāna, samādhi, various absorptions

These are not obstacles in themselves, but become obstacles through attachment.

**Objects 7-8: Cognitive Errors**

With states comes the danger of misinterpretation:

- **Wrong Views** (諸見): Misunderstanding the meaning of experiences
- **Pride** (慢): Inflating the significance of attainment

These corrupt genuine progress.

**Objects 9-10: Incomplete Goals**

Finally, even correct understanding can aim at wrong destinations:

- **Two Vehicles** (二乘): Settling for personal liberation
- **Bodhisattva** (菩薩): Advanced but not yet complete

These represent the subtlest traps.

---

## The Interrelationship of the Ten Objects

### Horizontal Integration (橫論十境)

All ten objects are present simultaneously in any moment of experience:

| Object | Present As |
|--------|------------|
| Aggregates | The experience itself |
| Afflictions | Subtle reactive patterns |
| Illness | Physical sensations |
| Karma | Conditioning of perception |
| Māra | Resistance to liberation |
| Dhyāna | Quality of concentration |
| Wrong Views | Interpretive framework |
| Pride | Sense of self-reference |
| Two Vehicles | Habitual goal-orientation |
| Bodhisattva | Aspiration and its limits |

> "In a single moment, all ten are present. The classification into sequence is for teaching purposes. Reality does not arise in sequence."

### Vertical Integration (竪論十境)

In practice, objects tend to arise in the given order:

```
1. Aggregates (always present)
      ↓
2-4. Internal obstacles surface
      ↓
5. External interference begins
      ↓
6. Meditative states develop
      ↓
7-8. Cognitive errors arise
      ↓
9-10. Aspirational limits encountered
```

This sequence describes a typical progression, but individuals may experience variations.

---

## Each Object Contains All Ten

### The Principle of Mutual Inclusion

Miao-lo emphasizes:

> "One object contains all ten. Ten objects are one object. This is not metaphor but reality."

**Example: Aggregates Containing All Ten**

When contemplating the aggregates:

1. **Aggregates**: The aggregates themselves
2. **Afflictions**: Clinging to aggregates
3. **Illness**: Physical aspect of form aggregate
4. **Karma**: Conditioned nature of aggregates
5. **Māra**: Aggregates as the basis for māra's operation
6. **Dhyāna**: Subtle states within perception
7. **Wrong Views**: Mistaking aggregates for self
8. **Pride**: "My" aggregates
9. **Two Vehicles**: Using aggregate contemplation for personal escape
10. **Bodhisattva**: Seeing aggregates of all beings

**Example: Māra Containing All Ten**

When the Māra Object arises:

1. **Aggregates**: Māra appears within body-mind experience
2. **Afflictions**: Fear, desire, confusion triggered by māra
3. **Illness**: Physical symptoms from māra interference
4. **Karma**: Māra activating karmic traces
5. **Māra**: The māra phenomenon itself
6. **Dhyāna**: Māra appearing as false samādhi states
7. **Wrong Views**: Māra teaching false doctrine
8. **Pride**: "I defeated māra"
9. **Two Vehicles**: Māra encouraging limited aspiration
10. **Bodhisattva**: Māra appearing as false bodhisattva

---

## Practical Implications

### The One-Method Approach

Because each object contains all ten, and the Ten Modes apply to any object, the practitioner needs only one fundamental method:

> "Contemplate the inconceivable. Whatever arises is the 3,000 worlds in one thought. Empty, provisional, middle. No need to change the method when the object changes."

This is the simplicity on the far side of complexity.

### The Adaptive Approach

However, Miao-lo also validates adapting to circumstances:

> "For those whose roots are sharp, one method suffices. For those whose roots are dull, specific treatments for specific objects may be needed."

The Ten Modes were created precisely for this: to provide multiple tools for practitioners who cannot immediately realize the direct approach.

### The Signs of Mastery

How does the practitioner know the Ten Objects are understood?

1. **Nothing surprising**: Whatever arises is recognized as one of the ten
2. **No panic**: Even dramatic experiences are met with equanimity
3. **No attachment**: Even blissful states are not grasped
4. **No rejection**: Even painful states are not fled
5. **Continuity**: Practice continues regardless of what arises

---

## The Ten Objects and the Ten Modes

### Their Relationship

| Ten Objects | Ten Modes |
|-------------|-----------|
| What can arise | How to work with what arises |
| The territory | The tools |
| Passive (encountered) | Active (applied) |
| Descriptive | Prescriptive |

> "The Ten Objects describe the field. The Ten Modes describe the work. A farmer must know both the land and the tools."

### The First Mode as Master Key

While all ten modes can be applied to any object, the first mode—Contemplate the Inconceivable Object—is the master key:

> "One who truly realizes the 3,000 worlds in one thought has, in that realization, completed the work of all ten modes. The remaining nine are elaborations for those who have not yet seen."

---

## The Aggregates Object as Primary

### Why Begin with Aggregates?

Miao-lo returns to this fundamental point:

> "All objects are modifications of the aggregates. Afflictions arise within the aggregate of formations. Illness arises within the aggregate of form. Karma is stored in consciousness. Even māra operates through the aggregates."

Therefore:

> "The practitioner who thoroughly contemplates the aggregates has, in principle, contemplated all ten objects. The distinction into ten is pedagogical, not ontological."

### The Trap of Chasing Objects

Some practitioners, learning about the ten objects, begin to look for them:

- "When will māra appear?"
- "I haven't experienced wrong views yet—am I missing something?"
- "The bodhisattva object sounds advanced—I want that one"

Miao-lo warns:

> "Do not seek the objects. Contemplate what is present. The aggregates are present now. Begin there. What needs to arise will arise."

---

## The Completion of Correct Contemplation

### The Scope of Chapter Seven

The seventh chapter of the *Mohe Zhiguan*—Correct Contemplation (正觀)—covers:

- The ten objects
- The ten modes
- Their interrelationship
- Their application

With this section, the treatment of the seventh chapter is essentially complete.

### What Remains

The final three chapters address what happens after correct contemplation is established:

- **Fruit** (果報): The results that arise
- **Teaching** (起教): How realization leads to teaching others
- **Return** (旨歸): The ultimate destination

These are treated briefly in Section 3.

---

## Summary

The ten objects form a complete map of contemplative territory. Whether the practitioner encounters internal turmoil, external opposition, altered states, cognitive errors, or aspirational limitations, the map locates the experience and the Ten Modes provide the tools.

The deepest teaching: all ten objects are one object, all ten modes are one mode. The apparent complexity resolves into the simplicity of:

> "This very mind is Buddha. This very object is the dharma-realm. Nothing to add, nothing to remove."

---

**[End of Section 2]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 10 SECTION 03 -->

# Fascicle 10: Section 3 — The Final Three Chapters
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 10, Part 3
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## The Structure of the Mohe Zhiguan

The *Mohe Zhiguan* consists of ten chapters:

| # | Chinese | English | Status |
|---|---------|---------|--------|
| 1 | 大意 | Grand Outline | Covered (F1) |
| 2 | 釋名 | Explanation of Name | Covered (F2) |
| 3 | 顯體 | Revealing Essence | Covered (F2) |
| 4 | 攝法 | Embracing Dharmas | Covered (F2) |
| 5 | 偏圓 | Partial vs. Complete | Covered (F2) |
| 6 | 方便 | Skillful Means | Covered (F2) |
| 7 | 正觀 | Correct Contemplation | Covered (F3-F10) |
| 8 | 果報 | Fruit and Result | This section |
| 9 | 起教 | Arising of Teaching | This section |
| 10 | 旨歸 | Ultimate Return | This section |

The seventh chapter on Correct Contemplation occupied the vast majority of Zhiyi's teaching and Miao-lo's commentary. The final three chapters, while important, are treated more briefly because they describe what happens after the essential work is done.

---

## Chapter 8: Fruit and Result (果報)

### The Question

What are the fruits of correct contemplation? What results from applying the Ten Modes to the Ten Objects?

### The Two Types of Fruit

Miao-lo, following Zhiyi, distinguishes:

**1. Causal Fruit (因果)**

Results that arise during practice:

| Stage | Fruit | Characteristics |
|-------|-------|-----------------|
| **Name Identity** (名字即) | Initial understanding | Intellectual grasp of the teaching |
| **Contemplation Identity** (觀行即) | Glimpses | Moments of seeing the Three Truths |
| **Resemblance Identity** (相似即) | Stability | Consistent realization in meditation |
| **Partial Realization** (分真即) | Breakthrough | Actual elimination of ignorance |
| **Ultimate Identity** (究竟即) | Completion | Full Buddhahood |

**2. Fruit Proper (果報)**

The final result:

> "The fruit of correct contemplation is the three bodies of Buddha: Dharma-body (法身), Reward-body (報身), and Transformation-body (化身). These are not three separate things but one reality viewed from different angles."

### The Three Bodies as Fruit

| Body | Sanskrit | Fruit Of | Manifestation |
|------|----------|----------|---------------|
| **Dharma-body** | *Dharmakāya* | Wisdom realizing emptiness | Universal truth-nature |
| **Reward-body** | *Saṃbhogakāya* | Wisdom realizing provisional | Buddha's glorified form in pure lands |
| **Transformation-body** | *Nirmāṇakāya* | Wisdom realizing middle | Buddha appearing in the world to teach |

### The Relationship to Practice

Miao-lo clarifies:

> "The three bodies are not attained from outside. They are the inherent nature of mind, revealed through practice. Contemplation does not create Buddha-nature; it removes what obscures Buddha-nature."

This echoes the teaching from Fascicle 7 on "removing the film, nurturing the pearl" (除膜養珠).

### Caution Regarding Fruit

Zhiyi warned against preoccupation with results:

> "If you practice for the sake of attaining fruit, you will not attain fruit. Fruit arises naturally when conditions are complete. The practitioner's job is to create the conditions, not to grasp at results."

---

## Chapter 9: Arising of Teaching (起教)

### The Question

Once realization is attained, how does teaching arise? What enables the Buddha to teach in ways that benefit different beings?

### The Logic of Teaching

Teaching arises from three factors:

**1. Wisdom (智)**

The Buddha's wisdom sees:
- The nature of reality (dharma)
- The capacities of beings (根機)
- The appropriate means for each (方便)

**2. Compassion (悲)**

Wisdom without compassion would not result in teaching. The Buddha teaches because beings suffer and teaching can end suffering.

**3. Skillful Means (方便)**

Wisdom and compassion require appropriate expression. The Buddha adapts teaching to circumstance.

### The Four Siddhāntas Revisited

The teaching arises through four modes, as discussed in the early fascicles:

| Siddhānta | Sanskrit | Function | Example |
|-----------|----------|----------|---------|
| **Worldly** (世界悉檀) | *Laukika* | Meet beings where they are | Using local language, customs |
| **Individual** (各各為人悉檀) | *Prātipauruṣika* | Address specific needs | Teaching generosity to the stingy |
| **Therapeutic** (對治悉檀) | *Prātipākṣika* | Cure specific ills | Teaching impermanence to the attached |
| **Supreme** (第一義悉檀) | *Paramārtha* | Reveal ultimate truth | Teaching the Three Truths |

### From Realization to Expression

Miao-lo traces the movement:

```
Realization (inner)
      ↓
Compassion arises (response to suffering)
      ↓
Wisdom discerns (what is needed)
      ↓
Teaching emerges (appropriate expression)
```

> "The Buddha does not deliberate: 'What shall I teach?' Teaching arises spontaneously from the conjunction of realization, compassion, and circumstance. It is like a bell that sounds when struck—the sound is appropriate to the strike."

### The Practitioner's Responsibility

What does this mean for the practitioner?

1. **First, realize**: Teaching without realization is empty words
2. **Develop compassion**: Realization without compassion is incomplete
3. **Trust emergence**: The appropriate teaching will arise naturally
4. **Stay humble**: Even after teaching, continue to practice

---

## Chapter 10: Ultimate Return (旨歸)

### The Question

Where does the path lead? What is the final destination?

### The Three Destinations

Depending on the teaching followed:

| Path | Destination | Characteristics |
|------|-------------|-----------------|
| **Tripiṭaka** | Arhat | Personal liberation from saṃsāra |
| **Shared** | Bodhisattva (initial) | Liberation plus aspiration |
| **Distinct** | Buddha (sequential) | Full realization through stages |
| **Perfect** | Buddha (immediate) | Full realization here and now |

### The Tiantai Teaching on Ultimate Return

The ultimate return is to:

> "Return to the origin (歸源). But the origin was never left. The 'return' is the recognition that there was never any departure."

This is expressed in the famous formula:

> "即心是佛" — "This very mind is Buddha."

### The Three Truths as Return

| Truth | Return |
|-------|--------|
| **Emptiness** (空) | Return to the unborn nature of phenomena |
| **Provisional** (假) | Return to the functional manifestation |
| **Middle** (中) | Return to the non-dual identity of emptiness and provisional |

> "The ultimate return is not to a place. It is to the nature that was always present. Saṃsāra and nirvāṇa are not two places. Liberation is seeing through the illusion of separation."

### The Practical Meaning

What does "ultimate return" mean in practice?

1. **Continue practicing**: Even the Buddha continued to sit
2. **Teach as appropriate**: Realization naturally expresses itself
3. **Remain humble**: "Ultimate" does not mean "finished"
4. **Benefit beings**: The return is not solitary

---

## The Completion of the Commentary

### What Has Been Accomplished

Across ten fascicles, Miao-lo has:

1. Established the lineage and context (F1)
2. Explained the structure and methodology (F2)
3. Detailed the practice (F3-F10)
4. Addressed all ten objects
5. Explained all ten modes
6. Integrated the whole into Tiantai understanding

### What Remains for the Practitioner

The commentary points the way. The practitioner must walk:

> "Reading the map is not the journey. Understanding the tools is not using them. The commentary has done what commentary can do. Now practice."

---

## The Final Teaching

### From Miao-lo

> "This commentary is called 'Transmitting the Expansive Explanation' (傳弘決). 'Transmitting' because it passes on what Zhiyi taught. 'Expansive' because it opens what was condensed. 'Explanation' because it clarifies what was obscure."

> "But the ultimate transmission is not in words. It is from mind to mind. The reader who understands not just the letter but the spirit has received the transmission."

### From Zhiyi (as preserved by Miao-lo)

> "All Buddha's teaching aims at one thing: ending suffering. The *Mohe Zhiguan* is one expression of this aim. The Ten Objects and Ten Modes are tools for this work. Do not mistake the tools for the goal."

> "The goal is simple: See the nature of mind. Everything else serves this seeing."

---

## Summary

The final three chapters of the *Mohe Zhiguan* address what happens after the essential practice is established:

1. **Fruit**: The results of practice—the three bodies of Buddha
2. **Teaching**: How realization naturally expresses itself in teaching
3. **Return**: The ultimate destination, which is the origin, which was never left

These chapters are brief not because they are unimportant but because they describe what happens naturally when practice is correct. The practitioner's work is in the seventh chapter—Correct Contemplation. When that is complete, fruit, teaching, and return arise without additional effort.

---

**[End of Section 3]**


---



<!-- FASCICLE 10 CLOSING -->

# Fascicle 10: Closing Summary
## Scholarly Translation

**Source**: T46n1912, Fascicle 10
**Translator**: William Altig / Claude
**Date**: January 23, 2026

---

## What This Fascicle Covered

### Part 1: The Māra Object (魔事境)

The fifth of the Ten Objects, addressing external interference during practice.

**The Four Māras**:
1. **Affliction-Māra** (煩惱魔) — Internal saboteur
2. **Aggregate-Māra** (陰魔) — Body-mind trap
3. **Death-Māra** (死魔) — Fear of impermanence
4. **Heavenly-Māra** (天魔) — External adversary

**The Twelve Categories of Demonic Activity**:
- Physical (pain, pleasure, abnormal states, illness)
- Emotional (grief, terror, rage, false devotion)
- Cognitive (confusion, false insight, visions, dreams)

**The Core Teaching**:
> "The māra-realm is suchness; the Buddha-realm is suchness. One suchness, not two suchnesses."

### Part 2: Integration of the Ten Objects

The complete map of contemplative territory:

| # | Object | Chinese | Category |
|---|--------|---------|----------|
| 1 | Aggregates | 陰入界境 | Universal |
| 2 | Afflictions | 煩惱境 | Internal |
| 3 | Illness | 病患境 | Internal |
| 4 | Karma | 業相境 | Internal |
| 5 | Māra | 魔事境 | External |
| 6 | Dhyāna | 禪定境 | Practice-Induced |
| 7 | Wrong Views | 諸見境 | Cognitive |
| 8 | Pride | 慢境 | Cognitive |
| 9 | Two Vehicles | 二乘境 | Aspirational |
| 10 | Bodhisattva | 菩薩境 | Aspirational |

**Key Principles**:
- Each object contains all ten
- The Ten Modes apply to any object
- The aggregates are primary
- Everything reduces to one contemplation

### Part 3: The Final Three Chapters

| Chapter | Chinese | Content |
|---------|---------|---------|
| 8 | 果報 | Fruit/Result — The three bodies of Buddha |
| 9 | 起教 | Arising of Teaching — From realization to expression |
| 10 | 旨歸 | Ultimate Return — The destination that was never left |

---

---

## The Six Identities — Progress Through Practice

| Identity | Chinese | Description |
|----------|---------|-------------|
| **Principle** | 理即 | Buddha in principle (everyone) |
| **Name** | 名字即 | Hearing and understanding (student) |
| **Contemplation** | 觀行即 | Beginning practice (practitioner) |
| **Resemblance** | 相似即 | Stable realization (adept) |
| **Partial Realization** | 分真即 | Breaking ignorance (sage) |
| **Ultimate** | 究竟即 | Complete Buddhahood (Buddha) |

---

## The Essential Teaching

### The Three Truths

| Truth | Chinese | Meaning |
|-------|---------|---------|
| **Emptiness** | 空諦 | No inherent existence |
| **Provisional** | 假諦 | Functional manifestation |
| **Middle** | 中諦 | Neither and both |

> "Three truths, one reality. One reality, three aspects. Not sequence. Not separation. Simultaneous."

### The Core Formula

> "一念三千" — "3,000 worlds in one thought-moment."

Every moment of mind contains:
- 10 dharma-realms × 10 (mutual inclusion) = 100
- 100 × 10 suchnesses = 1,000
- 1,000 × 3 domains = 3,000

This is the inconceivable that is contemplated in the first mode.

---

## Miao-lo's Contribution

### What the Commentary Provides

1. **Clarification**: Making explicit what Zhiyi left implicit
2. **Organization**: Structuring the vast material
3. **Defense**: Responding to criticisms from other schools
4. **Transmission**: Ensuring the teaching survives

### The Lineage Preserved

```
Nāgārjuna → Huiwen → Huisi → Zhiyi → Guanding → ... → Zhanran (Miao-lo)
```

Miao-lo is the ninth patriarch of Tiantai. His commentary ensures the teaching reaches future generations.

---

## For the Practitioner

### The Work

1. **Sit**: Establish regular practice
2. **Contemplate**: Apply the method to what arises
3. **Continue**: Through obstacles internal and external
4. **Trust**: The path has been walked before

### The Method

> "Contemplate this thought-moment as containing the 3,000 worlds. See the empty, provisional, and middle nature of what arises. Do not grasp, do not reject. Rest in seeing."

### The Fruit

Comes naturally when conditions are complete. Do not chase it.

### The Return

To where you already are. To what you already are.

---

## Final Words

### From the Commentary

> "The Mohe Zhiguan is the heart of Tiantai. This commentary is the explication of that heart. But explication is not the heart itself. The practitioner must find the heart in practice, not in words."

### The Transmission

> "What is transmitted? Not the words. Not even the concepts. What is transmitted is the recognition: 'This very mind is Buddha.' When that recognition is stable, the transmission is complete."
